Distinction of Non-Existents in the Intellect: Definition and Examples
Defines and exemplifies distinction of non-existents in the intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how non-existents can be distinguished in the intellect based on their logical relations, and why this distinction is not about external reality.

Core Idea
Non-existents are distinct in the mind through their logical roles, not externally.

This lesson introduces a key concept in Islamic theology and logic: the distinction of non-existents in the intellect. While non-existents have no external reality, they can be clearly distinguished in the mind based on their causal and conditional relationships. The passage provides three examples and clarifies a common misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the examples given.
The passage uses technical terms like 'عدم المعلول' and 'عدم الشرط'.
2
Identify the three examples
Find the three types of non-existents: non-existence of the effect, non-existence of the condition, and non-existence of the contrary.
Each has a specific logical relationship.
3
Understand the distinction between external and conceptual
The passage distinguishes between external distinction (impossible for non-existents) and conceptual distinction in the intellect ( possible).
This resolves an apparent disagreement.
4
Connect to mental existence debate
The passage ends by linking this distinction to the broader debate on whether mental existence is real.
This is a key issue in Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس
تمايز الإعدام في العقل وما يتعلق بذلك
(قال): المبحث الخامس : للاعدام تمايز في العقل كاختصاص عدم المعلول بالاستناد إلى عدم العلة، وعدم الشرط بمنافاة وجود المشروط، وعدم الضد بتصحيح وجود الآخر.
قد اشتهر خلاف في تمايز الإعدام فإن أريد أن ليس التمايز أمراً محققاً في الخارج أو ليست للمعدمات أو المعدومات عينية متمايزة فضروري لا يتصور فيه نزاع، وإن أريد أن ليس لمفهوم العدم أفراد متمايزة عند العقل يختص كل منها بأحكام مخصوصة صادقة في نفس الأمر فباطل، لأن عدم موجب لعدم المعلول من غير عكس وعدم الشرط مناف لوجود المشروط، وعدم المشروط لا ينافي وجود الشرط وعدم الضد عن المحل يصحح طريان الضد الآخر، بخلاف عدم غير الضد ولما لم يكن التمايز إلا بحسب التعقل، الذي وقع الخلاف في أنه هل هو وجود ذهني أو لا . . ؟
Fifth Section. Distinction of Non-Existents in the Intellect and What Pertains to That. He said: Fifth Section: Non-existents have distinction in the intellect, such as the specific attribution of the non-existence of the effect to the non-existence of the cause, and the non-existence of the condition being incompatible with the existence of the conditioned, and the non-existence of the contrary enabling the existence of the other contrary. There has been a well-known disagreement concerning the distinction of non-existents. If it is intended that distinction is not a realized matter in the external world or that non-existents or non-existent things do not have distinct essences, then it is necessary and no dispute can be conceived about it. But if it is intended that the concept of non-existence has no distinct individuals in the intellect each characterized by specific judgments true in themselves, then it is false. Because the non-existence of the cause necessitates the non-existence of the effect without the reverse, and the non-existence of the condition is incompatible with the existence of the conditioned, and the non-existence of the conditioned does not negate the existence of the condition, and the non-existence of the contrary in the subject makes possible the occurrence of the other contrary, unlike the non-existence of what is not a contrary. And since distinction is only according to intellection, about which disagreement has occurred as to whether it is mental existence or not.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage opens the fifth section by defining the doctrine that non-existents are distinct in the intellect. It first quotes the matn, which gives three examples: (1) the non-existence of the effect is specifically linked to the non-existence of its cause, (2) the non-existence of a condition is incompatible with the existence of the conditioned, and (3) the non-existence of a contrary allows the other contrary to occur. Then the commentary explains the disagreement: if one means external distinction, it is obvious that non-existents have no external reality, so no dispute; but if one means conceptual distinction in the intellect, it is true because each non-existence has unique logical relations (e.g., the non-existence of the cause implies the non-existence of the effect, but not vice versa). The passage concludes by linking this distinction to the broader debate on mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). The argument flows from accepting that distinction exists in the mind to asking whether that constitutes a kind of existence.

The Correct Relation Between Mental Existence and Distinction of Non-Existents
Al-Taftazani corrects al-Isfahani on the subordination of the two debates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct relationship between the debate on mental existence and the distinction of non-existents, and how al-Taftazani corrects al-Isfahani's view.

Core Idea
Affirming mental existence enables distinction of non-existents; denying it prevents distinction.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid tackles a subtle connection between two famous kalam debates: the distinction of non-existents and mental existence. Al-Isfahani claimed that affirming mental existence leads to denying the distinction of non-existents. But al-Taftazani shows that the truth is the opposite. Let's examine his argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first a reported view, then a correction, then the reasoning.
2
Understand al-Isfahani's claim
Identify what the author of al-Mawaqif held: that the disagreement on distinction of non-existents is a branch of the disagreement on mental existence. Note his claimed correlation: affirmers of mental existence deny distinction, deniers affirm it.
This is the view that al-Taftazani will correct.
3
See how al-Taftazani corrects it
The text then says 'the matter is the opposite'. Al-Taftazani points out that the philosophers who affirm mental existence actually affirm the distinction, while the mutakallimun who deny mental existence deny distinction.
This is the key correction.
4
Grasp the reasoning
The reason given: distinction is a positive attribute requiring the positive existence of the described. Mental existence provides that positive existence for non-existents as concepts, enabling distinction. Without any existence, no distinction.
This shows why the correct subordination is the opposite of what al-Isfahani claimed.
5
Reflect on the implications
Think about why this matters: it clarifies the positions of philosophers and mutakallimun on related issues and demonstrates how subtle distinctions in metaphysics have concrete effects on theological positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهب صاحب المواقف إلى أن الخلاف في تمايز الإعدام فرع الخلاف في الوجود الذهني، فمن أثبته نفاه لأن التمايز لا يكون إلا في العقل، أي بحسب التعقل والتصور فإن كان ذلك بوجود في الذهن، على ما هو رأي المثبتين لم يتصور معدوم مطلقاً. أي معدوم ليس له شائبة الوجود، لأن كل وجود ذهني، فلا يكون التمايز إلا للموجودات، ومن نفاه أثبته . لأن الإعدام ليست لها شائبة الوجود متمايزة في التصور، وأنت خبير بأن الأمر بالعكس. لأن الفلاسفة المثبتين للوجود الذهني يقولون : بتمايز الإعدام وجمهور المتكلمين النافين له هم القائلون بعدم تمايزها، فالأولى أن يقال في بيان التفرع أنه لما كان التميز عندهم وصفاً ثبوتياً، يستدعي ثبوت الموصوف به فمن أثبت الوجود الذهني حكم بتمايز الإعدام عند تصورها لما لها من الثبوت الذهني، وإن كانت هي إعداماً في أنفسها، ومن نفاه حكم بعدم التمايز لعدم الثبوت أصلاً.
The author of al-Mawaqif held that the disagreement on the distinction of non-existents is a branch of the disagreement on mental existence. Whoever affirms mental existence denies the distinction, because distinction can only be in the intellect, i.e., according to intellection and conception. If that is by an existence in the mind, according to the view of the affirmers, no non-existent can be conceived absolutely, i.e., a non-existent that has no trace of existence, because every [conception] is a mental existence. So distinction applies only to existents. And whoever denies mental existence affirms the distinction, because non-existents have no trace of existence yet are distinct in conception. And you are aware that the matter is the opposite. For the philosophers who affirm mental existence say: non-existents are distinct; and the majority of the mutakallimun who deny it are the ones who say they are not distinct. So it is more appropriate to say in explaining the branching: Since distinction in their view is a positive attribute requiring the positive existence of the described, so whoever affirms mental existence judges that non-existents are distinct when they are conceived, because they have mental existence, even though they are non-existents in themselves. And whoever denies mental existence judges that they are not distinct due to the lack of any existence at all.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage examines the connection between the debate on the distinction of non-existents and the controversy over mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). It first reports the view of al-Isfahani (sahib al-Mawaqif) that the former is subordinate to the latter: mental existence affirmers deny distinction, and deniers affirm it. But the author (al-Taftazani) corrects this, stating that the truth is the opposite: the philosophers (falasifa) who affirm mental existence also affirm the distinction of non-existents, while the majority of mutakallimun who deny mental existence deny distinction. The reason is that distinction is a positive attribute requiring the positive existence of the thing described; mental existence provides that positive existence for non-existents as concepts, enabling their distinction. Without any existence, there is no basis for distinction. Thus the correct subordination is: affirmation of mental existence leads to affirmation of distinction of non-existents, and denial of mental existence leads to denial of their distinction.

Key Terms
صاحب المواقف
الوجود الذهني
تمايز الإعدام
الفلاسفة
المتكلمون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That affirming mental existence necessarily leads to denying the distinction of non-existents (as claimed by al-Isfahani).
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that the opposite is true: affirming mental existence actually leads to affirming the distinction, because mental existence provides the positive existence needed for distinction.

Checkpoint

B) Mutakallimun denying mental existence
C) The author of al-Mawaqif
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The distinction of non-existents is based on their mental existence; those who affirm mental existence affirm their distinction, and those who deny mental existence deny their distinction.

Twofold Consideration of Non-Existence and the Objection on Judgment
Addresses an objection about judging non-existence and the twofold consideration of non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a kalam scholar resolves the apparent contradiction of judging non-existence by distinguishing two aspects of consideration.

Core Idea
Judging non-existence is not a contradiction—it depends on which aspect you consider.

This passage tackles a subtle logical puzzle: if non-existence is nothing, how can we say anything about it? The author first explains that non-existence can be considered in two ways—as a conceived concept and as absent reality. Then he addresses the objection that any judgment about non-existence would be self-contradictory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two considerations
Read the Arabic passage and translation once to grasp the twofold nature of non-existence. Look for the key terms that signal the two aspects.
Notice the phrase 'باعتبار' which indicates each consideration.
2
Understand the objection
The objection states that it is impossible to judge non-existence because it is not established, yet the very act of judging seems to affirm its existence as a subject.
This is a classic logical paradox.
3
Study the answer: two aspects prevent contradiction
The answer says that from the aspect where non-existence is established as a mental concept, judgment is valid; from the aspect where it is absent, judgment is impossible. The two considerations differ, so there is no contradiction.
Contradiction occurs only when the same aspect is used.
4
Apply the reasoning to similar cases
The same reasoning applies to the absolute unknown and the unimaginable—they can be judged as concepts even though they are not real objects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قال: والعدم قد يعرض لنفسه، بأن يعقل فيغفل عنه فيكون نوعاً من العدم باعتبار ومقابلا له باعتبار كما أن المعدوم المطلق ثابت باعتبار فيصح الحكم عليه وقسيم له باعتبار فيمتنع.
فإن قيل : فمن حيث إنه ليس بثابت يمتنع الحكم عليه وهذا حكم .
قلنا : نعم. لكن من حيث أنه ثابت ولا يناقض لاختلاف الاعتبارين، وكذا الحكم بامتناع الحكم على المجهول المطلق واللاممكن التصور .
He said: Non-existence may occur to itself, such that it is conceived and then overlooked; so it is a type of non-existence from one consideration and its opposite from another consideration. Similarly, absolute non-existence is established from one consideration so judgment about it is valid, and it is its opposite from another consideration so it is impossible. For it is said: Insofar as it is not established, judgment about it is impossible, yet this is a judgment. We say: Yes. But insofar as it is established, and there is no contradiction due to the difference of the two considerations. Similarly, the judgment that it is impossible to judge about the absolute unknown and the unimaginable.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage presents a matn and an objection-answer pair. The matn states that non-existence can be considered in two ways: as something conceived (hence a type of non-existence) and as something overlooked (hence opposite). Likewise, absolute non-existence is established from one aspect (as a concept) so judgment is valid, but from another aspect it is non-existent so judgment is impossible. The objection notes a paradox: if it is not established, judgment is impossible, yet we just made a judgment about it. The answer resolves this by distinguishing two considerations: from the aspect where it is established (as a mental concept), judgment is valid; from the aspect where it is not established, judgment is impossible. There is no contradiction because the considerations differ. The same applies to judgments about the absolute unknown and the unimaginable: from the conceptual aspect, we can judge that they cannot be judged, but from the aspect of their own reality, no judgment applies.

Key Terms
العدم
المعدوم المطلق
المجهول المطلق
اللاممكن التصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any judgment about non-existence is contradictory.
Correction: Judgment is possible from the aspect of its conceptual establishment, while impossible from the aspect of its real absence.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, how is judgment about non-existence possible?
A) By ignoring the contradiction
B) By considering it from the aspect of its establishment as a concept
C) By denying mental existence
D) By affirming mental existence

Key Takeaway
Non-existence can be judged in kalam by considering it as a mental concept, not as an absent reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The objection in the passage mirrors an inner voice that says, 'If this is impossible, why am I even thinking about it?' How can you act on the aspect where judgment is valid?

The passage says non-existence can be 'conceived and then overlooked.' What 'non-existence' in your faith—such as a sin you committed and then ignored—have you overlooked? How can you bring it into the light of judgment by considering it as a mental reality (i.e., acknowledging it) without getting stuck in the aspect of its absence (i.e., despair)?

The tamayyuz of non-existents depends on mental existence
Al-Taftazani explains that the disagreement on tamayyuz of non-existents is a branch of the mental existence debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the debate about mental existence determines the view on whether non-existents can be distinguished in the mind.

Core Idea
The tamayyuz of non-existents hinges on whether they have mental existence.

This passage shows how a seemingly abstract debate about mental existence has direct implications for distinguishing non-existents. Al-Taftazani clarifies the actual positions and corrects a common misconception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical flow and the correction Al-Taftazani makes.
Pay attention to the phrase 'the matter is the reverse'.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the positions: those who affirm mental existence and those who deny it. Note their respective views on tamayyuz of non-existents according to the initial statement.
3
Note the correction
Al-Taftazani says the matter is the reverse. Identify the actual alignment: philosophers (affirmers of mental existence) hold for tamayyuz, while mutakallimun (deniers) reject it.
4
Understand the dependency
The passage explains that tamayyuz is a positive attribute requiring existence. Those who affirm mental existence grant non-existents mental subsistence, thus they can be distinguished. Those who deny it have no basis for distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذهب صاحب المواقف إلى أن الخلاف في تمايز الإعدام فرع الخلاف في الوجود الذهني، فمن أثبته نفاه لأن التمايز لا يكون إلا في العقل، أي بحسب التعقل والتصور فإن كان ذلك بوجود في الذهن، على ما هو رأي المثبتين لم يتصور معدوم مطلقاً. أي معدوم ليس له شائبة الوجود، لأن كل وجود ذهني، فلا يكون التمايز إلا للموجودات، ومن نفاه أثبته . لأن الإعدام ليست لها شائبة الوجود متمايزة في التصور، وأنت خبير بأن الأمر بالعكس. لأن الفلاسفة المثبتين للوجود الذهني يقولون : بتمايز الإعدام وجمهور المتكلمين النافين له هم القائلون بعدم تمايزها، فالأولى أن يقال في بيان التفرع أنه لما كان التميز عندهم وصفاً ثبوتياً، يستدعي ثبوت الموصوف به فمن أثبت الوجود الذهني حكم بتمايز الإعدام عند تصورها لما لها من الثبوت الذهني، وإن كانت هي إعداماً في أنفسها، ومن نفاه حكم بعدم التمايز لعدم الثبوت أصلاً.
The author of al-Mawaqif held that the disagreement concerning the distinction (tamayyuz) of non-existents (al-`adam) is a branch of the disagreement concerning mental existence (al-wujud al-dhihni). For whoever affirms it denies it, because distinction only occurs in the mind, i.e., with respect to intellection and conception. If that is by an existence in the mind, according to the opinion of those who affirm it, then an absolute non-existent—i.e., a non-existent that has no trace of existence—cannot be conceived, because every existence is mental. Thus distinction only applies to existents. And whoever denies it affirms it, because non-existents have no trace of existence and are distinguished in conception. But you are well aware that the matter is the reverse. For the philosophers who affirm mental existence say that non-existents are distinguished, while the majority of the mutakallimun who deny it are those who say they are not distinguished. So it is better to say in explanation of the branching: since distinction (tamayyuz) in their view is a positive attribute requiring the existence of the qualified, whoever affirms mental existence judges that non-existents are distinguished upon their conception because of the mental subsistence they have, even though they are non-existents in themselves. And whoever denies it judges that there is no distinction due to the lack of any subsistence at all.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a key point: the disagreement about whether non-existents can be distinguished in the mind is dependent on the debate about mental existence. Al-Taftazani first states the position of the author of al-Mawaqif (al-Iji) that the disagreement over tamayyuz of non-existents is a branch of the disagreement over mental existence. He then explains: those who affirm mental existence deny tamayyuz of non-existents because distinction only occurs in the mind by way of conception. If that conception involves an existence in the mind (as held by those who affirm mental existence), then absolute non-existent—that which has no trace of existence—cannot be conceived. Thus distinction applies only to existents. Conversely, those who deny mental existence affirm tamayyuz of non-existents because non-existents have no trace of existence and are distinguished in conception. However, al-Taftazani corrects this: actually, the reverse is true. The philosophers who affirm mental existence are the ones who hold for tamayyuz of non-existents, while the majority of mutakallimun who deny mental existence are the ones who deny tamayyuz. He then rephrases the dependency: since tamayyuz for them is a positive attribute requiring the existence of the qualified, the one who affirms mental existence judges that non-existents are distinguished in conception because they have mental existence, even though they are non-existents in themselves. The one who denies mental existence judges that they are not distinguished due to lack of any existence.

Key Terms
تمايز
إعدام
وجود ذهني
معدوم مطلق
ثبوت ذهني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the debate about tamayyuz of non-existents is about their external existence rather than their mental existence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the debate is entirely about mental existence (wujud dhihni) and its implications for conception (tasawwur).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do those who affirm mental existence deny tamayyuz of non-existents?
A) Because distinction requires existence, and non-existents have no existence at all.
B) Because non-existents are not conceivable at all.
C) Because the philosophers affirm it, so the mutakallimun must deny it.
D) Because distinction can only occur in the external world.

Key Takeaway
The tamayyuz of non-existents is a direct consequence of one's stance on mental existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about abstract concepts like 'nothingness' or 'impossibility', do you grant them any reality in your mind? How does that affect the way you reason about them? Are you willing to accept that your own thoughts carry a kind of existence, and what does that mean for your accountability before Allah?
?
This lesson reveals that a common assumption about who holds which position was actually reversed. Have you ever discovered that a firmly held belief of yours was exactly the opposite of the truth? What would it take for you to undergo such a reversal today, even in matters of your own life and faith?
?
The distinction of non-existents requires that they have some subsistence to be distinguished. What 'non-existents' in your own spiritual life—sins omitted, duties neglected—do you fail to distinguish because you treat them as non-existent? How might acknowledging their mental reality change your response to them?

The Paradox of Judging Non-Existence
First objection-answer on judging non-existents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Taftazani resolves the apparent contradiction in judging non-existents by distinguishing between two respects (iʿtibārān).

Core Idea
Non-existence can be judged, but only in a qualified sense.

Al-Taftazani opens with a seeming paradox: if non-existence is not established, how can we judge it? Yet we do judge, as in 'non-existence is impossible to judge.' This lesson unpacks his initial answer, which relies on the crucial distinction between two respects (iʿtibārān) to avoid contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
Read the first sentence: 'Non-existence may occur to itself...' and note how Al-Taftazani introduces the idea that non-existence can be considered from two respects.
The passage uses 'iʿtibār' (respect) as a key term.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection: 'If it is said: Insofar as it is not established, judgment about it is impossible, yet this itself is a judgment.' This captures the paradox.
3
Analyze the answer
Examine the answer: 'Yes, but insofar as it is established, and there is no contradiction due to the difference of the two respects.' Understand how the two respects dissolve the contradiction.
The answer is short but foundational. Reflect on its implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: والعدم قد يعرض لنفسه، بأن يعقل فيغفل عنه فيكون نوعاً من العدم باعتبار ومقابلا له باعتبار كما أن المعدوم المطلق ثابت باعتبار فيصح الحكم عليه وقسيم له باعتبار فيمتنع .
فإن قيل : فمن حيث إنه ليس بثابت يمتنع الحكم عليه وهذا حكم .
قلنا : نعم. لكن من حيث أنه ثابت ولا يناقض لاختلاف الاعتبارين، وكذا الحكم بامتناع الحكم على المجهول المطلق واللاممكن التصور .
لما كان الحكم بتمايز الإعدام في التصور مظنة الاعتراض، بأن التمايز حينئذ يكون للموجودات الذهنية على ما هو رأي المحققين من الحكماء والمتكلمين. حاول التنبيه على الجواب بذكر مسائل تدل على أن العدم بالذات لا ينافي الوجود باعتبار منها أن العدم يعرض لنفسه بأن يتصور العدم المطلق، الذي هو نفي الكون في الأعيان، ثم يزول ذلك عن الذهن فيكون ذلك عروضاً للعدم على ما هو عدم في نفسه، وإن كان موجوداً من حيث حصوله في الذهن. ومنها أن زوال العـدم عن الذهن نوع من العدم المطلق من حيث كونه مضافاً إلى العدم، ومقابل له من حيث كونه نفياً لـه وسلباً. وفيها أن المعدوم المطلق، أعني ما ليس له ثبوت في الخارج ولا صورة في العقل ثابت من حيث إنه متصور الحكم عليه بامتناع الحكم عليه، وقسيم للثابت من حيث ذاته فيمتنع الحكم عليه لاستدعائه ثبوت المحكوم عليه في الجملة .
فإن قيل : فما لا يكون ثابتاً بوجه من الوجوه من حيث إنه لا ثابت يمتنع الحكم عليه، والحكم بامتناع الحكم حكم فيتناقض .
قلنا : صحة الحكم عليه بامتناع الحكم ليست من جهة أنه لا ثابت، بل من جهة أنه متصور ثابت في العقل وامتناع الحكم من جهة أنه لا ثابت في نفسه، وبحسب مفهومه ولا تناقض لاختلاف الجهتين وهذا هو الجواب عن الشبهة المشهورة على قولهم : الحكم على الشيء مشروط بتصوره بوجه ما وهي أنه لو صح ذلك لصدق قولنا لا شيء مما انتفى فيه هذا الشرط كالمجهول مطلقاً، يصح الحكم عليه ضرورة انتفاء المشروط بانتفاء الشرط ؛ واللازم باطل. لأن موضوع هذه السالبة ، إن كان ثابتاً، معلوماً بوجه صح الحكم عليه في الجملة فيكذب الحكم بعدم صحة الحكم.
He said: Non-existence ('adam) may occur to itself, by being conceived and then forgotten, so it becomes a kind of non-existence in one respect and its opposite in another respect, just as the absolute non-existent is established in one respect so that judgment about it is valid, and a counterpart to it in another respect so that judgment is impossible.
If it is said: Insofar as it is not established, judgment about it is impossible, yet this itself is a judgment.
We say: Yes, but insofar as it is established, and there is no contradiction due to the difference of the two respects. Likewise the judgment of the impossibility of judgment about the absolutely unknown and the inconceivable.
Since the judgment of the distinction of non-existents in conception is a point of objection, because distinction would then apply to mental existents according to the view of the verifiers among the philosophers and mutakallimun, he attempted to hint at the answer by mentioning matters that indicate that non-existence in itself does not contradict existence in a certain respect. Among them is that non-existence occurs to itself: by conceiving absolute non-existence, which is the negation of existence in external things, and then it ceases from the mind; so that is an occurrence of non-existence upon what is non-existence in itself, even if it is existent in terms of its occurrence in the mind. And among them is that the cessation of non-existence from the mind is a kind of absolute non-existence in terms of being added to non-existence, and an opposite to it in terms of being a negation and removal of it. And among them is that the absolute non-existent—I mean that which has no subsistence in the external and no form in the mind—is established insofar as it is conceived such that judgment of the impossibility of judgment about it is valid, and a counterpart to the established in terms of its essence, so judgment about it is impossible because it requires the subsistence of the subject in some sense.
If it is said: That which is not established in any respect, insofar as it is not established, judgment about it is impossible, and the judgment of the impossibility of judgment is a judgment, so there is a contradiction.
We say: The validity of the judgment of the impossibility of judgment about it is not because it is not established, but rather because it is conceived and established in the mind, and the impossibility of judgment is because it is not established in itself and according to its concept; and there is no contradiction due to the difference of the two aspects. And this is the answer to the famous objection to their statement: 'Judgment about a thing is conditioned by conceiving it in some respect', which is that if that were valid, our statement 'Nothing for which this condition is absent, like the absolutely unknown, is valid to judge about' would be true, due to the necessary negation of the conditioned by the negation of the condition; but the consequent is false, because the subject of this negative proposition, if it is established and known in some respect, judgment about it is valid in some sense, and thus the judgment of the impossibility of judgment would be false.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer complex that defends the possibility of judging non-existents. Al-Taftazani begins by presenting an initial statement: non-existence may 'occur to itself'—meaning it can be conceived and then forgotten, so it becomes a type of non-existence in one respect and its opposite in another. For example, the absolute non-existent is established (as a concept) in one respect, so judgment about it is valid, but it is a counterpart to the established in another respect, so judgment is impossible. An objection is then raised: if non-existence is not established, then judgment about it is impossible, yet the very judgment that it is impossible is itself a judgment, creating a contradiction. The answer is that there is no contradiction because the two aspects differ: it is not established in itself, but it is established as a concept in the mind. Al-Taftazani then expands: since the judgment of tamayyuz of non-existents in conception is a point of objection—because it would then apply to mental existents—he tries to hint at the answer by mentioning matters that show that non-existence in itself does not contradict existence in a certain respect. He lists three points: (1) non-existence occurs to itself by conceiving absolute non-existence and then it leaves the mind; (2) the cessation of non-existence from the mind is a type of absolute non-existence; (3) the absolute non-existent, which has no external subsistence and no mental form, is established as a concept such that the judgment of the impossibility of judgment about it is valid. Then another objection: if something is not established in any respect, then judgment about it is impossible, but the judgment of impossibility is a judgment, so there is a contradiction. The answer: the judgment that judgment is impossible is valid not because it is not established, but because it is conceived and established in the mind, while the impossibility is because it is not established in itself; no contradiction due to differing aspects. Finally, this is the answer to the famous objection about the principle that judgment is conditioned by conception: if that principle were taken absolutely, it would lead to the false conclusion that even the statement 'Nothing unknown can be judged' would be true, but its subject (the concept of 'nothing unknown') is itself known in some respect, so the judgment fails.

Key Terms
العدم
الحكم
الاعتبارين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something is not established in any way, any judgment about it is impossible, leaving no room for even the judgment of impossibility.
Correction: The judgment of impossibility is possible because the concept of the non-existent is established in the mind. Without mental subsistence, even that judgment would be impossible.

Checkpoint
What resolves the apparent contradiction in judging non-existents?
A) The judgment is about something else.
B) The judgment of impossibility is not a real judgment.
C) The two judgments refer to different respects (iʿtibārān).
D) Non-existents cannot be judged at all.

Key Takeaway
Judging non-existents is possible without contradiction by consistently applying the distinction between mental subsistence and external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever made an absolute judgment about something you only partially conceived, dismissing it as 'nothing' or 'impossible' without considering the perspective from which it might be real? How does this lesson urge you to pause and examine your assumptions before finalizing such judgments? Will you commit to distinguishing between what is mentally conceived and what is externally real in your daily reasoning?
?
This passage demonstrates that even the concept of 'nothing' can be discussed without contradiction if we acknowledge different respects. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes treat abstract theological concepts as if they must have full external reality, leading to confusion? How can you apply the principle of al-iʿtibārayn to better understand matters of faith that seem paradoxical? Are you willing to hold two 'respects' in tension rather than forcing a simplistic absolute?

Elaboration on Judging Non-Existents
Elaboration and final answer to the famous objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Taftazani's three-point elaboration and his answer to the famous objection (al-shubha al-mashhūra) regarding the condition of conception for judgment.

Core Idea
Non-existence itself can be an occasion for judgment through mental existence.

Al-Taftazani now provides a detailed response to an anticipated objection that the distinction of non-existents in conception would require them to be mental existents. He offers three examples showing that non-existence in itself does not contradict existence in a certain respect. He then addresses the famous objection about the condition of conception, concluding that the subject of the negative proposition is itself conceived, so the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the three points
Read the three examples starting with 'Among them is that non-existence occurs to itself...' and understand how each shows that non-existence can be considered in two ways.
Note that each point uses the concept of 'absolute non-existent' (al-maʿdūm al-muṭlaq).
2
Identify the second objection
Find the second objection: 'If it is said: That which is not established in any respect...' and note how it is similar to the first but stronger.
3
Analyze the answer to the famous objection
The final paragraph addresses the 'famous objection' about the condition of conception. Understand why the alleged conclusion 'Nothing unknown can be judged' is false because its subject is itself known.
This is a key argument in Islamic epistemology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: والعدم قد يعرض لنفسه، بأن يعقل فيغفل عنه فيكون نوعاً من العدم باعتبار ومقابلا له باعتبار كما أن المعدوم المطلق ثابت باعتبار فيصح الحكم عليه وقسيم له باعتبار فيمتنع .
فإن قيل : فمن حيث إنه ليس بثابت يمتنع الحكم عليه وهذا حكم .
قلنا : نعم. لكن من حيث أنه ثابت ولا يناقض لاختلاف الاعتبارين، وكذا الحكم بامتناع الحكم على المجهول المطلق واللاممكن التصور .

Checkpoint
According to Al-Taftazani, why does the famous objection fail?
A) Because the condition of conception is not necessary.
B) Because the subject of the negative proposition is itself known in some respect.
C) Because the impossibility of judging non-existents is absolute.
D) Because the objection is based on a logical fallacy.

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani's three examples and his answer to the famous objection firmly establish that non-existents can be judged in a qualified sense, contingent on their mental subsistence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a truth about yourself—like a sin or weakness—because you deemed it 'impossible' to confront, ignoring that you have mentally conceived it? How will you bring that concept to judgment this week?
?
The passage shows that even absolute non-existence can be judged through mental conception. What 'non-existent' virtue in your life—patience, gratitude, diligence—have you failed to conceive clearly and thus not pursued? Commit to forming a clear mental image of it today.
?
Al-Taftazani distinguishes between mental existence and external impossibility. Is there a change in your character that you accept mentally but treat as impossible to achieve? Identify one concrete step you can take to move that change from mental conception to external reality in the next 24 hours.

6 min
The Problem of Judging Non-Existents
Taftazani introduces the objection about differentiating non-existents and presents two initial issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the differentiation of non-existents in conception raises an objection and how Taftazani introduces two issues to show that absolute non-existence can be considered in relation to existence.

Core Idea
Non-existence can be conceived, and that conception itself has a kind of mental existence.

Taftazani begins by addressing a fundamental objection regarding judgments on non-existents. If non-existents are differentiated in the mind, that differentiation applies to mental existents, not to the non-existents themselves. To resolve this, he presents two issues that demonstrate that absolute non-existence is not entirely incompatible with existence when considered from certain perspectives.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main objection
Focus on the opening sentence where Taftazani states that the differentiation of non-existents in conception is a plausible occasion for objection.
The objection is that the differentiation would then be of mental existents.
2
Note the two issues Taftazani raises
The first issue: absolute non-existence is conceived, and its removal from the mind is an occurrence of non-existence on what is non-existent in itself. The second: the removal itself is a type of absolute non-existence.
3
Reflect on the purpose of these issues
Consider how these issues show that non-existence can be related to existence through mental consideration, preparing for the resolution of the sophism.
These issues indicate that absolute non-existence does not conflict with existence in some respects.

لما كان الحكم بتمايز الإعدام في التصور مظنة الاعتراض، بأن التمايز حينئذ يكون للموجودات الذهنية على ما هو رأي المحققين من والمتكلمين. حاول التنبيه على الجواب بذكر مسائل تدل على أن العدم بالذات لا ينافي الوجود باعتبار منها أن العدم يعرض لنفسه بأن يتصور العدم المطلق، الذي هو نفي الكون في الأعيان، ثم يزول ذلك عن الذهن فيكون ذلك عروضاً للعدم على ما هو عدم في نفسه، وإن كان موجوداً من حيث حصوله في الذهن. ومنها أن زوال العدم عن الذهن نوع من العدم المطلق من حيث كونه مضافاً إلى العدم، ومقابل له من حيث كونه نفياً له وسلباً. وفيها أن المعدوم المطلق، أعني ما ليس له ثبوت في الخارج ولا صورة في العقل ثابت من حيث إنه متصور الحكم عليه بامتناع الحكم عليه، وقسيم للثابت من حيث ذاته فيمتنع الحكم عليه لاستدعائه ثبوت المحكوم عليه في الجملة. فإن قيل: فما لا يكون ثابتاً بوجه من الوجوه من حيث إنه لا ثابت يمتنع الحكم عليه، والحكم بامتناع الحكم حكم فيتناقض. قلنا: صحة الحكم عليه بامتناع الحكم ليست من جهة أنه لا ثابت، بل من جهة أنه متصور ثابت في العقل وامتناع الحكم من جهة أنه لا ثابت في نفسه، وبحسب مفهومه ولا تناقض لاختلاف الجهتين وهذا هو الجواب عن الشبهة المشهورة على قولهم: الحكم على الشيء مشروط بتصوره بوجه ما وهي أنه لو صح ذلك لصدق قولنا لا شيء مما انتفى فيه هذا الشرط كالمجهول مطلقاً، يصح الحكم عليه ضرورة انتفاء المشروط بانتفاء الشرط؛ واللازم باطل. لأن موضوع هذه السالبة، إن كان ثابتاً، معلوماً بوجه صح الحكم عليه في الجملة فيكذب الحكم بعدم صحة الحكم أصلا، وإن كان مجهولاً مطلقاً، والحكم بعدم صحة الحكم حكم فتناقض، لأن بعض المجهول المطلق صح الحكم عليه، وقد يجاب بأن القضية مشروطة، أي لا يصح الحكم عليه ما دام مجهولاً مطلقاً، وهي لا تناقض المطلقة، وهو مدفوع بأدنى تغير، وهو أن يقيد انتفاء الشرط بالدوام، أي ما يكون مجهولاً مطلقاً دائماً لا يصح الحكم عليه دائماً، أو يعتبر إمكان التصور. فيقال: لو كان الحكم على الشيء مشروطاً بتصوره، لكان مشروطاً بإمكان تصوره ضرورة فيلزم أن لا يمكن الحكم على ما لا يمكن تصوره أصلاً، والحكم بعدم الإمكان حكم، وبالجملة فالشبهة مما يورد في موارد كثيرة مثل قولنا ضرب فعل ماض ومن حرف جر، وليس باسم، ومالا أصلا، ليس بكلي إلى غير ذلك، فينبغي أن يكون الجواب حاسماً للمادة، وحاصله أن الموضوع في أمثال هذه القضايا متعدد. فالمجهول المطلق من حيث ذاته ممتنع الحكم عليه، ومن حيث كونه متصوراً محكوم عليه، وضرب من حيث ذاته فعل ومن حيث كونه هذا اللفظ اسم وهكذا. وقد يقال في بيان بطلان قولنا: لا شيء من المجهول مطلقاً، يصح الحكم عليه أن كل مجهول مطلقاً فهو شيء أو لا شيء وممكن أو لا ممكن وبالجملة فإما (ب) أو ليس (ب). ضرورة امتناع ارتفاع النقيضين وفيه منع أنا لا نسم ظاهر، وهو نسلم صدق شيء من هذه القضايا، إنما يلزم ارتفاع النقيضين لو سلبا عن شيء واحد وههنا كما لا سلب لا إيجاب، لأن كلا منهما حكم مشروط بتصور الموضوع فلذا بينه القوم بطريق الترديد على ما ذكرنا.
Since the judgment of the differentiation of non-existents in conception is a plausible occasion for objection, that the differentiation then would be for mental existents according to the view of the verifiers among the philosophers and theologians, he attempted to alert to the answer by mentioning issues that indicate that absolute non-existence does not conflict with existence by consideration, among them that non-existence occurs to itself in that absolute non-existence is conceived, which is the negation of existence in external objects, then that disappears from the mind, so that is an occurrence of non-existence upon what is non-existence in itself, although it is existent in terms of its occurrence in the mind. And among them that the removal of non-existence from the mind is a type of absolute non-existence in terms of being added to non-existence, and opposite to it in terms of being a negation and privation of it. And in it that the absolutely non-existent, meaning that which has no reality in the external nor a form in the intellect, is established in terms of being conceived, the judgment upon it of the impossibility of judgment upon it, and it is a counterpart of the established in terms of its essence, so judgment upon it is impossible because it requires the establishment of the subject of judgment in some way. If it is said: That which is not established in any way, in terms of its not being established, is impossible to judge upon, and the judgment of impossibility of judgment is a judgment, so there is a contradiction. We say: The validity of judgment upon it of the impossibility of judgment is not from the perspective that it is not established, but from the perspective that it is conceived, established in the intellect, and the impossibility of judgment is from the perspective that it is not established in itself, and according to its concept, and there is no contradiction because of the difference of the two perspectives. And this is the answer to the well-known sophism regarding their statement: Judgment on a thing is conditional upon its conception in some way, which is that if that were correct, then our statement 'Nothing in which this condition is absent, like the absolutely unknown, is valid to judge upon' would be true, necessarily because the conditioned is negated by the negation of the condition; and the consequent is false. Because the subject of this negative proposition, if it is established, known in some way, then judgment upon it is valid in some respect, so the judgment that judgment is not valid at all is false; and if it is absolutely unknown, and the judgment that judgment is not valid is a judgment, so there is a contradiction, because some of the absolutely unknown has been judged upon. And it may be answered that the proposition is conditional, i.e., judgment is not valid upon it as long as it is absolutely unknown, and it does not contradict the absolute proposition, but this is refuted by the slightest change: that the negation of the condition is restricted to perpetuity, i.e., that which is perpetually absolutely unknown is not valid to judge upon perpetually, or the possibility of conception is considered. So it is said: If judgment on a thing were conditional upon its conception, it would necessarily be conditional upon the possibility of its conception, so it would follow that judgment is not possible upon that which cannot be conceived at all, and the judgment of impossibility is a judgment. In short, the sophism is adduced in many instances, such as our statement 'daraba is a past tense verb, and min is a particle of jar, and it is not a noun, and that which has no origin is not a universal,' etc. So the answer should be decisive regarding the matter, and its summary is that the subject in such propositions is multiple. So the absolutely unknown, in terms of its essence, is impossible to judge upon, and in terms of being conceived, it is judged upon; and 'daraba' in terms of its essence is a verb, and in terms of being this utterance is a noun, and so on. And it may be said in explaining the falsity of our statement 'Nothing of the absolutely unknown is valid to judge upon' that every absolutely unknown is either a thing or not a thing, and possible or not possible, and in short either B or not B, necessarily because of the impossibility of the removal of the two contradictories. But there is the objection that we do not concede that any of these propositions are true; the removal of the two contradictories would only follow if both negations were about one thing, but here there is neither negation nor affirmation, because each of them is a judgment conditional upon the conception of the subject. That is why the people clarified it by way of disjunction as we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage discusses the philosophical and theological problem of how we can make judgments about non-existent things. The author begins by noting that the differentiation of non-existents in conception might be objected to because it would mean the differentiation is of mental existents, not of non-existents themselves. To address this, he proposes issues showing that absolute non-existence does not conflict with existence in some respects. For example, absolute non-existence (like the negation of external existence) can be conceived, and when it ceases to be conceived, that change is an occurrence of non-existence on something that is non-existent in itself, though it is existent as a mental entity. Another example is that the removal of non-existence from the mind is itself a type of non-existence. Then he explains that the absolutely non-existent (something with no external reality and no mental form) is still 'established' as a conceived object, and we can judge that it cannot be judged (i.e., we can say 'it is impossible to judge it'), but that judgment is from the perspective of its being conceived, not from its own non-existence. This avoids contradiction because the two perspectives differ. The author then addresses a famous sophism: 'Judgment on a thing is conditional upon its conception.' If that were true, then the statement 'Nothing that lacks this condition (like the absolutely unknown) is valid to judge' would be true, but it leads to self-contradiction because the subject of that negative proposition either is known (so judgment is possible, making the claim false) or is absolutely unknown (but then the claim itself is a judgment about it, contradicting itself). The answer is that the proposition is conditional, but that is refuted by restricting the condition to perpetuity. The real resolution is that the subject is multiple: the absolutely unknown in itself is not judged, but as conceived, it is judged. Similarly, 'daraba' in itself is a verb, but as an utterance it can be considered a noun (in terms of its word form). Finally, the author discusses another way to show the falsity of denying judgment on the absolutely unknown by using disjunction (every absolutely unknown is either a thing or not a thing, etc.), but notes that this might be rejected because such judgments themselves require conception of the subject. The correct approach is the one mentioned: the subject is multiple.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Non-existence is absolutely nothing and cannot be related to existence in any way.
Correction: Taftazani shows that absolute non-existence can be conceived and that the conception has mental existence, so there is a relation.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, what is the objection to the differentiation of non-existents in conception?
A) It leads to infinite regress.
B) It would make the differentiation apply to mental existents.
C) It contradicts the principle of non-contradiction.
D) It requires external existence.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani introduces two issues to show that absolute non-existence can be conceived and thus has a mental aspect that allows judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You entertain many thoughts every day—some about things that do not exist in the external world. This lesson shows that even those thoughts have a mental existence that is real before Allah. Do you take responsibility for the content of your mind as seriously as you take your actions? What false conceptions are you allowing to exist in your mind right now that you need to remove?
?
When you conceive of your own death or the Day of Judgment, you are engaging with non-existents that have no external present reality. Yet your conception of them will be accounted for. Will your mental image of the Hereafter be accurate enough to drive you to action, or is it a vague notion that disappears without effect?
?
The removal of a false belief from your mind is itself a type of non-existence. Have you ever genuinely removed a mistaken belief about Allah or His attributes? Or do you merely suppress it? This lesson calls you to actively negate falsehood from your mind. Will you take a specific false idea today and work to erase it completely?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
5 min
The Absolutely Non-Existent and the Resolution of Contradiction
Taftazani defines the absolutely non-existent and resolves the objection of contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani defines the absolutely non-existent and resolves the apparent contradiction of judging it.

Core Idea
The absolutely non-existent is conceived in the mind, so it can be judged from that perspective without contradiction.

After setting up the issues, Taftazani defines the absolutely non-existent (al-ma'dum al-mutlaq) as something with no external reality and no mental form. Yet he asserts that it is 'established' as a conceived object. This leads to a potential contradiction: if it is truly non-existent, how can we judge about it? He resolves this by distinguishing two perspectives: the thing in itself and the thing as conceived.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the absolutely non-existent
Identify what Taftazani means by 'absolutely non-existent': that which has no reality in the external and no form in the intellect.
2
Identify the objection and Taftazani's answer
The objection: if something is not established at all, judging that it cannot be judged is itself a judgment, leading to contradiction. Taftazani's answer: the judgment is valid from the perspective of its being conceived, not from its own essence.
3
Understand why there is no contradiction
The two perspectives differ: as conceived it is established in the mind; as non-existent in itself it is not. Thus, no contradiction.
This is the key insight: difference of perspectives (ikhitilaf al-jahat).

Then he explains that the absolutely non-existent (something with no external reality and no mental form) is still 'established' as a conceived object, and we can judge that it cannot be judged (i.e., we can say 'it is impossible to judge it'), but that judgment is from the perspective of its being conceived, not from its own non-existence. This avoids contradiction because the two perspectives differ. The author then addresses a famous sophism: 'Judgment on a thing is conditional upon its conception.' If that were true, then the statement 'Nothing that lacks this condition (like the absolutely unknown) is valid to judge' would be true, but it leads to self-contradiction because the subject of that negative proposition either is known (so judgment is possible, making the claim false) or is absolutely unknown (but then the claim itself is a judgment about it, contradicting itself). The answer is that the proposition is conditional, but that is refuted by restricting the condition to perpetuity. The real resolution is that the subject is multiple: the absolutely unknown in itself is not judged, but as conceived, it is judged. Similarly, 'daraba' in itself is a verb, but as an utterance it can be considered a noun (in terms of its word form). Finally, the author discusses another way to show the falsity of denying judgment on the absolutely unknown by using disjunction (every absolutely unknown is either a thing or not a thing, etc.), but notes that this might be rejected because such judgments themselves require conception of the subject. The correct approach is the one mentioned: the subject is multiple.

Key Terms
المعدوم المطلق
الحكم
الثبوت
التناقض
الجهات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is absolutely non-existent, we cannot think about it or say anything about it at all.
Correction: We can conceive of absolute non-existence, and from that perspective we can make judgments about it, such as that it cannot be judged from its own essence.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, why is there no contradiction in judging that the absolutely non-existent cannot be judged?
A) Because the absolutely non-existent does not exist.
B) Because the judgment is made from the perspective of its being conceived, not from its own essence.
C) Because the judgment itself is false.
D) Because the absolutely non-existent is actually existent.

Key Takeaway
The key to resolving the contradiction is the difference of perspectives: the absolutely non-existent as conceived in the mind is the subject of judgment, while as non-existent in itself it cannot be judged.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held two beliefs that seemed contradictory until you realized you were looking at them from different perspectives? How can you apply Taftazani's distinction between mental concept and external reality to resolve a doubt you currently have about Allah's attributes?
?
The absolutely non-existent has a mental existence as a concept. This means that even non-existence is 'something' in our minds. How does this truth affect your understanding of what it means for something to be 'nothing'? Can you truly conceive of absolute nothingness?
?
Taftazani says the judgment of impossibility is valid because it is from the perspective of the concept. When you say 'God cannot be seen in this world' or 'nothing is like Him', are you aware that these judgments are made from a particular perspective (e.g., God's essence vs. our perception)? How can this awareness guard you against anthropomorphism or denying divine reality?

The Sophism on Judgment and Its Resolution
Taftazani explains and resolves the well-known sophism regarding judgment and conception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the well-known sophism about judgment being conditional on conception, and Taftazani's decisive resolution using the multiple-subject analysis.

Core Idea
The sophism is resolved by recognizing that the subject of judgment is multiple: in itself and as conceived.

Taftazani now presents the famous sophism that arises from the principle 'Judgment on a thing is conditional upon its conception.' This sophism leads to a contradiction when applied to the absolutely unknown. He discusses various attempted answers and ultimately provides the definitive resolution: the subject is considered from multiple perspectives, allowing judgment without contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the sophism and its reasoning
The sophism: if judgment requires conception, then 'Nothing lacking conception can be judged' would be true, but this leads to contradiction because its subject either is known (so the judgment is false) or is unknown (but then the judgment itself is a judgment about it).
2
See why the initial conditional answer is insufficient
One says the judgment is only conditionally true while the thing is unknown, but this fails because if we specify perpetual unknown, the problem remains.
3
Learn Taftazani's definitive answer: the subject is multiple
The absolutely unknown in itself cannot be judged, but as conceived it is judged. This applies to other examples like 'daraba' being a verb in essence but a noun as an utterance.
4
Consider the additional disjunction argument
An alternative attempt uses disjunction to show falsity, but it is rejected because the disjunctive judgments themselves require conception of the subject.
The correct is the multiple-subject analysis.

لما كان الحكم بتمايز الإعدام في التصور مظنة الاعتراض، بأن التمايز حينئذ يكون للموجودات الذهنية على ما هو رأي المحققين من والمتكلمين. حاول التنبيه على الجواب بذكر مسائل تدل على أن العدم بالذات لا ينافي الوجود باعتبار منها أن العدم يعرض لنفسه بأن يتصور العدم المطلق، الذي هو نفي الكون في الأعيان، ثم يزول ذلك عن الذهن فيكون ذلك عروضاً للعدم على ما هو عدم في نفسه، وإن كان موجوداً من حيث حصوله في الذهن. ومنها أن زوال العدم عن الذهن نوع من العدم المطلق من حيث كونه مضافاً إلى العدم، ومقابل له من حيث كونه نفياً له وسلباً. وفيها أن المعدوم المطلق، أعني ما ليس له ثبوت في الخارج ولا صورة في العقل ثابت من عليه، والحكم بامتناع الحكم حكم فيتناقض. قلنا: صحة الحكم عليه بامتناع الحكم ليست من جهة أنه لا ثابت، بل من جهة أنه متصور ثابت في العقل وامتناع الحكم من جهة أنه لا ثابت في نفسه، وبحسب مفهومه ولا تناقض لاختلاف الجهتين وهذا هو الجواب عن الشبهة المشهورة على قولهم: الحكم على الشيء مشروط بتصوره بوجه ما وهي أنه لو صح ذلك لصدق قولنا لا شيء مما انتفى فيه هذا الشرط كالمجهول مطلقاً، يصح الحكم عليه ضرورة انتفاء المشروط بانتفاء الشرط؛ واللازم باطل. لأن موضوع هذه السالبة، إن كان ثابتاً، معلوماً بوجه صح الحكم عليه في الجملة فيكذب الحكم بعدم صحة الحكم أصلا، وإن كان مجهولاً مطلقاً، والحكم بعدم صحة الحكم حكم فتناقض، لأن بعض المجهول المطلق صح الحكم عليه، وقد يجاب بأن القضية مشروطة، أي لا يصح الحكم عليه ما دام مجهولاً مطلقاً، وهي لا تناقض المطلقة، وهو مدفوع بأدنى تغير، وهو أن يقيد انتفاء الشرط بالدوام، أي ما يكون مجهولاً مطلقاً دائماً لا يصح الحكم عليه دائماً، أو يعتبر إمكان التصور. فيقال: لو كان الحكم على الشيء مشروطاً بتصوره، لكان مشروطاً بإمكان تصوره ضرورة فيلزم أن لا يمكن الحكم على ما لا يمكن تصوره أصلاً، والحكم بعدم الإمكان حكم، وبالجملة فالشبهة مما يورد في موارد كثيرة مثل قولنا ضرب فعل ماض ومن حرف جر، وليس باسم، ومالا أصلا، ليس بكلي إلى غير ذلك، فينبغي أن يكون الجواب حاسماً للمادة، وحاصله أن الموضوع في أمثال هذه القضايا متعدد. فالمجهول المطلق من حيث ذاته ممتنع الحكم عليه، ومن حيث كونه متصوراً محكوم عليه، وضرب من حيث ذاته فعل ومن حيث كونه هذا اللفظ اسم وهكذا. وقد يقال في بيان بطلان قولنا: لا شيء من المجهول مطلقاً، يصح الحكم عليه أن كل مجهول مطلقاً فهو شيء أو لا شيء وممكن أو لا ممكن وبالجملة فإما (ب) أو ليس (ب). ضرورة امتناع ارتفاع النقيضين وفيه منع أنا لا نسم ظاهر، وهو نسلم صدق شيء من هذه القضايا، إنما يلزم ارتفاع النقيضين لو سلبا عن شيء واحد وههنا كما لا سلب لا إيجاب، لأن كلا منهما حكم مشروط بتصور الموضوع فلذا بينه القوم بطريق الترديد على ما ذكرنا.
Since the judgment of the differentiation of non-existents in conception is a plausible occasion for objection, that the differentiation then would be for mental existents according to the view of the verifiers among the philosophers and theologians, he attempted to alert to the answer by mentioning issues that indicate that absolute non-existence does not conflict with existence by consideration, among them that non-existence occurs to itself in that absolute non-existence is conceived, which is the negation of existence in external objects, then that disappears from the mind, so that is an occurrence of non-existence upon what is non-existence in itself, although it is existent in terms of its occurrence in the mind. And among them that the removal of non-existence from the mind is a type of absolute non-existence in terms of being added to non-existence, and opposite to it in terms of being a negation and privation of it. And in it that the absolutely non-existent, meaning that which has no reality in the external nor a form in the intellect, is established in terms of being conceived, the judgment upon it of the impossibility of judgment upon it, and it is a counterpart of the established in terms of its essence, so judgment upon it is impossible because it requires the establishment of the subject of judgment in some way. If it is said: That which is not established in any way, in terms of its not being established, is impossible to judge upon, and the judgment of impossibility of judgment is a judgment, so there is a contradiction. We say: The validity of judgment upon it of the impossibility of judgment is not from the perspective that it is not established, but from the perspective that it is conceived, established in the intellect, and the impossibility of judgment is from the perspective that it is not established in itself, and according to its concept, and there is no contradiction because of the difference of the two perspectives. And this is the answer to the well-known sophism regarding their statement: Judgment on a thing is conditional upon its conception in some way, which is that if that were correct, then our statement 'Nothing in which this condition is absent, like the absolutely unknown, is valid to judge upon' would be true, necessarily because the conditioned is negated by the negation of the condition; and the consequent is false. Because the subject of this negative proposition, if it is established, known in some way, then judgment upon it is valid in some respect, so the judgment that judgment is not valid at all is false; and if it is absolutely unknown, and the judgment that judgment is not valid is a judgment, so there is a contradiction, because some of the absolutely unknown has been judged upon. And it may be answered that the proposition is conditional, i.e., judgment is not valid upon it as long as it is absolutely unknown, and it does not contradict the absolute proposition, but this is refuted by the slightest change: that the negation of the condition is restricted to perpetuity, i.e., that which is perpetually absolutely unknown is not valid to judge upon perpetually, or the possibility of conception is considered. So it is said: If judgment on a thing were conditional upon its conception, it would necessarily be conditional upon the possibility of its conception, so it would follow that judgment is not possible upon that which cannot be conceived at all, and the judgment of impossibility is a judgment. In short, the sophism is adduced in many instances, such as our statement 'daraba is a past tense verb, and min is a particle of jar, and it is not a noun, and that which has no origin is not a universal,' etc. So the answer should be decisive regarding the matter, and its summary is that the subject in such propositions is multiple. So the absolutely unknown, in terms of its essence, is impossible to judge upon, and in terms of being conceived, it is judged upon; and 'daraba' in terms of its essence is a verb, and in terms of being this utterance is a noun, and so on. And it may be said in explaining the falsity of our statement 'Nothing of the absolutely unknown is valid to judge upon' that every absolutely unknown is either a thing or not a thing, and possible or not possible, and in short either B or not B, necessarily because of the impossibility of the removal of the two contradictories. But there is the objection that we do not concede that any of these propositions are true; the removal of the two contradictories would only follow if both negations were about one thing, but here there is neither negation nor affirmation, because each of them is a judgment conditional upon the conception of the subject. That is why the people clarified it by way of disjunction as we have mentioned.

Commentary
This passage discusses the philosophical and theological problem of how we can make judgments about non-existent things. The author begins by noting that the differentiation of non-existents in conception might be objected to because it would mean the differentiation is of mental existents, not of non-existents themselves. To address this, he proposes issues showing that absolute non-existence does not conflict with existence in some respects. For example, absolute non-existence (like the negation of external existence) can be conceived, and when it ceases to be conceived, that change is an occurrence of non-existence on something that is non-existent in itself, though it is existent as a mental entity. Another example is that the removal of non-existence from the mind is itself a type of non-existence. Then he explains that the absolutely non-existent (something with no external reality and no mental form) is still 'established' as a conceived object, and we can judge that it cannot be judged (i.e., we can say 'it is impossible to judge it'), but that judgment is from the perspective of its being conceived, not from its own non-existence. This avoids contradiction because the two perspectives differ. The author then addresses a famous sophism: 'Judgment on a thing is conditional upon its conception.' If that were true, then the statement 'Nothing that lacks this condition (like the absolutely unknown) is valid to judge' would be true, but it leads to self-contradiction because the subject of that negative proposition either is known (so judgment is possible, making the claim false) or is absolutely unknown (but then the claim itself is a judgment about it, contradicting itself). The answer is that the proposition is conditional, but that is refuted by restricting the condition to perpetuity. The real resolution is that the subject is multiple: the absolutely unknown in itself is not judged, but as conceived, it is judged. Similarly, 'daraba' in itself is a verb, but as an utterance it can be considered a noun (in terms of its word form). Finally, the author discusses another way to show the falsity of denying judgment on the absolutely unknown by using disjunction (every absolutely unknown is either a thing or not a thing, etc.), but notes that this might be rejected because such judgments themselves require conception of the subject. The correct approach is the one mentioned: the subject is multiple.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sophism proves that we cannot make any judgment about the absolutely unknown.
Correction: We can judge about the absolutely unknown from the perspective of its being conceived, but not from its own essence.

Checkpoint
What is Taftazani's definitive answer to the sophism?
A) The judgment is conditional only while the thing is unknown.
B) The subject is multiple, so what is impossible from one perspective is possible from another.
C) The sophism is invalid because judgment does not require conception.
D) The absolutely unknown does not exist.

Key Takeaway
The sophism is resolved by recognizing that the same thing can be considered from multiple perspectives, allowing judgment from one perspective while denying it from another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter something you do not understand at all—like a complex theological concept—do you instinctively pass a judgment of rejection or dismissal without first trying to conceive it? Is that judgment itself a violation of the principle that judgment requires conception?
?
You have probably made absolute statements about people or groups you barely know. In light of Taftazani’s resolution that the 'absolutely unknown' can be judged only in terms of its being conceived, how often do you confuse the essence of a person with your limited conception of them? Will you stop and separate the two before your next unjust verdict?
?
The sophism shows that if you treat the subject as single, you fall into contradiction. Where in your own life are you treating a complex matter (your own soul, another person, a divine attribute) as if it has only one dimension, thereby pulling yourself into confusion? What would it cost you to admit the multiplicity and see from the other perspective?

Resolving the Sophism of Judging the Absolutely Unknown
Logical analysis of a sophism about the absolutely unknown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that nothing can be judged about the absolutely unknown is self-refuting, and how the resolution lies in distinguishing between the essence of a thing and its conception.

Core Idea
Even the unknown can be discussed if we separate its essence from its conception.

The following passage addresses a classic logical puzzle: Is it valid to make any judgment about something that is completely unknown? At first glance, it seems contradictory to speak of the unknown, yet we often do. Sanusi presents the sophism, considers an attempted solution, and then delivers the decisive answer by showing that the subject can be viewed under multiple aspects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the paradox
Read the opening lines carefully. Notice how claiming 'no judgment is valid on the absolutely unknown' becomes a judgment itself, creating a contradiction.
This is the initial problem that the passage seeks to resolve.
2
Examine the conditional answer and its refutation
The passage presents a possible answer: the judgment is conditional on being unknown. Then it shows a minimal change refutes this, leading to a stronger argument about conception.
Pay attention to the phrase 'wa huwa maddūf' – the refutation.
3
Understand the correct resolution
Sanusi explains that the subject must be considered under different aspects: as an essence (dhāt) it cannot be judged, but as something conceived (mutasawwar) it can.
The example of 'daraba' as a verb vs. a noun illustrates this.
4
Analyze the disjunctive argument and its rejection
A disjunctive proof is offered but rejected on the grounds that judgments are conditioned on conception; without conception, no affirmation or negation exists.
This reinforces the central point that judgment requires prior conception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصلا، وإن كان مجهولاً مطلقاً، والحكم بعدم صحة الحكم حكم فتناقض، لأن بعض المجهول المطلق صح الحكم عليه، وقد يجاب بأن القضية مشروطة، أي لا يصح الحكم عليه ما دام مجهولاً مطلقاً، وهي لا تناقض المطلقة، وهو مدفوع بأدنى تغير، وهو أن يقيد انتفاء الشرط بالدوام، أي ما يكون مجهولاً مطلقاً دائماً لا يصح الحكم عليه دائماً، أو يعتبر إمكان التصور. يتصور فيقال: لو كان الحكم على الشيء مشروطاً بتصوره، لكان مشروطاً بإمكان تصوره ضرورة فيلزم أن لا يمكن الحكم على ما لا يمكن تصوره أصلاً، والحكم بعدم الإمكان حكم، وبالجملة فالشبهة مما يورد في موارد كثيرة مثل قولنا ضرب فعل ماض ومن حرف جر، وليس باسم، ومالا أصلا، ليس بكلي إلى غير ذلك، فينبغي أن يكون الجواب حاسماً للمادة، وحاصله أن الموضوع في أمثال هذه القضايا متعدد. فالمجهول المطلق من حيث ذاته ممتنع الحكم عليه، ومن حيث كونه متصوراً محكوم عليه، وضرب من حيث ذاته فعل ومن حيث كونه هذا اللفظ اسم وهكذا. وقد يقال في بيان بطلان قولنا : لا شيء من المجهول مطلقاً، يصح الحكم عليه أن كل مجهول مطلقاً فهو شيء أو لا شيء وممكن أو لا ممكن وبالجملة فإما أو ليس. ضرورة امتناع ارتفاع النقيضين وفيه منع أنا لا نسم ظاهر، وهو نسلم صدق شيء من هذه القضايا، إنما يلزم ارتفاع النقيضين لو سلبا عن شيء واحد وههنا كما لا سلب لا إيجاب، لأن كلا منهما حكم مشروط بتصور الموضوع فلذا بينه القوم بطريق الترديد على ما ذكرنا.
As for the absolutely unknown, judging that there is no valid judgment is itself a judgment, leading to contradiction, because some absolutely unknown things have been judged. It may be answered that the proposition is conditional, i.e., 'no judgment is valid as long as it is absolutely unknown,' and this does not contradict the absolute [proposition]. But this is refuted by a minimal change, namely that the negation of the condition be restricted to perpetuity, i.e., that which is perpetually absolutely unknown cannot be judged perpetually, or that possibility of conception be considered. It is conceived and said: if judgment on a thing is conditioned by its conception, it would be conditioned by the possibility of its conception necessarily, so it would be impossible to judge what cannot be conceived at all, and the judgment of impossibility is a judgment. In short, the sophism occurs in many cases such as our saying 'daraba is a past tense verb' and 'min is a preposition' and 'not a noun,' and 'that which has no asl is not a universal,' etc. So the answer must be decisive for the matter. Its gist is that the subject in such propositions is multiple. Thus the absolutely unknown, in terms of its essence, is impossible to judge, but in terms of its being conceived, it is judged. And 'daraba' in terms of its essence is a verb, but in terms of this expression is a noun, etc. And it may be said in demonstrating the falsity of our statement 'nothing of the absolutely unknown is valid to judge' that every absolutely unknown is either a thing or not a thing, possible or not possible, in short either B or not B, necessarily due to the impossibility of two alternatives being lifted. But there is a prevention: we do not concede that any of these propositions are true; the elevation of the two opposites would only follow if they are both negated of one thing, whereas here there is no negation nor affirmation because each of them is a judgment conditioned by the conception of the subject. That is why the scholars explained it by way of disjunction as we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage discusses a logical sophism (shubha) regarding judgment on the absolutely unknown (majhul mutlaq). It begins with the observation that if one claims that no judgment is valid on the absolutely unknown, this claim itself is a judgment, leading to a contradiction because some things that are absolutely unknown have indeed been judged. The author then presents an attempted answer: the proposition is conditional, meaning judgment is invalid only as long as the thing remains unknown, but this does not contradict the absolute statement. However, this answer is refuted by a minimal modification: either the condition is restricted to perpetuity, or the possibility of conception is considered. Next, a stronger argument is presented: if judgment is conditioned by conception, then it is conditioned by the possibility of conception, so it would be impossible to judge something that cannot be conceived at all, which is self-contradictory. The correct answer is that the subject in such cases is considered under multiple aspects. For example, the absolutely unknown as a self (dhat) cannot be judged, but as something conceived it can be judged. Similarly, 'daraba' as a word meaning 'he struck' is a verb, but as a phonetic expression it is a noun. The passage then considers a disjunctive proof: every absolutely unknown is either a thing or not a thing, etc., but this is rejected because such judgments are conditioned on conception, and without conception there is no affirmation or negation. Thus the resolution lies in distinguishing the aspects of the subject.

Key Terms
مجهول مطلق
حكم
شرطية
تصور
تناقض
ترديد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The absolutely unknown is completely inaccessible and cannot be discussed or judged in any way.
Correction: The passage shows that while the essence of the absolutely unknown cannot be judged, it can be judged under the aspect of being conceived (e.g., as a mental object).

Checkpoint
How does the author resolve the paradox of judging the absolutely unknown?
A) By rejecting the premise that the absolutely unknown exists.
B) By distinguishing the aspects (dhāt vs. conception) of the subject.
C) By appealing to divine knowledge beyond human reason.
D) By asserting that all judgments are conditional.

Key Takeaway
The absolutely unknown can be judged when considered under the aspect of its conception, but not under its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter something you claim to be completely unknown, do you catch yourself still making judgments about it—like labeling it 'impossible' or 'irrelevant'? What does this lesson teach you about the dishonesty of such hidden judgments?
?
Are you quick to accept an easy answer (like the conditional solution here) when faced with a logical difficulty, or do you press until you reach the decisive resolution? How has avoiding intellectual rigor affected your faith or understanding?
?
The passage shows that a single subject can be considered under multiple aspects. In your own life, where are you treating a person or situation as a single, fixed reality when you should be distinguishing between essence and conception? Who needs you to see them differently?
