The Intellect's Freedom in Conception: No Restriction
Sanusi enumerates the mind's unrestricted conceptual abilities and distinguishes mental from real existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the intellect can conceive contradictory things and non-existence without violating logical consistency, because conception is distinct from external reality.

Core Idea
The mind can conceive what cannot exist — and that is perfectly rational.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid establishes a foundational principle of Islamic rational theology: the intellect has complete freedom in what it can conceive. Before we judge something as true or false, possible or impossible, the mind can picture any combination, even contradictions. Sanusi explains this through the famous statement 'no restriction on mental conceptions,' and shows how this applies to conceiving non-existence, dividing existence into mental and extra-mental, and handling contradictories.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle
Read the opening statement: 'In short, there is no restriction on the conceptions of the intellect.' Recognize that this is a key principle in kalam and logic.
It means the mind can conceive anything, even logical opposites.
2
Explore the examples
The passage lists four operations: considering contradictories, considering non-existence of everything even itself, dividing existence into mental and extra-mental, and dividing into possible and impossible to conceive. Notice how each example illustrates the principle.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: If the mind can conceive contradictories, does that mean reality can contain them? The commentary clarifies that contradictories exist in the mind but cannot both be true in external reality. This distinction is crucial.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد يجمع بين متنافيين. قال: وبالجملة: لا حجر في تصورات العقل، فله أن يعتبر النقيضين ويحكم بينهما بالتناقض، ويعتبر عدم كل شيء حتى نفسه، ويقسم الموجود إلى ثابت في الذهن، وغير ثابت فيه، وإلى ممكن التصور، واللاممكن التصور ويحكم بالتمايز بينها، فيكون كل من اللاثابت، واللاممكن التصور لا هوية له من حيث الذات، مع أن له هوية من حيث الثبوت في العقل كالهوية واللاهوية. فلا حجر زيادة تعميم لتصرفات العقل واعتباراته يعني أن له أن يعتبر النقيضين من المفردات كالموجود واللاموجود، أو من القضايا، مثل موجود وهذا ليس بموجود ويحكم بينهما بالتناقض بمعنى امتناع صدق المفردين على شيء واحد، وامتناع صدق النقيضين في نفس الأمر، فيكون النقيضان موجودين في العقل، وإن كان أحدهما عبارة عما لا وجود له أصلا، وله أن يعتبر عدم كل شيء حتى عدم نفسه، مع أن تصور العقل عدمه يستدعي ثبوته، فيكون هذا جمعاً بين وجوده وعدمه، لكن أحدهما بحسب والآخر بحسب التصور، وله أن يعتبر تقسيم الموجود إلى ثابت في الذهن، وغير ثابت فيه، فيكون اللاثابت في الذهن، قسماً للثابت فيه بحسب الذات.
He can combine between two contradictories. He said: In short, there is no restriction on the conceptions of the intellect. Thus it can consider the two contradictories and judge between them as contradictories, and consider the non-existence of everything, even itself, and divide existence into what is established in the mind and what is not established therein, and into what is possible to conceive and the impossible to conceive, and judge the distinction between them, so that each of the non-established and the impossible to conceive has no identity (huwiyya) in terms of essence, while it has an identity in terms of mental subsistence (thubut), like identity and non-identity. So 'no restriction' is a further generalization for the intellect's operations and considerations, meaning that it can consider the two contradictories from among individuals, like the existent and the non-existent, or from among propositions, like 'something exists' and 'this is not existent,' and judge between them as contradictories in the sense of the impossibility of the truth of both individuals about one thing, and the impossibility of the truth of the two contradictories in actual fact. Thus the two contradictories exist in the mind, even if one of them expresses that which has no existence at all. And it can consider the non-existence of everything, even its own non-existence, while the mind's conception of its non-existence requires its existence, so this is a combination of existence and non-existence, but one is in terms of essence and the other in terms of conception. And it can consider the division of existence into what is established in the mind and what is not established therein, such that what is not established in the mind is a division of what is established therein in terms of essence.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage explains the principle that there is no restriction on the mind's conceptions (la hajara 'ala tasawwurat al-'aql). It begins by stating that the mind can combine contradictories (yajma' bayn mutanafiyayn), meaning it can conceive both a thing and its negation simultaneously. The original text (matn) is quoted: 'In short, there is no restriction on the conceptions of the intellect.' The commentator explains that the mind can consider the two contradictories (al-naqidayn) and judge between them as contradictory, meaning that they cannot both be true of the same thing in reality. The mind can also consider the non-existence of everything, even its own non-existence. It can divide existence into what is established in the mind (thabit fi al-dhihn) and what is not established, and into what is possible to conceive and what is impossible to conceive. The commentator then elaborates that each of the non-established and the impossible to conceive has no identity (huwiyya) in terms of essence (dhat), but it does have an identity in terms of mental subsistence. This is like the concept of identity and non-identity. Then the commentator paraphrases: 'so no restriction' is a generalization for the mind's operations. The mind can consider the two contradictories from among individuals (e.g., existent and non-existent) or from among propositions (e.g., 'this exists' and 'this does not exist') and judge them as contradictory in the sense that they cannot both be true of the same thing or both be false in actual reality. Nevertheless, both contradictories exist in the mind, even if one refers to something with no extra-mental existence. The mind can also consider the non-existence of everything, even its own non-existence, but the act of conceiving non-existence implies the existence of the conceiver, thus combining existence and non-existence under different aspects (bi-hasab al-dhat vs. bi-hasab al-tasawwur). Finally, the mind can divide existence into mental and extra-mental, such that the non-mental is a division of the mental in terms of essence.

Key Terms
لا حجر (no restriction)
تصور العقل (mental conception)
نقيضان (two contradictories)
ثابت في الذهن (established in the mind)
هوية (identity)
ثبوت (subsistence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the mind cannot conceive contradictory things at the same time, because that would violate logic.
Correction: The passage shows the mind can conceive contradictories, but they are distinct in realm: they can coexist as concepts in the mind, though they cannot both be true in external reality.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how can the mind combine existence and non-existence?
A) By conceiving them both simultaneously in reality, thus violating the law of non-contradiction.
B) By considering one in terms of essence (dhat) and the other in terms of conception (tasawwur).
C) By denying that non-existence can be conceived at all.
D) By confining both to extra-mental existence.

Key Takeaway
The intellect can conceive anything, including contradictions and non-existence, but this does not imply they can exist externally; mental consideration is distinct from real existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a belief that seems contradictory to your own, do you dismiss it as impossible without examination, or do you first acknowledge that your mind can conceive of it? How might the principle 'no restriction in concepts' help you listen more carefully to others before judging?
?
If the mind can conceive its own non-existence, what does that reveal about the dependency of your existence on the Creator? Are you willing to sit with the fragility of your being and turn your reflection into gratitude and reliance on Allah?
?
Where in your daily life do you confuse mental conceptions—like fears or worries about the future—with reality? How can the lesson's distinction between mental subsistence and external existence help you distinguish between what is truly real and what is only in your mind, so you can place your trust in Allah rather than anxiety?

The Unlimited Scope of Intellectual Conception
Taftazani explains the unlimited scope of intellectual conceptualization and resolves apparent contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the intellect can conceive seemingly contradictory ideas without logical inconsistency by distinguishing between different aspects (iʿtibārāt).

Core Idea
The intellect faces no restriction; apparent contradictions are resolved by considering different aspects.

Taftazani begins by asserting the absolute freedom of the intellect to conceive anything, even contradictions. This might seem paradoxical, but he shows that such conceptualizations do not imply real-world inconsistency because each consideration is under a distinct aspect. In this lesson, we will explore his key examples and the principle that resolves many sophistries.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the opening claim
Taftazani states that there is no restriction on the intellect's conceptualizations. Identify this claim and then list the examples he gives.
The examples include combining contradictories, considering non-existence of self, and dividing the existent.
2
Analyze the first example: combining contradictories
Notice that the intellect can conceive two contradictories (like existent and non-existent) and judge them as contradictory. This does not mean they both exist externally; they exist only in the mind.
3
Analyze the second example: considering non-existence of self
Consider the seeming paradox: to conceive non-existence, the intellect must exist. Taftazani resolves it by saying existence is with respect to essence and non-existence with respect to conceptualization.
4
Analyze the divisions of the existent
The intellect divides the existent into fixed in the mind and not fixed, and into possible and impossible to conceive. Each of these divisions involves items that have no identity in essence but have identity in the mind.
5
Understand the key principle of differentiation by aspect
All apparent contradictions are resolved by noting that the intellect considers things under different aspects. This principle resolves sophistries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة: لا حجر في تصورات العقل، فله أن يجمع بين النقيضين ويحكم بينهما بالتناقض، ويعتبر عدم كل شيء حتى نفسه، ويقسم الموجود إلى ثابت في الذهن وغير ثابت فيه، وإلى ممكن التصور واللاممكن التصور ويحكم بالتمايز بينها، فيكون كل من اللاثابت واللاممكن التصور لا هوية له من حيث الذات، مع أن له هوية من حيث الثبوت في العقل كالهوية واللاهوية. فلا حجر زيادة تعميم لتصرفات العقل واعتباراته يعني أن له أن يعتبر النقيضين من المفردات كالموجود واللاموجود، أو من القضايا، مثل موجود وهذا ليس بموجود ويحكم بينهما بالتناقض بمعنى امتناع صدق المفردين على شيء واحد، وامتناع صدق النقيضين في نفس الأمر، فيكون النقيضان موجودين في العقل، وإن كان أحدهما عبارة عما لا وجود له أصلا، وله أن يعتبر عدم كل شيء حتى عدم نفسه، مع أن تصور العقل عدمه يستدعي ثبوته، فيكون هذا جمعاً بين وجوده وعدمه، لكن أحدهما بحسب الذات والآخر بحسب التصور، وله أن يعتبر تقسيم الموجود إلى ثابت في الذهن وغير ثابت فيه، فيكون اللاثابت في الذهن قسماً للثابت فيه بحسب الذات وقسماً منه باعتبار كونه متصوراً، وكذا في تقسيمه إلى ممكن التصور واللاممكن التصور، فيكون الثاني قسماً من ممكن التصور، بل من المتصور له أن يحكم بالتمايز بين الثابت في الذهن واللاثابت فيه، وكذا بين ممكن التصور واللاممكن التصور، مع أنه يستدعي أن يكون للممتازين هویتان عند العقل ولا هوية للاثابت في العقل واللاممكن التصور، فيكون كل منهما لا هوية له عند العقل من حيث الذات، وله هوية عنده من حيث التصور، وهذا كما أنه يعتبر الهوية واللاهوية ويحكم بينهما بالتمايز، فتكون اللاهوية قسيماً للهوية بحسب الذات وقسماً منها باعتبار ثبوتها في العقل، ولا تناقض في شيء من ذلك، وهذه أصول يستعان بها على حل كثير من المغالطة.
And in summary: there is no restriction in the conceptualizations of the intellect. So it has the ability to combine two contradictories and judge between them by contradiction, and to consider the non-existence of everything, even itself, and to divide the existent into what is fixed in the mind and what is not fixed in it, and into what is possible to conceive and what is impossible to conceive, and to judge distinction between them. So each of the non-fixed and the impossible-to-conceive has no identity (huwiyya) with respect to essence, while it has identity with respect to being established (thubūt) in the intellect, like identity and non-identity. So 'no restriction' is an additional generalization for the operations of the intellect and its considerations, meaning that it has the ability to consider the two contradictories among individuals, like the existent and the non-existent, or among propositions, such as 'existent' and 'this is not existent', and to judge between them by contradiction, meaning the impossibility of the two individuals being true of one thing, and the impossibility of the two contradictories being true in reality. So the two contradictories exist in the intellect, even if one of them expresses that which has no existence at all. And it has the ability to consider the non-existence of everything, even its own non-existence, while the intellect's conceptualization of its non-existence necessitates its existence. So this is a combination between its existence and non-existence, but one is with respect to essence and the other with respect to conceptualization. And it has the ability to consider the division of the existent into what is fixed in the mind and what is not fixed in it. So the non-fixed in the mind is a part of the fixed in it with respect to essence and a part of it with respect to its being conceived. Similarly in its division into what is possible to conceive and what is impossible to conceive. So the second is a part of what is possible to conceive, rather it is for the conceiver to judge distinction between the fixed in the mind and the non-fixed in it, and similarly between what is possible to conceive and what is impossible to conceive, while it necessitates that the two distinguished things have two identities in the intellect, and there is no identity for the non-fixed in the intellect and the impossible-to-conceive. So each of them has no identity with respect to essence in the intellect, and has identity with respect to conceptualization. And this is like when it considers identity and non-identity and judges distinction between them. So non-identity is a counterpart of identity with respect to essence and a part of it with respect to its being established in the intellect. And there is no contradiction in any of that. And these are principles that are used to resolve many sophistries.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the unrestricted nature of intellectual conceptualization (taṣawwurāt al-ʿaql). The author begins by stating the principle: there is no restriction on what the intellect can conceive. It can combine two contradictories (naqīḍayn) and judge them as contradictory, it can consider the non-existence of everything including itself, and it can divide the existent into categories. The key point is that these operations do not entail real contradiction because each consideration is under a different aspect (iʿtibār). The author then elaborates: when the intellect judges between two contradictories, it is impossible for both to be true of the same thing in reality, but they can both exist in the mind as conceptual objects. Similarly, considering the non-existence of the intellect itself seems paradoxical because conceptualizing non-existence requires existence, but this is resolved by noting that the existence is in one respect (as a conceptualizer) and non-existence in another (as the object of conceptualization). The division of the existent into fixed in the mind (thābit fī al-dhihn) and not fixed, and into possible to conceive and impossible to conceive, also involves distinctions: the non-fixed or impossible-to-conceive have no identity (huwiyya) in essence, but they have identity in the mind as conceived objects. The author gives the example of identity and non-identity: non-identity is a counterpart of identity in essence but is a part of it in terms of mental existence. The passage concludes that these principles help resolve many sophistries (mugālaṭāt).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conceiving contradictories in the mind entails a real contradiction in external existence.
Correction: The mind can hold together two contradictories under different aspects (e.g., essence vs. conceptualization) without any logical inconsistency, as they do not both apply to the same thing in the same respect.

B) The intellect's existence is in one respect (as conceptualizer) and non-existence in another (as object of conceptualization).
C) The intellect must cease to exist to conceive non-existence.

Key Takeaway
The intellect can conceive anything, even contradictions, without logical inconsistency because each consideration pertains to a different aspect (essence vs. conceptualization).

Existence and Non-existence as Predicate and Connective
Sanusi explains two uses and the condition for valid predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that existence and non-existence can function either as predicates or as connectives, and that affirmative predication requires identity in essence but difference in concept.

Core Idea
Affirmative predication unites subject and predicate in essence while keeping their concepts distinct.

This lesson explores a key logical topic: how existence (wujūd) and non-existence (ʿadam) are used in statements. You will learn that they can serve as simple predicates or as relational connectives. Most importantly, you will grasp the subtle condition that makes an affirmative judgment both valid and informative: the subject and predicate must refer to the same thing but mean something different.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the examples given (man exists, phoenix non-existent, man exists as a writer).
The passage uses technical terms like mahmūl and rābiṭa.
2
Identify the two functions of existence/non-existence
The author states that each may occur as a predicate or as a connective. Find where he explains each function and write down the examples.
3
Understand the condition for affirmative predication
Focus on the sentence starting with 'لابد في حمل الإيجاب...'. The condition is that subject and predicate must be identical in identity (huwiyya) but different in concept (mafhūm). Ask yourself why both parts are necessary.
If they were identical in concept, the statement would be a tautology (man is man). If they were different in identity, the statement would be false.
4
Reflect on the conventional nature of judgment
Notice the author says that affirmative and negative judgments are perceptions of whether the relation occurs, and this is a conventional reality (ḥaqīqa ʿurfiyya). Think about what this means for the reliability of such judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السادس: كل من الوجود والعدم قد يقع محمولاً، وقد يقع رابطة، ولا بد في حمل الايجاب من اتحاد الطرفين هوية ليصح، وتغيرهما مفهوماً ليفيد. كما في قولنا: الإنسان موجود والعنقاء معدوم، وقد يقع رابطة بين الموضوع والمحمول، كما في قولنا: الإنسان يوجد كاتباً أو بعدم، أو بين غيرهما كما في وجود زيد في الزمان أو المكان وفي الأعيان أو الأذهان، والحمل قد يكون إيجاباً وهو الحكم بثبوت المحمول للموضوع، وقد يكون سلباً وهو الحكم بانتفائه عنه، وحقيقتهما إدراك أن النسبة واقعة أو ليست بواقعة، وهو حقيقة عرفية فيهما. فلذا قلنا: ولا بد في حمل الإيجاب من اتحاد الموضوع والمحمول بحسب الذات والهوية ليصح الحكم بأن هذا ذاك للقطع بأن هذا لا يصح فيما بين الموجودين المتمايزين بالهوية، ومن تغايرهما مفهوماً ليفيد.
The sixth investigation: Each of existence and non-existence may occur as a predicate, and may occur as a connective. In affirmative predication, it is necessary that the two terms be united in identity to be valid, and that they differ in concept to be informative. As in our saying: 'Man exists' and 'Phoenix is non-existent'. And it may occur as a connective between subject and predicate, as in our saying: 'Man exists as a writer' or 'as non-existent', or between other things, as in the existence of Zayd in time or place, or in external objects or minds. Predication may be affirmative, which is the judgment of the affirmation of the predicate for the subject, or negative, which is the judgment of its negation from it. Their reality is the perception that the relation occurs or does not occur, and this is a conventional reality regarding them. Hence we said: In affirmative predication, it is necessary that the subject and predicate be united in terms of essence and identity for the judgment 'this is that' to be valid, because it is certain that this is not valid between two existents that are distinct in identity, and that they differ in concept to be informative.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is the sixth investigation in a series. It defines the two main uses of existence (wujud) and non-existence ('adam): as predicates (mahmul) in simple propositions like 'Man exists', and as connectives (rabitah) linking subject and predicate in complex propositions like 'Man exists as a writer'. The author then lays down a crucial condition for affirmative predication (haml al-ijab): the subject and predicate must be identical in identity (huwiyya) to make the judgment true (e.g., 'this is that'), but they must differ in concept (mafhum) to be informative (otherwise it would be a tautology like 'water is water'). The passage also explains that affirmative and negative judgments are perceptions of whether the relation occurs or not, and this is a conventional (urfiyya) reality. The final statement reiterates the condition: identity in essence but difference in concept.

Key Terms
وجود (existence)
عدم (non-existence)
محمول (predicate)
رابطة (connective)
حمل (predication)
إيجاب (affirmation)
سلب (negation)
هوية (identity)
مفهوم (concept)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That affirmative predication requires the subject and predicate to be identical in both essence and concept.
Correction: The author states that they must be identical in identity (essence) but different in concept; otherwise the statement would be either false (if distinct in identity) or uninformative (if identical in concept).

Checkpoint
What is required for an affirmative predication to be both valid and informative?
A) Subject and predicate must be identical in both essence and concept.
B) Subject and predicate must be identical in essence but different in concept.
C) Subject and predicate must be different in both.
D) No condition is needed.

Key Takeaway
Existence and non-existence can be predicates or connectives, but for affirmative predication to be valid and informative, subject and predicate must share the same identity but have distinct concepts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson teaches that for a statement like 'I exist' to be valid and informative, the subject 'I' and the predicate 'existent' must share the same essence but be different in concept. Do you live as if your existence and your true identity are united, or do you sometimes act as if they are two separate things? What change would it demand from you to make them truly one?

?
The passage says that if subject and predicate are identical in concept, the statement is not informative. When you say 'Allah is Existent,' is your understanding of His existence merely a copy of your understanding of existence in general, or do you truly grasp that His existence is unique and beyond concept? How can you deepen your awareness of that difference?

Existence and Non-Existence as Predicates and Copulas
Defines the dual function of existence and non-existence in predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how existence and non-existence function in predication and the logical condition that makes affirmative statements both correct and informative.

Core Idea
Meaningful predication demands both unity of subject and predicate and a difference in concept.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid introduces a foundational topic in logic and kalam: the roles of existence and non-existence in statements. Students of theology need to grasp how simple assertions like 'man exists' work, because theological arguments hinge on precise predication. Let's explore the passage step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage attentively
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author distinguishes between 'predicate' and 'copula' functions.
These are technical terms from Aristotelian logic.
2
Identify the two types of predication
The passage defines affirmative and negative predication. Find the definitions and note how they relate to existence and non-existence.
Affirmative: judgment that predicate belongs to subject. Negative: judgment that it does not.
3
Understand the condition for affirmative predication
Focus on the key condition: subject and predicate must be united in identity but different in concept. Why is this necessary? The author explains with examples.
Union in identity avoids falsehood; difference in concept provides new information.
4
Reflect on the examples
Consider 'Man exists' (union in identity, difference in concept) vs. 'Earth is earth' (no difference in concept – uninformative).
The passage explicitly says there is no benefit in tautologies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السادس: كل من الوجود والعدم قد يقع محمولاً، وقد يقع رابطة، ولا بد في حمل الإيجاب من اتحاد الطرفين هوية ليصح، وتغيرهما مفهوماً ليفيد. كما في قولنا: الإنسان موجود والعنقاء معدوم، وقد يقع رابطة بين الموضوع والمحمول، كما في قولنا: الإنسان يوجد كاتباً أو بعدم، أو بين غيرهما كما في وجود زيد في الزمان أو المكان وفي الأعيان أو الأذهان، والحمل قد يكون إيجاباً وهو الحكم بثبوت المحمول للموضوع، وقد يكون سلباً وهو الحكم بانتفائه عنه، وحقيقتهما إدراك أن النسبة واقعة أو ليست بواقعة، وهو حقيقة عرفية فيهما. فلذا قلنا: ولا بد في حمل الإيجاب من اتحاد الموضوع والمحمول بحسب الذات والهوية ليصح الحكم بأن هذا ذاك للقطع بأن هذا لا يصح فيما بين الموجودين المتمايزين بالهوية، ومن تغايرهما بحسب المفهوم، ليفيد فائدة يعتد بها، وهي أن هذين المتغايرين بحسب المفهوم متحدان بحسب الذات والوجود للقطع بعدم الفائدة في مثل الأرض أرض، والسماء سماء.
The sixth topic: Both existence and non-existence may occur as a predicate, and may occur as a copula. In affirmative predication, it is necessary that the two terms be united in identity for it to be correct, and that they differ in concept for it to be informative. As in our saying: 'Man exists' and 'Phoenix does not exist'. And it may occur as a copula between subject and predicate, as in our saying: 'Man exists as writer' or 'as non-writer', or between other than them as in the existence of Zayd in time or place, and in external entities or minds. Predication may be affirmative, which is the judgment that the predicate is established for the subject, or negative, which is the judgment of its absence from it. Their reality is the perception that the relation occurs or does not occur, and it is a conventional reality in them. Hence we said: In affirmative predication, it is necessary that the subject and predicate be united in terms of essence and identity for the judgment that 'this is that' to be correct, because it is certain that this is not correct between two existents that are distinct in identity; and that they differ in terms of concept, so that it conveys a useful benefit, which is that these two which differ in concept are united in terms of essence and existence, because it is certain that there is no benefit in something like 'earth is earth' and 'sky is sky'.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage defines the logical roles of existence and non-existence and sets out the condition for valid affirmative predication. The author first states that both existence and non-existence function either as predicates (e.g., 'man exists') or as copulas linking subject and predicate (e.g., 'man exists as writer'). He then defines predication: affirmative (the judgment that the predicate belongs to the subject) and negative (the judgment that it does not). Their reality is the mind's perception that the relation is either realized or not, and this is a 'conventional reality' (i.e., a construct of the intellect). He then emphasizes a key condition for affirmative predication: the subject and predicate must be united in essence/identity (so that the judgment 'this is that' is correct—otherwise they would be two distinct things and cannot be equated), yet they must differ in concept (so that the judgment conveys new information, e.g., 'earth is earth' is pointless). Thus the condition combines identity of referent with difference of meaning, which is essential for meaningful statements.

Correction: The passage clarifies that both existence and non-existence can function as copulas linking subject and predicate, e.g., 'man exists as writer'.

Union in Identity: Objection and Resolution
Responds to an objection about union condition when terms lack external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the condition of union in identity applies to affirmative propositions whose terms may lack external existence, and how the objector's challenge is resolved by clarifying that union refers to the referent, not necessarily external existence.

Core Idea
Union in identity is about referent, not external existence.

This passage tackles a critical objection to the condition of union in identity for valid affirmative predication. The objector points to propositions like 'The phoenix is non-existent' where terms have no external existence, challenging the union condition. The answer clarifies that union is not about external existence but about the identity of the referent, whether external or mental.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objector's challenge that union in external existence fails for many valid propositions whose terms do not exist externally. Note the examples given.
The objector presents two horns: external union fails, mental union conflates concept and existence.
2
Study the answer's core solution
The answer redefines union: union in essence means the subject-title and predicate-concept apply to the same individual. Distinguish between essence (referent) and concept (meaning).
3
Examine the author's clarifying examples
See how 'The triangle is a shape' works: the thing called triangle is the same as the thing called shape. This shows union in referent.
Contrast with a mistaken interpretation where the subject's essence is taken as the predicate concept, leading to invalid syllogisms.
4
Apply to the original objection
Recognize that the answer shows union remains valid for mental propositions as long as the referent is one. The objector's examples are accommodated without requiring external existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: إن أريد الاتحاد في الوجود الخارجي، فرب موجبة لا وجود لطرفيها في الخارج. كقولنا: العنقاء معدوم، وشريك الباري ممتنع، والوجوب ثبوتي، والإمكان اعتباري والجنس مقوم للنوع، والنوع كلي، والفصل علة للجنس، إلى ذلك فإنها وإن منع إيجاب بعضها فلا كلام في البعض، وإن أريد الأعم ليتناول أمثال هذه القضايا لم يستقم لأنه لا يتصور التغاير في المفهوم مع الاتحاد في الوجود الذهني، إذ لا معنى للموجود في الذهن إلا الحاصل فيه، وهو معنى المفهوم. قلنا: معنى الاتحاد بالذات والهوية والوجود هو أن يكون ما صدق عليه عنوان الموضوع هو بعينه ما يصدق عليه مفهوم المحمول، من غير أن ينفرد كل بوجود بل يكون موجوداً واحداً عينياً، كما في القضايا المعتبرة في العلوم، سيما اذا أخذت بحسب الحقيقة أو الخارج، أو ذهنياً، كما في القضايا الذهنية، على ما قالوا إن معنى قولنا: المثلث شكل هو أن الذي يقال له المثلث هو بعيته الذي يقال له الشكل وهذا هو المراد بقولهم: المراد بالموضوع الذات، وبالمحمول المفهوم. للقطع بأنه لو أريد أن ذات الموضوع نفس مفهوم المحمول، لم يستقم ولم يتكرر الوسط في الشكل الأول، فلم ينتج، كما إذا أخذت القضية طبيعية المحمول أو الموضوع. كقولنا: جزء مفهوم الإنسان ناطق، وكل ناطق ضاحك، وقولنا: بعض النوع إنسان ولا شيء من الإنسان بنوع، مع كذب النتيجة، لأن المعتبر عندهم في الأحكام من الموجبة المعنى الذي ذكرنا، وهذه ليست كذلك.
For it might be said: If what is meant is union in external existence, then there are many affirmative propositions whose two terms have no existence in the external world, like our saying: 'The phoenix is non-existent', 'The partner of God is impossible', 'Necessity is positive', 'Possibility is mental', 'Genus is constitutive of species', 'Species is universal', 'Differentia is a cause of the genus', and so on. Even if some of these are not admitted as affirmatives, there is no dispute about others. But if what is intended is something more general to cover such propositions, that would not be sound, because difference in concept cannot be conceived together with union in mental existence, since the existent in the mind is nothing other than what is present in it, which is the meaning of the concept. We say: The meaning of union in essence, identity, and existence is that the thing of which the subject-title is true is precisely that of which the predicate-concept is true, without each having a separate existence, but rather being one identical existent, as in the propositions considered in the sciences, especially when taken according to reality or the external world, or mentally, as in mental propositions, according to what they said: The meaning of our saying 'The triangle is a shape' is that that which is called 'triangle' is precisely that which is called 'shape'. This is what is meant by their saying: What is intended by subject is the essence, and by predicate is the concept. Because it is certain that if what were intended were that the essence of the subject is itself the concept of the predicate, it would not be sound, and the middle term would not recur in the first figure, and would not produce a conclusion, as when the proposition is taken with a natural predicate or subject, like our saying: 'Part of the concept of man is rational', 'Every rational is laughing', and our saying: 'Some of the species is man', 'No human being is a species', with the conclusion being false. For what is considered by them in judgments concerning affirmative propositions is the meaning we mentioned, and these are not like that.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its detailed answer regarding the condition of union in identity for affirmative predication. The objector raises a problem: If by 'union' we mean union in external existence, then many valid affirmative propositions have terms that do not exist externally (e.g., 'The phoenix is non-existent' or 'The partner of God is impossible'). If we instead mean a more general union (including mental existence), that fails because in mental existence, the existent is identical with its concept, so we cannot have difference in concept together with union (since the concept itself is what exists in the mind). This would undermine the requirement of conceptual difference. The answer clarifies that union is not merely in external existence nor in mental existence in a way that destroys conceptual difference. Rather, union in essence means that the individual thing that satisfies the subject description also satisfies the predicate description, without the subject and predicate each having a separate existence—they refer to the same entity. For example, 'The triangle is a shape': the thing called 'triangle' is exactly the thing called 'shape'. This is what they mean by 'subject refers to essence, predicate to concept'. The author also warns against confusing this with a case where the subject's essence is taken to be the predicate's concept, which would make the middle term in a syllogism fail to repeat and thus yield no valid conclusion. He illustrates with invalid examples like 'Part of the concept of man is rational' (which is not a proper categorical proposition) and 'Some of the species is man' (which is false). The correct understanding is that the subject is the essence (the concrete referent), and the predicate is the concept attributed to it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Union in identity requires both terms to have external existence.
Correction: Union in identity means the subject and predicate refer to the same entity, regardless of whether that entity exists externally or mentally. Mental propositions like 'The phoenix is non-existent' are valid because they satisfy union in referent.

Checkpoint

C) He clarifies that union is in the referent, not necessarily in external existence.
D) He argues that mental existence suffices for union without difference in concept.

Key Takeaway
Union in identity for affirmative predication means the subject-title and predicate-concept refer to the same entity, whether that entity exists externally or mentally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identity in Affirmative Propositions and Refuting an Objection
The author explains the meaning of identity between subject and predicate in affirmative propositions and responds to an objection about non-existent terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of identity (ittihad) between subject and predicate in affirmative propositions, and how the author refutes an objection regarding non-existent terms.

Core Idea
Identity in logic means the same entity, not the same concept — even in mental existence.

This passage addresses a subtle but crucial point in Aristotelian logic: what does it mean for a subject and predicate to be 'one' in an affirmative proposition? An objector raises a problem about propositions with non-existent terms. The author clarifies that identity is about the entity itself, whether external or mental, and explains how this preserves logical validity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's full argument come through before interpreting.
This passage is dense. Pause after each sentence.
2
Identify the objection
The objector presents two possible meanings of identity (external existence or general sense) and argues against each. Find these two points.
Notice the example 'the phoenix is non-existent' has no external existence.
3
Locate the author's answer
The author starts with 'قلنا'. He defines identity as the subject-entity being exactly the predicate-entity. Underline this definition.
He gives the example 'The triangle is a shape' to illustrate.
4
Understand the logical consequence
The author explains that if identity meant conceptual sameness, the middle term wouldn't repeat, causing invalid syllogisms. Then he applies the definition to mental propositions like 'the partner of the Creator is impossible'.
Think about how this definition solves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إن أريد الاتحاد في الوجود الخارجي، فرب موجبة لا وجود لطرفيها في الخارج. كقولنا : العنقاء معدوم، وشريك الباري ممتنع، والوجوب ثبوتي، والإمكان اعتباري، والجنس مقوم للنوع، والنوع كلي، والفصل علة للجنس، إلى ذلك. فإنها وإن منع إيجاب بعضها فلا كلام في البعض، وإن أريد الأعم ليتناول أمثال هذه القضايا لم يستقم لأنه لا يتصور التغاير في المفهوم مع الاتحاد في الوجود الذهني، إذ لا معنى للموجود في الذهن إلا الحاصل فيه، وهو معنى المفهوم. قلنا : معنى الاتحاد بالذات والهوية والوجود هو أن يكون ما صدق عليه عنوان الموضوع هو بعينه ما يصدق عليه مفهوم المحمول، من غير أن ينفرد كل بوجود بل يكون موجوداً واحداً عينياً، كما في القضايا المعتبرة في العلوم، سيما اذا أخذت بحسب الحقيقة أو الخارج، أو ذهنياً، كما في القضايا الذهنية، على ما قالوا إن معنى قولنا : المثلث شكل هو أن الذي يقال له المثلث هو بعينه الذي يقال له الشكل وهذا هو المراد بقولهم : المراد بالموضوع الذات، وبالمحمول المفهوم. للقطع بأنه لو أريد أن ذات الموضوع نفس مفهوم المحمول، لم يستقم ولم يتكرر الوسط في الشكل الأول، فلم ينتج، كما إذا أخذت القضية طبيعية المحمول أو الموضوع . كقولنا : جزء مفهوم الإنسان ناطق، وكل ناطق ضاحك، وقولنا : بعض النوع إنسان ولا شيء من الإنسان بنوع، مع كذب النتيجة، لأن المعتبر عندهم في الأحكام من الموجبة المعنى الذي ذكرنا، وهذه ليست كذلك. وبالجملة، فمعنى الإيجاب في الذهنيات، أن المعقول الأول الذي يصدق عليه في الذهن عنوان الموضوع هو بعينه الذي يصدق عليه مفهوم المحمول من غير تعدد في ذاته، ووجوده العقلي، وإنما التعدد مفهوميهما اللذين كلاهما أو أحدهما من ثواني المعقولات، فمعنى قولنا شريك الباري ممتنع أن ما يصدق عليه في الذهن أنه شريك الباري، يصدق عليه في الذهن أنه ممتنع الوجود في الخارج، وعلى هذا فقس.
For if it is said: If by identity is meant [identity] in external existence, then many affirmative propositions have no external existence for their two terms, such as our saying: 'The phoenix is non-existent', 'The partner of the Creator is impossible', 'Necessity is positive', 'Possibility is subjective', 'Genus is constitutive of species', 'Species is universal', 'Differentia is cause of the genus', and so on. For even if some of them do not admit affirmation, there is no dispute about some. And if what is meant is the more general [sense] to include such propositions, it is not sound because conceptual difference is inconceivable with unity in mental existence, since there is no meaning for 'existing in the mind' except that which occurs in it, and that is the concept. We say: The meaning of identity in essence, identity, and existence is that what the subject-term applies to is exactly what the predicate-term applies to, without each having a separate existence; rather, it is one identical existent, as in the propositions considered in the sciences, especially when taken according to reality or the external, or mentally, as in mental propositions, according to what they said: The meaning of our saying 'The triangle is a shape' is that what is called 'triangle' is exactly what is called 'shape', and this is what is meant by their saying: 'By the subject is meant the essence, and by the predicate the concept.' This is because it is certain that if it were meant that the essence of the subject is the concept of the predicate itself, it would not be sound, the middle term would not be repeated in the first figure, and so it would not produce a conclusion, as when the proposition is taken with natural predicate or subject, such as our saying: 'Part of the concept of man is rational', 'Every rational is risible', and our saying: 'Some of the species are man' and 'No man is a species', with the conclusion being false. That is because what they consider in judgments of the affirmative is the meaning we mentioned, and these [propositions] are not like that. In summary, the meaning of affirmation in mental [propositions] is that the first intelligible which in the mind is subject-term is exactly that to which the predicate-concept applies, without multiplicity in its essence and its mental existence; rather, the multiplicity is in their two concepts, both or one of which are among secondary intelligibles. So the meaning of our saying 'The partner of the Creator is impossible' is that what is in the mind as 'partner of the Creator' is what is in the mind as 'impossible of existence in the external', and analogize to this.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection regarding the nature of identity in affirmative propositions. The objector argues: if identity is taken in external existence, many meaningful affirmative propositions (like 'the phoenix is non-existent') have no external reality for their terms, so identity cannot apply. If it is taken more generally to include mental existence, then conceptual difference with mental unity is impossible because mental existence equals the concept itself. The author answers by clarifying that identity in essence and existence means the subject-entity and predicate-entity are one and the same individual existent, whether externally or mentally. He gives examples: 'The triangle is a shape' – the same thing is called both. He then explains that if the subject's essence were identical to the predicate's concept, deduction would fail (e.g., middle term not repeated, producing false conclusions). He concludes that in mental propositions, the first intelligible serving as subject is exactly the same mental thing as what the predicate-concept applies to, with no multiplicity in the mental object itself; multiplicity is only in the concepts (which are secondary intelligibles). Thus 'the partner of the Creator is impossible' means: the same mental concept 'partner of the Creator' is also mentally 'impossible in external existence'.

مفهوم
ثواني المعقولات

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The author explains identity also applies in mental existence; the entity is one regardless of external existence.

Key Takeaway
In affirmative propositions, identity means the entity subject refers to is the same entity predicate refers to, even if the terms exist only mentally.

Predicating Existence on Essence: Avoiding Tautology
Taftazani refutes the idea that predicating existence or non-existence on essence requires considering them in the subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how predicating existence or non-existence on essence does not require considering them in the subject, thus avoiding tautology or contradiction.

Core Idea
Consider the predicate, not the subject, when predicating existence on essence.

This passage addresses a subtle logical misunderstanding. One might think that predicating existence on essence requires already considering existence in the subject, leading to tautology ('the existing essence exists') or contradiction. Taftazani resolves this by distinguishing the consideration of the predicate from the subject, using the analogy of 'black' predicated on body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the author's main claim and the analogy to 'black'.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the misunderstanding being refuted
Find the part where the author describes the mistaken view: that because there is no middle between existence and non-existence, there is also no middle between considering them, leading to tautology or contradiction.
The key phrase: 'قد يتوهم أنه كما لا واسطة بين الوجود والعدم'
3
Understand the resolution
The author explains that the real opposition is between existence and non-existence themselves, not between considering them. One can consider neither, which is required for a meaningful predication. The predicate provides the attribute, not the subject.
The analogy with 'black' (أسود) and body (جسم) is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ولا يلزم في حملهما على الماهية اعتبار الوجود أو العدم فيهما ليلغيا أو يتناقض. كما أن في حمل الأسود على الجسم، لا يعتبر فيه السواد وعدمه، وإنما يجيء ذلك من قبل المحمول، وكذا الثبوت الذهني وإن كان لازماً. قد يتوهم أنه كما لا واسطة بين الوجود والعدم، لا واسطة بين اعتبارهما، فالماهية المحمول عليها الوجود إما مع اعتبار الوجود، فيكون الحمل لغواً بمنزلة أن يقال : الماهية الموجودة موجودة، وإما مع اعتبار العدم، فيتناقض. تحقيق ذلك أن التقابل الحقيقي إنما هو بين نفس الوجود والعدم لا بين اعتباريهما لأنه يجوز أن يعتبر هذا أو ذلك أو لا أحدهما. وعدم الاعتبار لواحد بخصوصه هو المتعين عند حمل أحدهما ليفيد. فإنه كالوجود الخارجي في أنه لا يعتبر في المحكوم عليه عند الحكم بذلك الثبوت عليه فيلغى اعتباره فيه. وكما لا يعتبر في المحكوم عليه الثبوت الذهني عند الحكم بذلك الثبوت عليه، فلا يعتبر فيه عدم الثبوت ذهناً لأنه يلزم في الأول اللغو، وفي الثاني التناقض.
He said: It does not follow, in predicating them [existence and non-existence] of essence, that existence or non-existence be considered in it [the essence], such that they become futile or contradictory. Just as in predicating 'black' of body, one does not consider blackness or its absence in it [the body]; rather, that comes from the predicate. Likewise for mental existence, even if it is necessary. It may be mistakenly thought that just as there is no intermediary between existence and non-existence, so there is no intermediary between considering them. So the essence to which existence is predicated is either with consideration of existence, making the predication futile like saying 'the existing essence exists', or with consideration of non-existence, making it contradictory. The resolution of that is that the real opposition is between existence and non-existence themselves, not between their considerations, because it is permissible to consider this or that or neither. The non-consideration of one specifically is what is necessary when predicating one of them to be useful. For it is like external existence in that it is not considered in the subject when judging that existence of it, so its consideration in it is negated. And just as mental existence is not considered in the subject when judging that mental existence of it, so non-existence in the mind is not considered in it, because in the first case there would be futility, and in the second, contradiction.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage refutes a potential misunderstanding about predicating existence or non-existence on essence. The misunderstanding: just as there is no middle between existence and non-existence, there is no middle between considering them; thus when predicating existence, if one considers existence in the subject, the predication is tautological ('the existing essence exists'); if one considers non-existence, it is contradictory. The author resolves this by distinguishing between the concepts themselves and the consideration of them in the subject. The real opposition is between existence and non-existence, not between considering them. It is permissible not to consider either in the subject; indeed, non-consideration of one is required for the predication to be informative. The author analogizes to predicating 'black' of body: we do not consider blackness in the body when saying 'the body is black'; the blackness comes from the predicate. Similarly, mental existence is not considered in the subject when judging that mental existence belongs to it. If we did consider it, the judgment would be futile; if we considered non-existence, it would be contradictory. Therefore, in predicating existence of essence, we do not consider existence in the essence; we only consider it as the predicate, making the judgment meaningful.

Key Terms
حمل
وجود
عدم
ماهية
اعتبار
لغو
تناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because there is no intermediary between existence and non-existence, there is also no intermediary between considering them, so predicating existence must involve considering existence (tautology) or non-existence (contradiction) in the subject.
Correction: The real opposition is between existence and non-existence themselves, not between their considerations. It is possible to consider neither, and this non-consideration is required for a meaningful predication.

Checkpoint
Why does predicating existence on essence not lead to tautology or contradiction?
A) Because existence is not a real predicate.
B) Because the existence considered comes from the predicate, not from the subject.
C) Because essence and existence are identical.
D) Because the subject already contains existence.

Key Takeaway
When predicating existence of essence, we do not consider existence in the subject; that makes the statement trivial or contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Have you ever reduced a deep truth of faith to a mere concept, treating its existence as something you already possess rather than a reality that confronts and transforms you? How can you break free from that assumption?
?
This passage warns against considering existence in the subject. In your own life, are there areas where you assume God's presence or attributes without truly letting them be the predicate that defines you? What would it look like to let divine attributes be genuinely predicated of your existence?

Affirmation in Mental Concepts
Defines affirmation in mental concepts with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that affirmation in mental concepts means the subject and predicate refer to the same mental entity, differing only in conceptual aspect.

Core Idea
Mental affirmation unifies subject and predicate under different concepts.

This passage from al-Maqasid explains a subtle logical point about judgments concerning mental concepts. The author distinguishes between the real entity (the first intelligible) and the conceptual labels we apply to it (secondary intelligibles). Using the example 'partner of God is impossible', he shows that in the mind, the subject and predicate are the same thing considered under different descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, noting the technical terms. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall meaning.
The phrase 'وبالجملة' signals a summary conclusion.
2
Identify the key concepts
Locate the terms 'المعقول الأول' (first intelligible) and 'ثواني المعقولات' (secondary intelligibles). Understand that the first intelligible is the mental entity itself, while secondary intelligibles are the concepts we attach to it.
3
Analyze the example
Consider how 'sharīk al-bārī' (partner of God) and 'mumtaniʿ' (impossible) apply to the same mental concept. The judgment is about a mental construct, not an external reality.
This example shows that theological statements about impossibility are often about mental concepts, not real entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة، فمعنى الإيجاب في الذهنيات، أن المعقول الأول الذي يصدق عليه في الذهن عنوان الموضوع هو بعينه الذي يصدق عليه مفهوم المحمول من غير تعدد في ذاته، ووجوده العقلي، وإنما التعدد مفهوميهما اللذين كلاهما أو أحدهما من ثواني المعقولات، فمعنى قولنا شريك الباري ممتنع أن ما يصدق عليه في الذهن أنه شريك الباري، يصدق عليه في الذهن أنه ممتنع الوجود في الخارج، وعلى هذا فقس.
In summary, the meaning of affirmation in mental concepts is that the first intelligible which in the mind is predicated of as the subject is exactly that which is predicated of as the concept of the predicate, without multiplicity in its essence and its mental existence. Multiplicity is only in their concepts, both or one of which are among secondary intelligibles. Thus the meaning of our saying 'partner of God is impossible' is that what in the mind is predicated of as partner of God is in the mind predicated of as impossible of existence in the external. And on this analogize.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage defines what is meant by affirmation (إيجاب) in the context of mental considerations (الذهنيات). The author first states a general rule: when we make an affirmative mental judgment, the first intelligible (المعقول الأول) that serves as the subject in the mind is exactly the same thing that serves as the predicate, with no real multiplicity in its essence or mental existence. The only multiplicity is in the concepts (مفهوميهما) which are secondary intelligibles (ثواني المعقولات). That is, the subject and predicate refer to the same mental entity but under different aspects of consideration. Then he gives an example: the statement 'the partner of God is impossible'. In the mind, what is designated as 'partner of God' is the very same thing that is designated as 'impossible of existence in the external'. The judgment is about a mental concept, not about a real external thing. The phrase 'وعلى هذا فقس' instructs us to apply the same reasoning to similar cases.

Key Terms
إيجاب
ذهنيات
المعقول الأول
المحمول
ثواني المعقولات
شريك الباري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that affirmation in mental judgments implies a real connection between distinct external entities.
Correction: In fact, it only concerns mental unity: the subject and predicate are the same mental entity considered under different concepts.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'إيجاب' in the context of mental concepts according to this passage?
A) A real relation between subject and predicate in the external world.
B) The subject and predicate are mentally the same thing, differing only in concept.
C) The predicate is not found in the subject at all.
D) It is a type of negative judgment.

Key Takeaway
Affirmation in mental concepts is a matter of conceptual identity: the subject and predicate refer to the same mental entity under different descriptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you mistake a mental concept for an external reality? Can you recall a belief you held that was only true in your mind's framing, not in actual existence? What would it take to distinguish them in your daily judgments?
?
This lesson shows that subject and predicate in mental affirmation are the same entity under different aspects. When you say 'I am a sinner,' do you realize that 'I' and 'sinner' are the same self under different descriptions? How does this affect your self-perception and your need for repentance?
?
If every mental affirmation is merely a conceptual identity, what does that mean for your most deeply held convictions? Are you willing to examine whether they correspond to external reality or are only mental constructs? Commit to testing one belief today.

Refuting the Redundancy/Contradiction Objection
Refutes the objection that predication of existence/absence to an essence is redundant or contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why predicating existence or non-existence to an essence is neither redundant nor contradictory, and how the qualification comes from the predicate.

Core Idea
The predicate gives the attribute; the subject does not pre-contain it.

In this passage, the author addresses a subtle logical objection: if we say 'the essence is existent,' we seem to either already assume existence (making it redundant) or assume non-existence (making it contradictory). By clarifying the role of the predicate, the author shows that the attribute is conferred by the predicate itself, not pre-assumed in the subject. This distinction is crucial for understanding how existence and non-existence can be meaningfully predicated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: a principle, an objection, and a refutation.
Pay attention to the terms ماهية, وجود, عدم.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that predicating existence or non-existence to an essence must be either redundant or contradictory. Understand why someone might think that.
The objection relies on the idea that there is no middle between considering existence and non-existence.
3
Understand the refutation
The author retorts that the predicate itself imparts the attribute; the subject is considered without the attribute. See the example of 'the body is black'.
The qualification comes from the predicate, not from the subject's prior state.
4
Extension to mental existence
The passage also discusses mental existence. Note that while judgment requires conception, this does not mean we consider that conception in the subject.
This clarifies a further subtlety.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ولا يلزم في حملهما على الماهية، اعتبار الوجود أو العدم فيها ليلغيا أو يتناقض. كما أن في حمل الأسود على الجسم، لا يعتبر فيه السواد وعدمه، وإنما يجيء ذلك من قبل المحمول، وكذا الثبوت الذهني وإن كان لازماً. قد يتوهم أنه كما لا واسطة بين الوجود والعدم، لا واسطة بين اعتبارهما، فالماهية المحمول عليها الوجود إما مع اعتبار الوجود، فيكون الحمل لغواً بمنزلة أن يقال : الماهية الموجودة موجودة، وإما مع اعتبار العدم فتناقضاً بمنزلة أن يقال : الماهية المعدومة موجودة، وكذا في حمل العدم بل كل وصف كقولنا : الجسم أسود فإن الموضوع إما مع اعتبار المحمول فلغو. ومع اعتبار عدمه فتناقض فأزال ذلك الوهم، بأن الموضوع وإن كان لا يخرج عن المحمول، أو نقيضه وجوداً كان أو عدماً أو غيرهما، لكن لا يلزم أن يعتبر فيه أحدهما، وإنما يجيء تقيده من قبل الحمل. فإن حمل عليه الوجود كان موجوداً، أو العدم فمعدوماً، أو السواد فأسود، أو البياض فأبيض، من غير أن يعتبر معه شيء من ذلك، وكذا الثبوت الذهني، وإن كان لازماً، من جهة أن الحكم على الشيء يستدعي تصوره، وهو ثبوت ذهني لكن لا يلزم اعتباره في الموضوع، لأن الحكم إنما هو على الذات من غير اعتبار الأوصاف لازمة كانت أو مفارقة، فليس معنى قولنا الماهية موجودة أن الماهية الثابتة في الذهن موجودة حتى لو كان المحمول هو الثبوت الذهني أو نفيه، لم يكن لغواً أو تناقضاً، إلا بالنسبة إلى من يعلم أن المحكوم عليه متصور البتة، وأن التصور ثبوت ذهني.
He said: It is not necessary, in predicating them (existence and non-existence) of the essence, to consider existence or non-existence in it, lest [the predication] be redundant or contradictory. Just as in predicating 'black' of the body, blackness and its absence are not considered in it; rather, that comes from the predicate. And likewise mental existence, even if it is necessary. One may mistakenly think that just as there is no middle between existence and non-existence, there is no middle between considering them. So the essence to which existence is predicated is either with the consideration of existence—then the predication is redundant, like saying 'the existent essence is existent'—or with the consideration of non-existence—then it is contradictory, like saying 'the non-existent essence is existent'. The same holds for predication of non-existence; indeed every attribute, like our saying 'the body is black', for the subject either with the consideration of the predicate it is redundant, or with the consideration of its absence it is contradictory. So he removed that misunderstanding by [saying] that the subject, even though it is not free from the predicate or its opposite, whether existence or non-existence or other, it is not necessary that one of them be considered in it; rather, its qualification comes from the predicate. For if existence is predicated of it, it becomes existent; or non-existence, then non-existent; or blackness, then black; or whiteness, then white; without considering anything of that with it. And likewise mental existence, even if it is necessary, in that the judgment about something requires its conception, which is mental existence, but it is not necessary to consider it in the subject, because the judgment is only about the essence without considering attributes, whether necessary or accidental. So the meaning of our saying 'the essence is existent' is not that the essence that is established in the mind is existent, such that if the predicate were mental existence or its negation, it would be redundant or contradictory, except relative to one who knows that the subject of judgment is absolutely conceived, and that conception is mental existence.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage refutes a potential objection concerning the predication of existence (وجود) and non-existence (عدم) to an essence (ماهية). The objection is that when we say 'the essence is existent' or 'the essence is non-existent', we must either consider the essence already as existent (making the statement redundant) or as non-existent (making it contradictory). The author first states the principle: in such predication, we do not need to consider existence or non-existence in the subject; rather, the attribute comes from the predicate itself, just as when we say 'the body is black', we do not consider blackness in the body beforehand. Next, he presents the objection: since there is no middle between existence and non-existence, there is no middle between considering them, so the predication must be either redundant or contradictory. He then removes the misunderstanding by clarifying that the subject, though it inevitably has either the predicate or its opposite in reality, does not require that one of them be considered in the subject at the moment of predication. The qualification is imparted by the predicate. For example, when we predicate existence, the essence becomes existent; when we predicate non-existence, it becomes non-existent. No prior consideration is needed. He extends the same reasoning to all attributes. Then he addresses mental existence (ثبوت ذهني): although every judgment requires a mental conception, this does not mean we must consider that mental existence in the subject. The judgment is about the essence itself, stripped of attributes. The statement 'the essence is existent' does not mean that the essence already established in the mind is existent—this would indeed cause redundancy or contradiction if the predicate were mental existence or its negation—unless one knows that the subject is mentally conceived, and that conception is a mental existence. So the objection is only valid for those who are aware of the mental conception.

Key Terms
حمل
وجود
عدم
ماهية
لغو
تناقض
ثبوت ذهني
اعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that predicating existence to an essence requires the essence to already be considered existent (making the predication redundant) or non-existent (making it contradictory).
Correction: The predicate itself confers the attribute; the subject is considered without that attribute, so the predication is neither redundant nor contradictory.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the predication of existence to an essence not redundant or contradictory?
A) Because the essence is considered without any attribute, and the predicate gives it the attribute.
B) Because existence and non-existence are not real attributes.
C) Because the essence already contains existence.
D) Because the subject is always taken with the predicate.

Key Takeaway
Predicating existence or non-existence to an essence does not require the essence to already possess the attribute; the predicate confers the attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe yourself as 'a believer' or 'a sinner', do you assume that attribute is already fixed in your essence, or do you recognize that it is a predicate conferred by your actions and Allah's decree? How does this shift your accountability for who you become?
?
The objection mistakenly treats existence as something the subject must already possess before predication. In your daily life, do you ever act as if your very existence is self-sustained, forgetting that it is granted by Allah at every moment? How can you cultivate a constant awareness that you are only 'existent' because He predicates existence upon you?
?
This lesson argues that attributes are not pre-contained in the subject but come from the predicate. What attribute do you most struggle to accept as a gift from Allah rather than something you inherently deserve? Will you commit to thanking Him for that attribute today?

The Subject in Predication: Avoiding Tautology and Contradiction
Author clarifies that the subject in predication does not require the predicate or its negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why predicating existence of an essence is not tautological or contradictory, because the subject is considered without any attributes, even mental existence.

Core Idea
The subject in predication is bare of all attributes, even mental existence.

This passage addresses a subtle logical concern: when we say "the essence is existent," does the subject already contain existence? The author explains that the subject is taken in itself, without any predicates, so the predication adds new information. This insight prevents tautology and contradiction in theological and philosophical statements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how the author sets up the problem of tautology and contradiction.
The passage begins mid-argument with 'فيكون تناقضاً'.
2
Identify the problem
The problem is that if the subject already includes the predicate or its negation, statements become either tautological (laghw) or contradictory (tanāquḍ). For example, saying 'the nonexistent essence is existent' is contradictory.
Consider the examples: 'the body is black' — if 'body' already implies blackness or non-blackness.
3
Understand the solution
The author explains that the subject is considered without any attributes, even necessary ones like mental existence. The predicate's content is not part of the subject; it comes from the predication itself.
Mental existence (thubūt dhihnī) is necessary for judgment but not part of the subject's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيكون تناقضاً بمنزلة أن يقال : الماهية المعدومة موجودة، وكذا في حمل العدم بل كل وصف كقولنا : الجسم أسود فإن الموضوع إما مع اعتبار المحمول فلغو. ومع اعتبار عدمه فتناقض فأزال ذلك الوهم، بأن الموضوع وإن كان لا يخرج عن المحمول، أو نقيضه وجوداً كان أو عدماً أو غيرهما، لكن لا يلزم أن يعتبر فيه أحدهما، وإنما يجيء تقيده من قبل الحمل. فإن حمل عليه الوجود كان موجوداً، أو العدم فمعدوماً، أو السواد فأسود، أو البياض فأبيض، من غير أن يعتبر معه شيء من ذلك، وكذا الثبوت الذهني، وإن كان لازماً، من جهة أن الحكم على الشيء يستدعي تصوره، وهو ثبوت ذهني لكن لا يلزم اعتباره في الموضوع، لأن الحكم إنما هو على الذات من غير اعتبار الأوصاف لازمة كانت أو مفارقة، فليس معنى قولنا الماهية موجودة أن الماهية الثابتة في الذهن موجودة حتى لو كان المحمول هو الثبوت الذهني أو نفيه، لم يكن لغواً أو تناقضاً، إلا بالنسبة إلى من يعلم أن المحكوم عليه متصور البتة، وأن التصور ثبوت ذهني.
It would be a contradiction, like saying: the nonexistent essence is existent, and likewise in the predication of non-existence, but rather every attribute like our saying: the body is black, for the subject if considered with the predicate is tautological, and if considered with its negation is contradictory. So he removed this illusion by [explaining] that the subject, although it does not go beyond the predicate or its opposite, whether existence or non-existence or other than them, does not necessitate that one of them be considered in it; rather its determination comes from the predication. For if existence is predicated of it, it becomes existent; or non-existence, then nonexistent; or blackness, then black; or whiteness, then white, without anything of that being considered with it. Likewise mental existence, although it is necessary from the aspect that the judgment on a thing requires its conception, and that is mental existence, yet its consideration in the subject is not necessary, because the judgment is only on the essence without consideration of attributes, whether necessary or accidental. So the meaning of our saying 'the essence is existent' is not that the essence established in the mind is existent, such that if the predicate were mental existence or its negation, it would be tautological or contradictory, except for one who knows that the subject of judgment is certainly conceived, and that conception is mental existence.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage resolves a potential logical problem in predication. The author addresses the worry that when we predicate existence of an essence (or any attribute), the subject might already contain the predicate (making it tautological) or its negation (making it contradictory). The key insight is that the subject, in itself, is considered without any attributes—even necessary ones like mental existence. The determination of the subject's state comes from the predication itself. For example, when we say 'the body is black,' the subject 'body' is not considered as black or not-black; the predication adds the attribute. Similarly, mental existence (thubūt dhihnī) necessarily accompanies our conception of the subject, but it is not part of the subject as considered in the judgment. Therefore, saying 'the essence is existent' is not tautological, because the essence is not taken as already existent; it only becomes existent through the predication. The exception is if one explicitly includes mental existence in the subject, but the judgment normally considers the essence in itself. This clarification guards against the mistake of thinking that existence is a redundant predicate.

Key Terms
māhiyya (essence)
ḥaml (predication)
wujūd (existence)
ʿadam (non-existence)
laghw (tautology)
tanāquḍ (contradiction)
dhāt (essence/self)
thubūt dhihnī (mental existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that predicating existence of an essence is tautological because the essence already has mental existence.
Correction: The essence is considered without any attributes, even mental existence. The predication adds the attribute; it is not already contained in the subject.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the statement 'the essence is existent' not tautological?
A) Because the essence is never in the mind
B) Because the subject is considered without any attributes, including mental existence
C) Because existence is not a real predicate
D) Because the essence is always nonexistent

Key Takeaway
Predicating existence of an essence is not redundant because the subject is considered without any attributes, even mental existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe yourself as a 'sinner' or a 'believer,' are you describing a fixed reality or are you allowing the judgment itself to determine your state? How might this change your accountability before Allah?
?
The subject is bare even of mental existence. Do you often confuse your mental image of yourself (what you think you are) with your actual essence before Allah? What would it mean to strip away that image and let the judgment of truth define you?
?
If every predication about yourself can be either tautological or contradictory if you assume the attribute is already there, then what are you implicitly assuming about your own soul? Will you dare to let the predicate of 'servant of Allah' be added to a truly bare subject—without pretense or self-deception?
