5 min
Truth is Not Correspondence to External Objects or Mental Representations
Al-Taftazani defines truth and rejects false criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the truth of a judgment cannot be defined as correspondence to external objects or to mental representations, and you will grasp the correct criterion: correspondence to the state of affairs itself.

Core Idea
Truth is not what is in the mind or in the world, but what is in the state of affairs itself.

Al-Taftazani begins by clarifying what truth is not. He argues that truth cannot be mere correspondence to external objects (aʿyān) because many judgments involve mental concepts that have no external existence. Nor can it be correspondence to mental representations (adhhan), since false beliefs can also be in the mind. He then establishes the correct standard: correspondence to 'what is in the state of affairs itself' (mā fī nafs al-amr), independent of any perceiver or informant. This fundamental point is the bedrock of all objective knowledge in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting how al-Taftazani systematically rejects two incorrect criteria before affirming the correct one.
Focus on the examples he gives, like 'the world is eternal' in the minds of philosophers.
2
Identify the two rejected criteria
Find where he rejects correspondence to external objects (aʿyān) and correspondence to mental representations (adhhan). Why does he reject each?
The first fails because judgments can be about mental concepts; the second would make false beliefs true.
3
Understand the positive criterion
Grasp the definition of 'what is in the state of affairs itself' (mā fī nafs al-amr) as the matter considered in itself, disregarding perception and report.
This is the objective standard that avoids both extremes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحاكم وتفسيره بما في العقل الفعال بعيداً جداً، اذا قصد بيان المفهوم، لأنه قد يقع التصديق ممن لا يعرفه بل ينكره وأما الاعتراض بأنه لا يشمل علمه، ولا العلم السابق عليه، ولا العلم بالجزئيات فيمكن دفعه : . يعني . أن الحكم قد يكون صحيحاً أي حقاً وصدقاً وقد يكون فاسداً أي باطلاً وكذباً وإن كان غالب استعمال الصدق والكذب في الأقوال خاصة، وليست صحة الحكم بمطابقته لما في الأعيان، إذ قد لا يتحقق طرفا الحكم في الخارج، كما في الحكم بالأمور الذهنية، على الأمور الذهنية أو الخارجية . كقولنا : الإمكان اعتباري ومقابل للامتناع واجتماع النقيضين ممتنع . " كقولنا : الإنسان ممكن أو أعمى ولا يكفي المطابقة لما في الأذهان لأنه قد يرتسم فيها الأحكام الغير المطابقة للواقع ، فلزم أن يكون قولنا : العالم قديم حقاً وصدقاً، لمطابقته لما في أذهان الفلاسفة، وهو باطل قطعاً، بل المعتبر في صحة الحكم مطابقته لما في نفس الأمر، وهو المراد بـالـواقـع والخارج، أي خارج ذات المدرك والمخبر ومعناه ما يفهم من قولنا هذا الأمر كذا في نفسه أو ليس كذا أي في حد ذاته وبالنظر إليه مع قطع النظر عن إدراك المدرك ، وإخبار المخبر على أن المراد بالأمر الشأن والشيء، وبالنفس الذات . " فإن قيل : كيف يتصور هذا فيما لا ذات له، ولا شيئية في الأعيان كالمعدومات سيما الممتنعات . فالجواب إجمالاً : أنا نعلم قطعاً أن قولنا : اجتماع الضدين مستحيل مطابق لما في نفس الأمر وقولنا : إنه ممكن غير مطابق، وإن لم يعلم كيفية تلك المطابقة بكنهها ، ولم يتمكن من تلخيص العبارة فيها. وتفصيلاً : أن المطابقة إضافة يكفيها تحقق المضافين بحسب العقل، ولا خفاء في أن العقل عند ملاحظة المعنيين والمقايسة بينهما سواء كانا من الموجودات أو المعلومات، تجد بينهما بحسب كل زمان نسبة إيجابية أو سلبية تقتضيها الضرورة أو البرهان فتلك النسبة من حيث إنها نتيجة الضرورة أو البرهان بالنظر إلى نفس ذلك المعقول من غير خصوصية المدرك والمخبر، هي المراد بالواقع وما في نفس الأمر، وبالخارج أيضاً عند من يجعله أعم مما في الأعيان على ما بينا، فصحة هذه النسبة تكون بمعنى أنها الواقع وما في نفس الأمر، وصحة النسبة المعقولة، أو الملفوظة من زيد أو عمرو أو غيرهما بين ذينك المعنين، يكون بمعنى أنها مطابقة لتلك النسبة الواقعة، أي على وفقها في الإيجاب والسلب ولما لم تتصور للنسبة المسماة بالواقع، ومما في نفس الأمر ، سيما فيما بين المعدومات حصول إلا بحسب التعقل، وكان عندهم أن جميع صور الكائنات وأحكام الموجودات والمعدومات مرتسمة في جوهر مجرد أزلي يسمى بالعقل الفعال فسر بعضهم ما في نفس الأمر بما في العقل الفعال ، ويستدل على وجوده بأن الأحكام مع اشتراكها في الثبوت الذهني، منها ما هو مطابق لما في نفس الأمر كالحكم بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين، ومنها ما هو غير مطابق كالحكم بنقيض ذلك. فللأول متعلق خارج عن الذهن يطابقه ما في الذهن ولأن من الأحكام ما هو أزلي ، لا يلحقه تغير أصلاً ولا خروج من قوة إلى فعل، ولا يتعلق بوضع أو زمان أو مكان، مع أن المطابقة لما في نفس الأمر في الكل معنى واحد، لزم أن يكون ذلك المتعلق الخارجي مرتسماً في مجرد أزلي مشتمل على الكل بالفعل،
The judge and its interpretation by what is in the Active Intellect is very far if one intends to explain the concept, because assent may occur from one who does not know it but rather denies it. As for the objection that it does not include His knowledge, nor the knowledge prior to it, nor knowledge of particulars, it can be repelled: meaning that the judgment may be correct, i.e., true and veracious, and may be corrupt, i.e., false and mendacious, although the usual application of truth and falsehood is specifically to statements. The correctness of a judgment is not its correspondence to what is in external objects (aʿyān), since the two terms of the judgment may not be realized in the external, as in a judgment about mental matters on mental or external matters, like our saying: 'Possibility is conceptual and opposite to impossibility, and the conjunction of two opposites is impossible.' Like our saying: 'Human being is possible' or 'blind.' Nor is correspondence to what is in minds sufficient, because judgments not corresponding to reality may be depicted in them. Thus it would follow that our saying 'The world is eternal' is true and veracious because of its correspondence to what is in the minds of the philosophers, but it is absolutely false. Rather, what is considered in the correctness of a judgment is its correspondence to what is in the state of affairs itself (mā fī nafs al-amr), which is meant by 'reality' (al-wāqiʿ) and 'external' (al-khārij), i.e., external to the essence of the perceiver and the informant. Its meaning is what is understood from our saying 'This matter is such in itself or not such,' i.e., in its very essence and with consideration of it, disregarding the perception of the perceiver and the report of the informant, given that by 'matter' is meant affair and thing, and by 'self' is meant essence. If it is said: 'How can this be conceived for what has no essence and no thingness in external objects, like non-existents (maʿdūmāt), especially impossibilities (mumtaniʿāt)?' The answer in summary: we know with certainty that our saying 'The conjunction of two opposites is impossible' corresponds to what is in the state of affairs itself, and our saying 'It is possible' does not correspond, even if we do not know the how of that correspondence in its reality and are unable to summarize the expression for it. In detail: correspondence is a relation for which the realization of the two relata according to the intellect is sufficient. It is no secret that the intellect, when observing the two meanings and comparing them, whether they are among existents or among objects of knowledge, finds between them in every time a positive or negative relation necessitated by necessity or demonstration. That relation, insofar as it is a result of necessity or demonstration with regard to the very intelligible, without particularity of the perceiver and informant, is what is meant by 'reality' and 'what is in the state of affairs itself,' and also by 'external' according to those who make it more general than what is in external objects as we have explained. The correctness of this relation is in the sense that it is reality and what is in the state of affairs itself. The correctness of the relation that is intellected or uttered by Zayd or ʿAmr or others between those two meanings is in the sense that it corresponds to that occurring relation, i.e., in accordance with it in affirmation and negation. Since the relation called 'reality' and 'what is in the state of affairs itself,' especially among non-existents, cannot be conceived to occur except with respect to intellection, and according to them all forms of beings and judgments of existents and non-existents are depicted in an eternal immaterial substance called the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl), some explained 'what is in the state of affairs itself' by what is in the Active Intellect. They adduce evidence for its existence: judgments, despite their commonality in mental subsistence, include some that correspond to what is in the state of affairs itself, like the judgment that one is half of two, and some that are non-corresponding, like the judgment to the contrary. For the first, there is an external object that the mental object corresponds to. And because among judgments there is what is eternal, not subject to any change, nor emergence from potency to act, nor dependent on position, time, or place, while correspondence to what is in the state of affairs itself is one meaning in all, it follows that that external object is depicted in an eternal immaterial substance containing all actually.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the criterion for truth in judgments (ḥukm). The author, al-Taftazani, begins by critiquing the interpretation of the 'judge' (al-ḥākim) as the Active Intellect, finding it far-fetched. He then clarifies that a judgment can be correct (ṣaḥīḥ) i.e., true (ḥaqq) and veracious (ṣidq), or corrupt (fāsid) i.e., false (bāṭil) and mendacious (kidhb). Truth and falsehood are commonly used for statements, but the truth of a judgment is not correspondence to external objects (aʿyān) because the terms may not exist externally, as in judgments about mental concepts. Nor is it correspondence to mental representations (adhhān), because false judgments may be in the mind; e.g., the philosophers' belief that the world is eternal would be true if truth were correspondence to their minds, but it is false. The correct standard is correspondence to 'what is in the state of affairs itself' (mā fī nafs al-amr), i.e., the matter as it is in itself, independent of perception or report. An objection is then raised: how can this apply to non-existents and impossibilities that have no essence or thingness? The answer is twofold. Summarily, we know with certainty that judgments about impossibilities (e.g., 'the conjunction of two opposites is impossible') correspond to reality, even if we cannot fully explain the mechanism. In detail, correspondence is a relation (iḍāfa) that only requires the two relata to be realized in the intellect. The intellect finds necessary or demonstrative relations between any two concepts, whether existent or not. This relation, considered in itself without regard to the perceiver, is the reality (wāqiʿ). The truth of a proposition is that it corresponds to this relation. Since such relations among non-existents only occur intellectually, some philosophers posited an eternal immaterial substance called the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl) in which all forms and judgments are depicted. They defined 'what is in the state of affairs itself' as what is in the Active Intellect. The proof for its existence: judgments include some that correspond to reality and some that do not; the first requires an external object. Moreover, some judgments are eternal and unchanging, so their object must be in an eternal substance containing all things actually. This argument ties the epistemological criterion of truth to a metaphysical substrate.

Key Terms
صدق (truth)
مطابقة (correspondence)
أعيان (external objects)
أذهان (mental representations)
نفس الأمر (state of affairs)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A judgment is true if it matches what exists in external reality or if it matches what is commonly believed in the mind.
Correction: Truth is correspondence to the state of affairs itself, independent of external existence or mental representation. Judgments about mental concepts can be true, and falsehoods can be in minds.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, what is the correct criterion for the truth of a judgment?
A) Correspondence to external objects (aʿyān)
B) Correspondence to what is in the minds of knowledgeable people
C) Correspondence to what is in the state of affairs itself (mā fī nafs al-amr)
D) Correspondence to the Active Intellect

Key Takeaway
Truth in judgments is determined by correspondence to the state of affairs itself, not to external objects or mental representations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you decide something is true, do you check whether it corresponds to the state of affairs itself, or do you rely on what makes sense to you personally? Give a recent example where your subjective impression was wrong.
?
If truth were only what is in your mind, how would you know if your belief about Allah is actually correct? How does this lesson change your responsibility in seeking knowledge?
?
Are there any beliefs you hold that you have never tested against nafs al-amr? What would it take to honestly examine them, even if it is uncomfortable?

Answering the Objection: How Can Truth Apply to Non-Existents?
Al-Taftazani answers an objection about non-existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the criterion of truth as correspondence to the state of affairs applies to judgments about non-existents and impossibilities, through summary and detailed arguments.

Core Idea
Even judgments about impossibilities can be true because correspondence is a relation realized in the intellect.

Al-Taftazani anticipates a natural objection: how can a judgment be true by correspondence to a 'state of affairs' if it deals with non-existents (maʿdūmāt) or impossibilities (mumtaniʿāt) that have no essence or thingness? He provides a twofold answer: first, a summary appeal to our certainty that such judgments are true; second, a detailed explanation that correspondence is a relation (iḍāfa) that only requires the relata to be realized in the intellect. This preserves the objectivity of truth without requiring external existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the two-part answer
Notice the objection is about non-existents and impossibilities. The answer has two parts: summary (ijmālan) and detail (tafsīlan).
The summary relies on direct certainty; the detail provides a mechanism.
2
Understand the summary answer
Al-Taftazani says we know with certainty that 'the conjunction of two opposites is impossible' corresponds to reality, even without fully understanding how.
This appeals to immediate knowledge (badīhī).
3
Analyze the detailed answer
Correspondence is a relation. The intellect finds a necessary or demonstrative relation between two concepts, whether existent or not. That relation, considered in itself, is the reality.
Thus truth is about the relation, not about external things.

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the criterion for truth in judgments (ḥukm). The author, al-Taftazani, begins by critiquing the interpretation of the 'judge' (al-ḥākim) as the Active Intellect, finding it far-fetched. He then clarifies that a judgment can be correct (ṣaḥīḥ) i.e., true (ḥaqq) and veracious (ṣidq), or corrupt (fāsid) i.e., false (bāṭil) and mendacious (kidhb). Truth and falsehood are commonly used for statements, but the truth of a judgment is not correspondence to external objects (aʿyān) because the terms may not exist externally, as in judgments about mental concepts. Nor is it correspondence to mental representations (adhhān), because false judgments may be in the mind; e.g., the philosophers' belief that the world is eternal would be true if truth were correspondence to their minds, but it is false. The correct standard is correspondence to 'what is in the state of affairs itself' (mā fī nafs al-amr), i.e., the matter as it is in itself, independent of perception or report. An objection is then raised: how can this apply to non-existents and impossibilities that have no essence or thingness? The answer is twofold. Summarily, we know with certainty that judgments about impossibilities (e.g., 'the conjunction of two opposites is impossible') correspond to reality, even if we cannot fully explain the mechanism. In detail, correspondence is a relation (iḍāfa) that only requires the two relata to be realized in the intellect. The intellect finds necessary or demonstrative relations between any two concepts, whether existent or not. This relation, considered in itself without regard to the perceiver, is the reality (wāqiʿ). The truth of a proposition is that it corresponds to this relation. Since such relations among non-existents only occur intellectually, some philosophers posited an eternal immaterial substance called the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl) in which all forms and judgments are depicted. They defined 'what is in the state of affairs itself' as what is in the Active Intellect. The proof for its existence: judgments include some that correspond to reality and some that do not; the first requires an external object. Moreover, some judgments are eternal and unchanging, so their object must be in an eternal substance containing all things actually. This argument ties the epistemological criterion of truth to a metaphysical substrate.

Key Terms
معدوم (non-existent)
ممتنع (impossible)
إضافة (relation)
ضرورة (necessity)
برهان (demonstration)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Truth cannot apply to judgments about non-existent things because there is nothing 'out there' to correspond to.
Correction: Truth is based on intellectual relations that are objective and independent of external existence, as the intellect finds necessary relations even among non-existents.

Checkpoint
In the detailed answer, what is sufficient for a correspondence relation to hold?
A) The two relata must exist in the external world
B) The two relata must be realized in the intellect
C) The two relata must be known to the majority of people
D) The two relata must be material objects

Key Takeaway
Truth about non-existents works because correspondence is an intellectual relation, not a physical one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief about something you cannot see or experience—like the impossibility of a perfect world without suffering—do you ever sense an inner certainty that this is true? What is that certainty based on? Would you be willing to stake your life on it, even without being able to explain how it is true?
?
Al-Taftazani says the intellect can find a necessary relation between two meanings regardless of whether they exist externally. Think of a time you judged a falsehood to be true because you wanted it to be real. Did you ignore an objective relation your intellect was showing you? What would it take for you to submit that judgment to the standard of truth he describes?
?
The passage affirms that even about impossibilities, our judgments can be true. If there are truths about God, the afterlife, or the purpose of life that you consider 'impossible' to know for sure, does this lesson challenge that? Are you content to live with uncertainty, or do you owe it to yourself to seek the intellectual relation that settles it?

The Active Intellect as a Metaphysical Ground for Truth
Al-Taftazani presents the Active Intellect as an explanation for the ground of truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some philosophers posited the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl) as the repository of all true relations, and how they argued for its existence from the nature of eternal judgments.

Core Idea
Eternal and unchanging judgments require an eternal mind where all truths exist actually.

After establishing that truth is correspondence to objective intellectual relations, al-Taftazani presents a philosophical explanation: some thinkers identified the locus of all such relations as the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl) — an eternal immaterial substance containing all forms of beings and judgments. They argued that because some judgments are eternal and unchanging, and because correspondence to the state of affairs must have a single objective ground, there must be an eternal mind where all these relations are actually depicted. This lesson examines that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the Active Intellect
Notice how the passage transitions from the nature of relations to the need for an eternal substrate containing all truths.
The concept of 'depicted forms' (murtasima) is key.
2
Identify the proof for the Active Intellect
The proof has two parts: (1) some judgments correspond to the state of affairs, requiring an external object; (2) some judgments are eternal and unchanging, requiring an eternal subject.
The proof is a syllogism: eternal truths need an eternal bearer.
3
Understand the conclusion
The external object of true judgments must be an eternal immaterial substance containing all truths actually, not potentially.
This is the Active Intellect.

Source Text

الحاكم وتفسيره بما في العقل الفعال بعيداً جداً، اذا قصد بيان المفهوم، لأنه قد يقع التصديق ممن لا يعرفه بل ينكره وأما الاعتراض بأنه لا يشمل علمه، ولا العلم السابق عليه، ولا العلم بالجزئيات فيمكن دفعه : . يعني . أن الحكم قد يكون صحيحاً أي حقاً وصدقاً وقد يكون فاسداً أي باطلاً وكذباً وإن كان غالب استعمال الصدق والكذب في الأقوال خاصة، وليست صحة الحكم بمطابقته لما في الأعيان، إذ قد لا يتحقق طرفا الحكم في الخارج، كما في الحكم بالأمور الذهنية، على الأمور الذهنية أو الخارجية . كقولنا : الإمكان اعتباري ومقابل للامتناع واجتماع النقيضين ممتنع . " كقولنا : الإنسان ممكن أو أعمى ولا يكفي المطابقة لما في الأذهان لأنه قد يرتسم فيها الأحكام الغير المطابقة للواقع ، فلزم أن يكون قولنا : العالم قديم حقاً وصدقاً، لمطابقته لما في أذهان الفلاسفة، وهو باطل قطعاً، بل المعتبر في صحة الحكم مطابقته لما في نفس الأمر، وهو المراد بـالـواقـع والخارج، أي خارج ذات المدرك والمخبر ومعناه ما يفهم من قولنا هذا الأمر كذا في نفسه أو ليس كذا أي في حد ذاته وبالنظر إليه مع قطع النظر عن إدراك المدرك ، وإخبار المخبر على أن المراد بالأمر الشأن والشيء، وبالنفس الذات . " فإن قيل : كيف يتصور هذا فيما لا ذات له، ولا شيئية في الأعيان كالمعدومات سيما الممتنعات . فالجواب إجمالاً : أنا نعلم قطعاً أن قولنا : اجتماع الضدين مستحيل مطابق لما في نفس الأمر وقولنا : إنه ممكن غير مطابق، وإن لم يعلم كيفية تلك المطابقة بكنهها ، ولم يتمكن من تلخيص العبارة فيها. وتفصيلاً : أن المطابقة إضافة يكفيها تحقق المضافين بحسب العقل، ولا خفاء في أن العقل عند ملاحظة المعنيين والمقايسة بينهما سواء كانا من الموجودات أو المعلومات، تجد بينهما بحسب كل زمان نسبة إيجابية أو سلبية تقتضيها الضرورة أو البرهان فتلك النسبة من حيث إنها نتيجة الضرورة أو البرهان بالنظر إلى نفس ذلك المعقول من غير خصوصية المدرك والمخبر، هي المراد بالواقع وما في نفس الأمر، وبالخارج أيضاً عند من يجعله أعم مما في الأعيان على ما بينا، فصحة هذه النسبة تكون بمعنى أنها الواقع وما في نفس الأمر، وصحة النسبة المعقولة، أو الملفوظة من زيد أو عمرو أو غيرهما بين ذينك المعنين، يكون بمعنى أنها مطابقة لتلك النسبة الواقعة، أي على وفقها في الإيجاب والسلب ولما لم تتصور للنسبة المسماة بالواقع، ومما في نفس الأمر ، سيما فيما بين المعدومات حصول إلا بحسب التعقل، وكان عندهم أن جميع صور الكائنات وأحكام الموجودات والمعدومات مرتسمة في جوهر مجرد أزلي يسمى بالعقل الفعال فسر بعضهم ما في نفس الأمر بما في العقل الفعال ، ويستدل على وجوده بأن الأحكام مع اشتراكها في الثبوت الذهني، منها ما هو مطابق لما في نفس الأمر كالحكم بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين، ومنها ما هو غير مطابق كالحكم بنقيض ذلك. فللأول متعلق خارج عن الذهن يطابقه ما في الذهن ولأن من الأحكام ما هو أزلي ، لا يلحقه تغير أصلاً ولا خروج من قوة إلى فعل، ولا يتعلق بوضع أو زمان أو مكان، مع أن المطابقة لما في نفس الأمر في الكل معنى واحد، لزم أن يكون ذلك المتعلق الخارجي مرتسماً في مجرد أزلي مشتمل على الكل بالفعل،
The judge and its interpretation by what is in the Active Intellect is very far if one intends to explain the concept, because assent may occur from one who does not know it but rather denies it. As for the objection that it does not include His knowledge, nor the knowledge prior to it, nor knowledge of particulars, it can be repelled: meaning that the judgment may be correct, i.e., true and veracious, and may be corrupt, i.e., false and mendacious, although the usual application of truth and falsehood is specifically to statements. The correctness of a judgment is not its correspondence to what is in external objects (aʿyān), since the two terms of the judgment may not be realized in the external, as in a judgment about mental matters on mental or external matters, like our saying: 'Possibility is conceptual and opposite to impossibility, and the conjunction of two opposites is impossible.' Like our saying: 'Human being is possible' or 'blind.' Nor is correspondence to what is in minds sufficient, because judgments not corresponding to reality may be depicted in them. Thus it would follow that our saying 'The world is eternal' is true and veracious because of its correspondence to what is in the minds of the philosophers, but it is absolutely false. Rather, what is considered in the correctness of a judgment is its correspondence to what is in the state of affairs itself (mā fī nafs al-amr), which is meant by 'reality' (al-wāqiʿ) and 'external' (al-khārij), i.e., external to the essence of the perceiver and the informant. Its meaning is what is understood from our saying 'This matter is such in itself or not such,' i.e., in its very essence and with consideration of it, disregarding the perception of the perceiver and the report of the informant, given that by 'matter' is meant affair and thing, and by 'self' is meant essence. If it is said: 'How can this be conceived for what has no essence and no thingness in external objects, like non-existents (maʿdūmāt), especially impossibilities (mumtaniʿāt)?' The answer in summary: we know with certainty that our saying 'The conjunction of two opposites is impossible' corresponds to what is in the state of affairs itself, and our saying 'It is possible' does not correspond, even if we do not know the how of that correspondence in its reality and are unable to summarize the expression for it. In detail: correspondence is a relation for which the realization of the two relata according to the intellect is sufficient. It is no secret that the intellect, when observing the two meanings and comparing them, whether they are among existents or among objects of knowledge, finds between them in every time a positive or negative relation necessitated by necessity or demonstration. That relation, insofar as it is a result of necessity or demonstration with regard to the very intelligible, without particularity of the perceiver and informant, is what is meant by 'reality' and 'what is in the state of affairs itself,' and also by 'external' according to those who make it more general than what is in external objects as we have explained. The correctness of this relation is in the sense that it is reality and what is in the state of affairs itself. The correctness of the relation that is intellected or uttered by Zayd or ʿAmr or others between those two meanings is in the sense that it corresponds to that occurring relation, i.e., in accordance with it in affirmation and negation. Since the relation called 'reality' and 'what is in the state of affairs itself,' especially among non-existents, cannot be conceived to occur except with respect to intellection, and according to them all forms of beings and judgments of existents and non-existents are depicted in an eternal immaterial substance called the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl), some explained 'what is in the state of affairs itself' by what is in the Active Intellect. They adduce evidence for its existence: judgments, despite their commonality in mental subsistence, include some that correspond to what is in the state of affairs itself, like the judgment that one is half of two, and some that are non-corresponding, like the judgment to the contrary. For the first, there is an external object that the mental object corresponds to. And because among judgments there is what is eternal, not subject to any change, nor emergence from potency to act, nor dependent on position, time, or place, while correspondence to what is in the state of affairs itself is one meaning in all, it follows that that external object is depicted in an eternal immaterial substance containing all actually.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is an argument about the criterion for truth in judgments (ḥukm). The author, al-Taftazani, begins by critiquing the interpretation of the 'judge' (al-ḥākim) as the Active Intellect, finding it far-fetched. He then clarifies that a judgment can be correct (ṣaḥīḥ) i.e., true (ḥaqq) and veracious (ṣidq), or corrupt (fāsid) i.e., false (bāṭil) and mendacious (kidhb). Truth and falsehood are commonly used for statements, but the truth of a judgment is not correspondence to external objects (aʿyān) because the terms may not exist externally, as in judgments about mental concepts. Nor is it correspondence to mental representations (adhhān), because false judgments may be in the mind; e.g., the philosophers' belief that the world is eternal would be true if truth were correspondence to their minds, but it is false. The correct standard is correspondence to 'what is in the state of affairs itself' (mā fī nafs al-amr), i.e., the matter as it is in itself, independent of perception or report. An objection is then raised: how can this apply to non-existents and impossibilities that have no essence or thingness? The answer is twofold. Summarily, we know with certainty that judgments about impossibilities (e.g., 'the conjunction of two opposites is impossible') correspond to reality, even if we cannot fully explain the mechanism. In detail, correspondence is a relation (iḍāfa) that only requires the two relata to be realized in the intellect. The intellect finds necessary or demonstrative relations between any two concepts, whether existent or not. This relation, considered in itself without regard to the perceiver, is the reality (wāqiʿ). The truth of a proposition is that it corresponds to this relation. Since such relations among non-existents only occur intellectually, some philosophers posited an eternal immaterial substance called the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl) in which all forms and judgments are depicted. They defined 'what is in the state of affairs itself' as what is in the Active Intellect. The proof for its existence: judgments include some that correspond to reality and some that do not; the first requires an external object. Moreover, some judgments are eternal and unchanging, so their object must be in an eternal substance containing all things actually. This argument ties the epistemological criterion of truth to a metaphysical substrate.

Key Terms
عقل فعال (Active Intellect)
جوهر مجرد (immaterial substance)
أزلي (eternal)
مرتسم (depicted)
قوة وفعل (potency and act)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Active Intellect is a necessary article of faith in Islam or a doctrine all Muslim theologians accept.
Correction: Al-Taftazani presents the Active Intellect as an interpretation by 'some' philosophers (not all), and it is a metaphysical explanation, not a required belief.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given for positing an eternal immaterial substance containing all truths?
A) Because all judgments are mental and need a mind to exist
B) Because some judgments are eternal and unchanging, so their ground must be eternal
C) Because philosophers liked the concept
D) Because the Quran mentions it

Key Takeaway
The Active Intellect is posited as the eternal substrate where all objective relations and truths are actually depicted, grounding the truth of eternal judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that eternal judgments like 'one is half of two' require an eternal mind as their ground. Do you live with the same certainty in moral truths as you do in mathematical ones? If not, what would it take for you to treat them as equally grounded?
?
The passage distinguishes judgments that correspond to reality from those that do not. Think of a belief you hold today that might not correspond to reality. Are you willing to test it against the truth, even if it costs you comfort? What concrete step can you take this week to check it?
?
If truth is anchored in an eternal, unchanging source, then your soul depends on aligning with it. Have you ever knowingly chosen a falsehood because it was easier? How would your life change if you committed to never again knowingly assent to a false judgment?

Definition of Logical Correspondence (Muṭābaqah)
Al-Taftazani defines muṭābaqah and outlines the notion of objective reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what logical correspondence means and how it relates to the concept of 'reality' (al-wāqiʿ) and 'what is in the mind' (mā fī nafs al-amr).

Core Idea
Logical correspondence is the agreement of a mental judgment with objective reality.

Before we can reason about the Active Intellect, we must understand what it means for a judgment to be true. Al-Taftazani begins by defining logical correspondence (muṭābaqah) and clarifying the notion of 'reality' that judgments aim to match. This lesson unpacks that foundation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that correspondence (muṭābaqah) is described as a relation (iḍāfa) that only requires the mental existence of its two relata.
2
Understand the discovery of the real relation
The next part explains that the intellect, when comparing two meanings, finds an affirmative or negative relation between them. This relation is determined by necessity or demonstration, not by individual opinion.
3
Identify what 'reality' and 'what is in the mind' refer to
The passage identifies that real relation as 'reality' (al-wāqiʿ) and 'what is in the mind' (mā fī nafs al-amr), independent of any particular perceiver or informant.
4
Grasp the definition of a correct judgment
A mental or spoken judgment is correct precisely when it corresponds to that real relation in affirmation or negation.
This is the core of logical correspondence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تلك المطابقة بكنهها، ولم يتمكن من تلخيص العبارة فيها. وتفصيلاً: أن المطابقة إضافة يكفيها تحقق المضافين بحسب العقل، ولا خفاء في أن العقل عند ملاحظة المعنيين والمقايسة بينهما سواء كانا من الموجودات أو المعلومات، تجد بينهما بحسب كل زمان نسبة إيجابية أو سلبية تقتضيها الضرورة أو البرهان فتلك النسبة من حيث إنها نتيجة الضرورة أو البرهان بالنظر إلى نفس ذلك المعقول من غير خصوصية المدرك والمخبر، هي المراد بالواقع وما في نفس الأمر، وبالخارج أيضاً عند من يجعله أعم مما في الأعيان على ما بينا، فصحة هذه النسبة تكون بمعنى أنها الواقع وما في نفس الأمر، وصحة النسبة المعقولة، أو الملفوظة من زيد أو عمرو أو غيرهما بين ذينك المعنين، يكون بمعنى أنها مطابقة لتلك النسبة الواقعة، أي على وفقها في الإيجاب والسلب ولما لم تتصور للنسبة المسماة بالواقع وما في نفس الأمر، سيما فيما بين المعدومات حصول إلا بحسب التعقل، وكان عندهم أن جميع صور الكائنات وأحكام الموجودات والمعدومات مرتسمة في جوهر مجرد أزلي يسمى بالعقل الفعال فسر بعضهم ما في نفس الأمر بما في العقل الفعال، ويستدل على وجوده بأن الأحكام مع اشتراكها في الثبوت الذهني، منها ما هو مطابق لما في نفس الأمر كالحكم بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين، ومنها ما هو غير مطابق كالحكم بنقيض ذلك. فللأول متعلق خارج عن الذهن يطابقه ما في الذهن ولأن من الأحكام ما هو أزلي، لا يلحقه تغير أصلاً ولا خروج من قوة إلى فعل، ولا يتعلق بوضع أو زمان أو مكان، مع أن المطابقة لما في نفس الأمر في الكل معنى واحد، لزم أن يكون ذلك المتعلق الخارجي مرتسماً في مجرد أزلي مشتمل على الكل بالفعل، وليس هو الواجب لامتناع اشتماله على الكثرة، ولا النفس لامتناع اشتمالها على الكل بالفعل فتعين العقل الفعال.
That correspondence in its essence, and he could not summarize the expression in it. And in detail: correspondence is a relation for which the verification of the two relata according to reason suffices. There is no concealment that the intellect, when observing the two meanings and comparing between them, whether they are from existing things or known things, finds between them according to every time an affirmative or negative relation that necessity or demonstration requires. So that relation, insofar as it is a result of necessity or demonstration, with regard to that very intelligible without particularity of the perceiver and the informant, is what is meant by 'reality' (al-wāqiʿ) and 'what is in the mind' (mā fī nafs al-amr), and also by 'external' (al-khārij) according to one who makes it more general than what is in concrete individuals (al-aʿyān) as we have explained. So the correctness (ṣiḥḥa) of this relation is in the sense that it is reality and what is in the mind, and the correctness of the relation that is conceived or uttered by Zayd or ʿAmr or others between those two meanings is in the sense that it corresponds to that real relation, i.e., in accordance with it in affirmation and negation. And since the relation called 'reality' and 'what is in the mind', especially among non-existents, is not conceived to obtain except by intellection, and they held that all forms of beings and judgments of existents and non-existents are imprinted in an eternal immaterial substance called the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl), so some interpreted 'what is in the mind' as what is in the Active Intellect. And one infers its existence by the fact that judgments, despite their sharing in mental subsistence, include what is corresponding to what is in the mind, like the judgment that one is half of two, and what is non-corresponding, like the judgment of its opposite. So the former has an object external to the mind to which what is in the mind corresponds. And because among judgments there is what is eternal, not affected by change at all, nor exit from potentiality to actuality, nor dependent on position, time, or place, while correspondence to what is in the mind in all is one meaning, it necessarily follows that that external object is imprinted in an eternal immaterial substance containing the whole actually. And it is not the Necessary Existent (al-wājib) due to the impossibility of its containing multiplicity, nor the soul due to the impossibility of its containing the whole actually, so the Active Intellect is determined.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage defines logical correspondence (muṭābaqah) and uses that definition to argue for the existence of the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl). The author begins by explaining that correspondence is a relation between two mental concepts that suffices with their verification in the mind. Then he clarifies that when the intellect considers two meanings and compares them, it finds an affirmative or negative relation between them, which is determined by necessity or demonstration. This relation, considered purely in itself, is what is meant by 'reality' (al-wāqiʿ) or 'what is in the mind' (mā fī nafs al-amr). The correctness of a mental or spoken judgment is its correspondence to that real relation. Next, he notes that some scholars (likely philosophers) hold that all forms and judgments are imprinted in an eternal immaterial substance called the Active Intellect, and they interpret 'what is in the mind' as being in the Active Intellect. The proof for the Active Intellect proceeds as follows: among mental judgments, some correspond to reality (e.g., 'one is half of two') and some do not. That which corresponds must have an external object. Moreover, some judgments are eternal and unchanging, independent of time, place, or potentiality. Since the meaning of correspondence is the same for all, the external object must be an eternal substance containing all such judgments actually. This substance cannot be the Necessary Existent (God) because He cannot contain multiplicity, nor the human soul because it does not contain all judgments actually. Therefore, it must be the Active Intellect.

Key Terms
مطابقة
واقع
ما في نفس الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'what is in the mind' (mā fī nafs al-amr) refers to subjective opinion.
Correction: It refers to an objective relation that the intellect discovers, independent of individual perception.

Checkpoint

A) The concrete external world
B) The relation found by the intellect independent of perceiver
C) The judgment of the majority
D) The Active Intellect

Key Takeaway
Logical correspondence is the agreement of a mental judgment with the objective relation that the intellect discovers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a claim about something, do you ensure that your inner conviction matches the reality of the matter, or do you sometimes let wishful thinking shape your judgment? What is one area of your life where you need to align your beliefs with what is objectively true?
?
The passage defines truth as correspondence to reality. In your daily interactions, are your words and actions a true reflection of what you know to be right? Reflect on a recent situation where you said or did something that did not correspond to the truth. What will you do differently?
?
If you truly internalized that God's knowledge of reality is the ultimate standard, how would it change the way you form opinions about others or about events you cannot fully see? Commit to one concrete change today.

Proof for the Existence of the Active Intellect
Al-Taftazani presents a proof for the Active Intellect based on the nature of eternal judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the need for a universal, eternal substrate for objective truths leads to the existence of the Active Intellect.

Core Idea
Objective, eternal truths require an eternal mind that holds them actually.

Having established what it means for a judgment to correspond to reality, Al-Taftazani now turns to a philosophical proof for the Active Intellect. He argues that because some judgments are eternal and unchanging, they must be grounded in an eternal immaterial substance that contains them all actually—and that substance is the Active Intellect, not God or the human soul.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise about judgments
Judgments share mental subsistence, but some correspond to reality (e.g., 'one is half of two') while others do not.
2
Recognize the need for an external object
Matching judgments must have an external object that they correspond to, beyond the mind.
3
Consider eternal, unchanging judgments
Some judgments are eternal—they do not change, depend on time/place, or involve potentiality. Their correspondence is always true.
4
Infer the nature of the external object
Since correspondence is the same for all such judgments, the external object must be an eternal substance containing them all actually.
5
Eliminate alternatives
This substance cannot be God (because He cannot contain multiplicity) nor the human soul (because it does not contain all actually). Therefore, it is the Active Intellect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تلك المطابقة بكنهها، ولم يتمكن من تلخيص العبارة فيها. وتفصيلاً: أن المطابقة إضافة يكفيها تحقق المضافين بحسب العقل، ولا خفاء في أن العقل عند ملاحظة المعنيين والمقايسة بينهما سواء كانا من الموجودات أو المعلومات، تجد بينهما بحسب كل زمان نسبة إيجابية أو سلبية تقتضيها الضرورة أو البرهان فتلك النسبة من حيث إنها نتيجة الضرورة أو البرهان بالنظر إلى نفس ذلك المعقول من غير خصوصية المدرك والمخبر، هي المراد بالواقع وما في نفس الأمر، وبالخارج أيضاً عند من يجعله أعم مما في الأعيان على ما بينا، فصحة هذه النسبة تكون بمعنى أنها الواقع وما في نفس الأمر، وصحة النسبة المعقولة، أو الملفوظة من زيد أو عمرو أو غيرهما بين ذينك المعنين، يكون بمعنى أنها مطابقة لتلك النسبة الواقعة، أي على وفقها في الإيجاب والسلب ولما لم تتصور للنسبة المسماة بالواقع وما في نفس الأمر، سيما فيما بين المعدومات حصول إلا بحسب التعقل، وكان عندهم أن جميع صور الكائنات وأحكام الموجودات والمعدومات مرتسمة في جوهر مجرد أزلي يسمى بالعقل الفعال فسر بعضهم ما في نفس الأمر بما في العقل الفعال، ويستدل على وجوده بأن الأحكام مع اشتراكها في الثبوت الذهني، منها ما هو مطابق لما في نفس الأمر كالحكم بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين، ومنها ما هو غير مطابق كالحكم بنقيض ذلك. فللأول متعلق خارج عن الذهن يطابقه ما في الذهن ولأن من الأحكام ما هو أزلي، لا يلحقه تغير أصلاً ولا خروج من قوة إلى فعل، ولا يتعلق بوضع أو زمان أو مكان، مع أن المطابقة لما في نفس الأمر في الكل معنى واحد، لزم أن يكون ذلك المتعلق الخارجي مرتسماً في مجرد أزلي مشتمل على الكل بالفعل، وليس هو الواجب لامتناع اشتماله على الكثرة، ولا النفس لامتناع اشتمالها على الكل بالفعل فتعين العقل الفعال.
That correspondence in its essence, and he could not summarize the expression in it. And in detail: correspondence is a relation for which the verification of the two relata according to reason suffices. There is no concealment that the intellect, when observing the two meanings and comparing between them, whether they are from existing things or known things, finds between them according to every time an affirmative or negative relation that necessity or demonstration requires. So that relation, insofar as it is a result of necessity or demonstration, with regard to that very intelligible without particularity of the perceiver and the informant, is what is meant by 'reality' (al-wāqiʿ) and 'what is in the mind' (mā fī nafs al-amr), and also by 'external' (al-khārij) according to one who makes it more general than what is in concrete individuals (al-aʿyān) as we have explained. So the correctness (ṣiḥḥa) of this relation is in the sense that it is reality and what is in the mind, and the correctness of the relation that is conceived or uttered by Zayd or ʿAmr or others between those two meanings is in the sense that it corresponds to that real relation, i.e., in accordance with it in affirmation and negation. And since the relation called 'reality' and 'what is in the mind', especially among non-existents, is not conceived to obtain except by intellection, and they held that all forms of beings and judgments of existents and non-existents are imprinted in an eternal immaterial substance called the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl), so some interpreted 'what is in the mind' as what is in the Active Intellect. And one infers its existence by the fact that judgments, despite their sharing in mental subsistence, include what is corresponding to what is in the mind, like the judgment that one is half of two, and what is non-corresponding, like the judgment of its opposite. So the former has an object external to the mind to which what is in the mind corresponds. And because among judgments there is what is eternal, not affected by change at all, nor exit from potentiality to actuality, nor dependent on position, time, or place, while correspondence to what is in the mind in all is one meaning, it necessarily follows that that external object is imprinted in an eternal immaterial substance containing the whole actually. And it is not the Necessary Existent (al-wājib) due to the impossibility of its containing multiplicity, nor the soul due to the impossibility of its containing the whole actually, so the Active Intellect is determined.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage defines logical correspondence (muṭābaqah) and uses that definition to argue for the existence of the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl). The author begins by explaining that correspondence is a relation between two mental concepts that suffices with their verification in the mind. Then he clarifies that when the intellect considers two meanings and compares them, it finds an affirmative or negative relation between them, which is determined by necessity or demonstration. This relation, considered purely in itself, is what is meant by 'reality' (al-wāqiʿ) or 'what is in the mind' (mā fī nafs al-amr). The correctness of a mental or spoken judgment is its correspondence to that real relation. Next, he notes that some scholars (likely philosophers) hold that all forms and judgments are imprinted in an eternal immaterial substance called the Active Intellect, and they interpret 'what is in the mind' as being in the Active Intellect. The proof for the Active Intellect proceeds as follows: among mental judgments, some correspond to reality (e.g., 'one is half of two') and some do not. That which corresponds must have an external object. Moreover, some judgments are eternal and unchanging, independent of time, place, or potentiality. Since the meaning of correspondence is the same for all, the external object must be an eternal substance containing all such judgments actually. This substance cannot be the Necessary Existent (God) because He cannot contain multiplicity, nor the human soul because it does not contain all judgments actually. Therefore, it must be the Active Intellect.

Key Terms
عقل فعال
ما في نفس الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Active Intellect is the same as God.
Correction: The passage explicitly distinguishes them: God cannot contain multiplicity, so He cannot be the eternal substrate.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, why cannot the Necessary Existent (al-wājib) be the eternal substrate for judgments?
A) Because it is not eternal
B) Because it cannot contain multiplicity
C) Because it is not immaterial
D) Because it does not know particulars

Key Takeaway
The Active Intellect is the eternal, immaterial substance containing all universal truths actually.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You've learned that the Active Intellect holds all eternal truths actually, beyond time and change. But consider: do you live as though truth is objective and unchanging, or do you subtly relativize it to suit your desires? Which judgments in your life have you treated as merely 'your opinion' when they are actually eternal truths? Take one such truth and commit to aligning your actions with it, not just your intellect.
?
The soul does not contain all judgments actually—you must learn and discover. Are you content with your current partial knowledge, or do you feel the ache to know the eternal truths that are already fully present in the Active Intellect? What is one area of ignorance you have been avoiding? Resolve today to seek that knowledge, trusting that its source is real and accessible.
?
Al-Taftazani distinguishes the Active Intellect from God because God cannot contain multiplicity. But you, like the soul, contain multiplicity—your thoughts, emotions, desires. How can you let the unity of the Active Intellect (the one truth behind all multiplicity) order your inner chaos? Pick one conflicting desire in your heart and subject it to a single, eternal truth from this lesson, such as 'correspondence to reality.'

Critique of Identifying the Active Intellect with the Preserved Tablet
The opponent's reasoning and initial Quranic objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasoning that leads some thinkers to identify the active intellect with the Preserved Tablet, and the author's initial Quranic objection to this identification.

Core Idea
The active intellect cannot be the Preserved Tablet because the Quran places all secrets with God alone.

This lesson presents the first part of the author's critique: an opponent argues that the active intellect must be the source of all forms, equating it with the Preserved Tablet. The author immediately objects with a Quranic verse showing that God alone holds the keys of the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's reasoning
Identify why the opponent rules out the Necessary and the soul, leading to the active intellect.
2
Note the identification with the Preserved Tablet
The opponent claims this active intellect is what the Quran calls the Preserved Tablet.
3
Observe the author's Quranic objection
The author says this contradicts the verse about the keys of the unseen being with God.
Consider why this is a strong theological objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس هو الواجب لامتناع اشتماله على الكثرة، ولا النفس لامتناع اشتمالها على الكل بالفعل فتعين العقل الفعال. ثم قال : وهو الذي عبر عنه في القرآن المجيد باللوح المحفوظ والكتاب المبين، المشتمل على كل رطب ويابس وأنت خبير بأن ما ذكره مع ضعف بعض مقدماته مخالف لصريح قوله تعالى : {وعنده مفاتح الغيب} الآية . فليته سكت عن التطبيق، ثم القول بأن المراد بما في نفس الأمر، ما في العقل الفعال باطل قطعاً ، لأن كل أحد من العقلاء يعرف أن قولنا : الواحد نصف الاثنين مطابق لما [؟] نفس الأمر، أنه لم يتصور العقل الفعال أصلاً، مع فضلا عن اعتقاد ثبوته، وارتسامه بصور الكائنات ، بلى مع أنه ينكر ثبوته، ويعتقد انتفاءه على ما هو رأي المتكلمين وكان المراد أن ما في نفس الأمر على وجه يعم الكل، ولا يحتمل النقيض أصلاً، هو ما في العقل الفعال وإن تغايرا بحسب المفهوم، وقد يقال : لو أريد بما في نفس الأمر في علم العقل الفعال امتنع اعتبار المطابقة لما في نفس الأمر في علم العقل الفعال لعدم الاثنينية، وفي العلم السابق عليه ولو بالذات كعلم الواجب لامتناع مطابقة الشيء لما لا تحقق له معه وفي العلم بالجزئيات مثل هذا الحرف، وقيام زيد في هذا الوقت لامتناع ارتسامها في العقل ويمكن الجواب عن الأول : بأن صحة الحكم الذي في نفس الأمر لا يكون لكونه مطابقا لما في نفس الأمر بل عينه . وعن الثاني : بعد تسليم امتناع مطابقة الشيء مع ما هو متأخر عنه بالذات، بأن اعتبار المطابقة، إنما يكون في العلم الذي هو بارتسام الصورة، ولا كذلك علم الواجب على أنهم لا يثبتون له أولاً إلا تعقل ذاته، وهو عين ذاته وعن الثالث بأن ارتسام الجزئي في العقل على الوجه الكلي كاف في المطابقة
And it is not the Necessary (al-wajib) because of the impossibility of it containing multiplicity, nor the soul because of the impossibility of it containing the whole in act, so the active intellect (al-`aql al-fa``al) is determined. Then he said: And it is that which is expressed in the Noble Qur'an as the Preserved Tablet (al-lawh al-mahfuz) and the Clear Book, containing every moist and dry. And you are aware that what he mentioned, despite the weakness of some of its premises, contradicts the explicit text of His saying: {And with Him are the keys of the unseen} the verse. Would that he had kept silent about the application, then the statement that what is meant by 'what is in the mind of the matter' (ma fi nafs al-amr) is the active intellect is absolutely false, because every rational person knows that our statement 'one is half of two' is in correspondence with what [؟] the mind of the matter, that he has not conceived the active intellect at all, let alone belief in its existence and its impression of the forms of beings. Indeed, with the fact that he denies its existence and believes in its non-existence according to the view of the theologians (al-mutakallimin). And it was intended that what is in the mind of the matter in a way that includes everything and does not admit of contradiction at all is what is in the active intellect, even if they differ in concept. And it may be said: If what is meant by 'what is in the mind of the matter' is in the knowledge of the active intellect, then the consideration of correspondence (al-mutabaqa) to what is in the mind of the matter in the knowledge of the active intellect is impossible due to the lack of duality, and in the knowledge prior to it even if by essence like the knowledge of the Necessary (al-wajib) due to the impossibility of a thing corresponding to what has no actuality with it, and in knowledge of particulars like this letter and Zayd's standing at this time due to the impossibility of their impression in the intellect. And the answer to the first is possible: that the correctness of the judgment that is in the mind of the matter is not because it corresponds to what is in the mind of the matter, but rather it is the very same. And regarding the second: after conceding the impossibility of a thing's correspondence to what is posterior to it in essence, that the consideration of correspondence is only in knowledge that is by impression of the form, and the knowledge of the Necessary is not so, especially since they only affirm for Him firstly the intellection of His essence, and it is His very essence. And regarding the third: that the impression of the particular (al-juz'i) in the intellect in a universal manner is sufficient for correspondence.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage is a complex argumentative refutation by the author (likely al-Taftazani) of the claim that the active intellect is identical with the Preserved Tablet and serves as the standard of truth (ma fi nafs al-amr). The author begins by presenting an opponent's reasoning: the Necessary Being cannot be the source of multiplicity, the soul cannot contain all forms actually, so the active intellect is determined as the source. The opponent then identifies this active intellect with the Preserved Tablet mentioned in the Qur'an. The author immediately objects that this contradicts the Qur'anic verse 'And with Him are the keys of the unseen' (Surah al-An'am, 6:59), which indicates that the knowledge of all things is with God, not a created intellect. Then the author says it would have been better for the opponent to remain silent rather than force such an application. Next, the author refutes the specific claim that 'ma fi nafs al-amr' (the objective truth) refers to the active intellect. He gives an example: Everyone knows that the statement 'one is half of two' corresponds to objective truth, without having any concept of the active intellect, let alone believing in its existence or that it contains the forms of all beings. In fact, many theologians deny the existence of the active intellect altogether. Then the author offers a clarification: the intended meaning is that what is in the mind of the matter in a universal and non-contradictory sense is what is in the active intellect, even if they differ in concept. This is a more nuanced position. Then an objection is raised: If the active intellect's knowledge is meant, correspondence would be impossible because there is no duality in the active intellect (since it is simple), and because knowledge prior to it (like God's knowledge) does not involve representation, and because particulars cannot be impressed in an intellect. The author answers each: (1) The correctness of a judgment is not due to correspondence with something else, but it is identical with the objective truth itself. (2) Even if we accept that correspondence with posterior knowledge is impossible, correspondence applies only to knowledge by impression (like human knowledge), not to God's knowledge which is identical with His essence. (3) The representation of a particular in the intellect in a universal manner is sufficient for correspondence. The passage thus defends a specific epistemological view while criticizing a crude identification.

Key Terms
العقل الفعال
اللوح المحفوظ
الكتاب المبين
مفاتح الغيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the active intellect is the Preserved Tablet because it contains all forms.
Correction: The Quran attributes knowledge of all unseen things to God alone, not to a created intellect.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the identification of the active intellect with the Preserved Tablet?
A) The active intellect cannot contain multiplicity.
B) The soul actually contains all forms.
C) The Quran says the keys of the unseen are with God.
D) The active intellect is the Necessary Being.

Key Takeaway
The active intellect cannot be the Preserved Tablet because the Quran reserves all knowledge of the unseen for God alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find a philosophical or logical argument that seems to make sense about Allah, do you immediately accept it, or do you check it against the Quran and Sunnah first? How often do you let human reasoning override a clear verse?
?
The opponent's identification of the Preserved Tablet with the active intellect was an attempt to harmonize philosophy with revelation. Are there areas in your own life where you try to fit Allah's commands into your personal ideology or comfort zone, rather than submitting to His words as they are?
?
If you were in the author's place and heard someone forcing a Quranic term to support a flawed argument, would you have the courage to call it out as he did? When was the last time you corrected someone who misused a religious text, even if it made them uncomfortable?

Why the Active Intellect Cannot Be the Standard of Truth
Refutation of active intellect as the standard of truth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author considers it absolutely false to equate 'ma fi nafs al-amr' with the active intellect, using the example of the truth 'one is half of two'.

Core Idea
Ordinary truths are known without any concept of the active intellect.

The author now directly refutes the specific claim that the objective standard of truth (ma fi nafs al-amr) is the active intellect. He argues that even someone who has never heard of the active intellect still knows that 'one is half of two' corresponds to reality.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim being refuted
The claim is that 'ma fi nafs al-amr' refers to the active intellect.
2
Analyze the example
Consider the statement 'one is half of two'. Everyone knows it is true without thinking of the active intellect.
This shows the active intellect is not necessary for truth.
3
Notice the author's strong language
He says this view is 'absolutely false' (bāṭil qaṭʿan) and gives the example.
He also points out that many theologians deny the active intellect's existence.

And it is not the Necessary (al-wajib) because of the impossibility of it containing multiplicity, nor the soul because of the impossibility of it containing the whole in act, so the active intellect (al-`aql al-fa``al) is determined. Then he said: And it is that which is expressed in the Noble Qur'an as the Preserved Tablet (al-lawh al-mahfuz) and the Clear Book, containing every moist and dry. Then an objection is raised: If the active intellect's knowledge is meant, correspondence would be impossible because there is no duality in the active intellect (since it is simple), and because knowledge prior to it (like God's knowledge) does not involve representation, and because particulars cannot be impressed in an intellect. The author answers each: (1) The correctness of a judgment is not due to correspondence with something else, but it is identical with the objective truth itself. (2) Even if we accept that correspondence with posterior knowledge is impossible, correspondence applies only to knowledge by impression (like human knowledge), not to God's knowledge which is identical with His essence. (3) The representation of a particular in the intellect in a universal manner is sufficient for correspondence. The passage thus defends a specific epistemological view while criticizing a crude identification.

Key Terms

العقل الفعال
المطابقة
الكائنات

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that objective truth requires a reference to the active intellect.
Correction: Ordinary truths like 'one is half of two' are known without any notion of the active intellect.

Checkpoint
What example does the author use to refute the claim that 'ma fi nafs al-amr' is the active intellect?
A) 'The whole is greater than the part'
B) 'One is half of two'
C) 'The Preserved Tablet contains all knowledge'
D) 'The active intellect is simple'

Key Takeaway
Objective truth does not depend on the active intellect; even those who deny its existence can know truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever hesitate to accept a simple truth about your faith because you think you lack intellectual credentials? Reflect on the example of 'one is half of two'—is there a truth about God or the afterlife that you already know but doubt because you feel unqualified? Will you embrace it today, trusting your innate certainty?
?
The passage shows that even those who reject the active intellect know that 'one is half of two' is true. Are you denying a clear truth about your own state — perhaps a sin, a duty, or a belief — because you fear being unsophisticated or because others deny it? What is that truth, and when will you act on it?
?
The theologians (mutakallimun) in the passage deny the active intellect entirely. Could your own certainty about basic Islamic beliefs (e.g., that God exists and is one) be more reliable than you think, even without complex philosophical proofs? Name one such belief that you are completely certain of, and ask yourself why you still sometimes act as if it were uncertain.

Objections to the Active Intellect as Standard and Their Answers
Clarification and objection-answer dialectic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will grasp the nuanced clarification offered by the author, as well as the three objections and their answers regarding the active intellect as the standard of truth.

Core Idea
Even if the active intellect were the standard, correspondence with it faces logical difficulties that the author resolves.

The author first clarifies a possible nuanced view: perhaps the active intellect is the standard in a universal sense. Then he raises three objections: lack of duality in the intellect, impossibility of correspondence with prior knowledge, and the inability of intellect to contain particulars. He answers each carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the nuanced clarification
The author allows that maybe the active intellect is the standard in a universal, non-contradictory sense.
This shows he is not dismissive, but precise.
2
Identify the three objections
There are three objections: (1) no duality in active intellect, (2) impossible to correspond with God's knowledge, (3) particulars cannot be impressed.
3
Study the first answer
The first answer: correctness of a judgment is not correspondence but identity with the truth.
4
Study the second and third answers
Second: correspondence only applies to knowledge by impression, not God's knowledge. Third: universal representation of particulars is sufficient.
These answers defend the possibility of the active intellect as standard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس هو الواجب لامتناع اشتماله على الكثرة، ولا النفس لامتناع اشتمالها على الكل بالفعل فتعين العقل الفعال. ثم قال : وهو الذي عبر عنه في القرآن المجيد باللوح المحفوظ والكتاب المبين، المشتمل على كل رطب ويابس وأنت خبير بأن ما ذكره مع ضعف بعض مقدماته مخالف لصريح قوله تعالى : {وعنده مفاتح الغيب} الآية . فليته سكت عن التطبيق، ثم القول بأن المراد بما في نفس الأمر، ما في العقل الفعال باطل قطعاً ، لأن كل أحد من العقلاء يعرف أن قولنا : الواحد نصف الاثنين مطابق لما [؟] نفس الأمر، أنه لم يتصور العقل الفعال أصلاً، مع فضلا عن اعتقاد ثبوته، وارتسامه بصور الكائنات ، بلى مع أنه ينكر ثبوته، ويعتقد انتفاءه على ما هو رأي المتكلمين وكان المراد أن ما في نفس الأمر على وجه يعم الكل، ولا يحتمل النقيض أصلاً، هو ما في العقل الفعال وإن تغايرا بحسب المفهوم، وقد يقال : لو أريد بما في نفس الأمر في علم العقل الفعال امتنع اعتبار المطابقة لما في نفس الأمر في علم العقل الفعال لعدم الاثنينية، وفي العلم السابق عليه ولو بالذات كعلم الواجب لامتناع مطابقة الشيء لما لا تحقق له معه وفي العلم بالجزئيات مثل هذا الحرف، وقيام زيد في هذا الوقت لامتناع ارتسامها في العقل ويمكن الجواب عن الأول : بأن صحة الحكم الذي في نفس الأمر لا يكون لكونه مطابقا لما في نفس الأمر بل عينه . وعن الثاني : بعد تسليم امتناع مطابقة الشيء مع ما هو متأخر عنه بالذات، بأن اعتبار المطابقة، إنما يكون في العلم الذي هو بارتسام الصورة، ولا كذلك علم الواجب على أنهم لا يثبتون له أولاً إلا تعقل ذاته، وهو عين ذاته وعن الثالث بأن ارتسام الجزئي في العقل على الوجه الكلي كاف في المطابقة
And it is not the Necessary (al-wajib) because of the impossibility of it containing multiplicity, nor the soul because of the impossibility of it containing the whole in act, so the active intellect (al-`aql al-fa``al) is determined. Then he said: And it is that which is expressed in the Noble Qur'an as the Preserved Tablet (al-lawh al-mahfuz) and the Clear Book, containing every moist and dry. And you are aware that what he mentioned, despite the weakness of some of its premises, contradicts the explicit text of His saying: {And with Him are the keys of the unseen} the verse. Would that he had kept silent about the application, then the statement that what is meant by 'what is in the mind of the matter' (ma fi nafs al-amr) is the active intellect is absolutely false, because every rational person knows that our statement 'one is half of two' is in correspondence with what [؟] the mind of the matter, that he has not conceived the active intellect at all, let alone belief in its existence and its impression of the forms of beings. Indeed, with the fact that he denies its existence and believes in its non-existence according to the view of the theologians (al-mutakallimin). And it was intended that what is in the mind of the matter in a way that includes everything and does not admit of contradiction at all is what is in the active intellect, even if they differ in concept. And it may be said: If what is meant by 'what is in the mind of the matter' is in the knowledge of the active intellect, then the consideration of correspondence (al-mutabaqa) to what is in the mind of the matter in the knowledge of the active intellect is impossible due to the lack of duality, and in the knowledge prior to it even if by essence like the knowledge of the Necessary (al-wajib) due to the impossibility of a thing corresponding to what has no actuality with it, and in knowledge of particulars like this letter and Zayd's standing at this time due to the impossibility of their impression in the intellect. And the answer to the first is possible: that the correctness of the judgment that is in the mind of the matter is not because it corresponds to what is in the mind of the matter, but rather it is the very same. And regarding the second: after conceding the impossibility of a thing's correspondence to what is posterior to it in essence, that the consideration of correspondence is only in knowledge that is by impression of the form, and the knowledge of the Necessary is not so, especially since they only affirm for Him firstly the intellection of His essence, and it is His very essence. And regarding the third: that the impression of the particular (al-juz'i) in the intellect in a universal manner is sufficient for correspondence.
Source pages p. 394

Commentary
This passage is a complex argumentative refutation by the author (likely al-Taftazani) of the claim that the active intellect is identical with the Preserved Tablet and serves as the standard of truth (ma fi nafs al-amr). The author begins by presenting an opponent's reasoning: the Necessary Being cannot be the source of multiplicity, the soul cannot contain all forms actually, so the active intellect is determined as the source. The opponent then identifies this active intellect with the Preserved Tablet mentioned in the Qur'an. The author immediately objects that this contradicts the Qur'anic verse 'And with Him are the keys of the unseen' (Surah al-An'am, 6:59), which indicates that the knowledge of all things is with God, not a created intellect. Then the author says it would have been better for the opponent to remain silent rather than force such an application. Next, the author refutes the specific claim that 'ma fi nafs al-amr' (the objective truth) refers to the active intellect. He gives an example: Everyone knows that the statement 'one is half of two' corresponds to objective truth, without having any concept of the active intellect, let alone believing in its existence or that it contains the forms of all beings. In fact, many theologians deny the existence of the active intellect altogether. Then the author offers a clarification: the intended meaning is that what is in the mind of the matter in a universal and non-contradictory sense is what is in the active intellect, even if they differ in concept. This is a more nuanced position. Then an objection is raised: If the active intellect's knowledge is meant, correspondence would be impossible because there is no duality in the active intellect (since it is simple), and because knowledge prior to it (like God's knowledge) does not involve representation, and because particulars cannot be impressed in an intellect. The author answers each: (1) The correctness of a judgment is not due to correspondence with something else, but it is identical with the objective truth itself. (2) Even if we accept that correspondence with posterior knowledge is impossible, correspondence applies only to knowledge by impression (like human knowledge), not to God's knowledge which is identical with His essence. (3) The representation of a particular in the intellect in a universal manner is sufficient for correspondence. The passage thus defends a specific epistemological view while criticizing a crude identification.

العقل الفعال
المطابقة
الاثنينية
الواجب
الجزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that correspondence with the active intellect is impossible because it is simple.
Correction: The author argues that the correctness of a judgment is identical with the truth itself, not a matter of correspondence between two distinct things.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that the active intellect lacks duality, making correspondence impossible?
A) He says the active intellect does have duality.
B) He says correctness of a judgment is identity with the truth, not correspondence.
C) He says the active intellect is not simple.
D) He says correspondence is possible in simple things.

Key Takeaway
The author defends a nuanced view of the active intellect as a universal standard by answering objections with identity theory, restriction of correspondence to representational knowledge, and sufficiency of universal impressions.
