Defining Quiddity: What Makes a Thing Itself
Sanusi defines quiddity and distinguishes it from efficient causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of quiddity as that by which a thing is what it is, and how it is distinct from efficient causality.

Core Idea
Quiddity answers 'what is it?' — not 'what caused it to exist?'.

Sanusi begins by laying down a foundational term: quiddity (mahiyah). Before discussing existence or creation, we must understand what a thing essentially is. This lesson clarifies that quiddity is about essence, not existence, and guards against confusing it with the efficient cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic definition: 'ماهية الشيء ما به يجاب عن السؤال بما هو'. Then read the translation. Notice how the author defines it as the answer to 'what is it?'.
This definition is the core of the passage.
2
Understand the explanation
The author explains quiddity as 'ما به الشيء هو هو' — that by which the thing is itself. Reflect on this: what does it mean for something to be itself?
This is a non-circular verbal definition.
3
Distinguish from efficient cause
The author refutes those who claim the definition applies to the efficient cause. Read the triangle example: we conceive a triangle without knowing its existence or cause. This shows quiddity is independent of existence.
This point is crucial for rejecting that quiddity is made by a maker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول: ماهية الشيء ما به يجاب عن السؤال بما هو ويفسره بما به الشيء هو هو ولا ينتقض بالفاعل إذ به وجود الشيء لا هو، وهي باعتبار التحقق تسمى ذاتا وحقيقة وباعتبار التشخص هوية. وهي لفظة مشتقة عما هو ولذا قالوا: ماهية الشيء ما به يجاب عن السؤال بما هو كما أن الكمية ما به يجاب عن السؤال بكم هو، ولا خفاء في أن المراد بما هو الذي تطلب الحقيقة دون الوصف أو شرح الاسم وتركوا اعتماداً على أنه المتعارف، واحترازاً عن ذكر الحقيقة في تفسير الماهية، ومنهم من صرح بالقيد فقال: الذي يطلب به جميع ما به الشيء هو هو وأنت خبير بأن ذلك بعينه معنى الماهية وأن هذا التفسير لفظي فلا ويشبه أن يكون هذا تحديداً إذ لا دور وقد يفسر بما به الشيء هو هو ولا مفهوم سوى هذا، وزعم بعضهم أنه صادق على العلة الفاعلية، وليس كذلك، لأن الفاعل ما به يكون الشيء موجوداً، لا ما به يكون الشيء ذلك الشيء، فإنا نتصور حقيقة المثلث وإن لم نعلم له وجوداً ولا فاعلاً. وبالجملة فمعنى هذا التفسير على أن نفس الماهية ليست بجعل الجاعل على ما سيجيء بيانه، ثم الماهية إذا اعتبرت مع التحقيق سميت ذاتاً وحقيقة، فلا يقال ذات العنقاء وحقيقته بل ماهيته أي ما يتعقل منه، وإذا اعتبرت مع التشخص سميت هوية، وقد يراد بالهوية التشخص وقد يراد الوجود الخارجي وقد يراد ما صدقت عليه الماهية من الأفراد.
The first investigation: The quiddity of a thing is that by which one answers the question 'what is it?', and it is explained by 'that by which the thing is itself'. It is not invalidated by the efficient cause because through it is the existence of the thing, not its essence. And it, in terms of realization, is called essence (dhat) and reality (haqiqa), and in terms of individuation is called identity (huwiyya). It is a word derived from 'what is it', and thus they said: The quiddity of a thing is that by which one answers the question 'what is it', just as quantity (kamiyya) is that by which one answers the question 'how much is it'. There is no hiddenness in that what is meant by 'what is it' is that by which the reality is sought, not the description or explanation of the name, and they omitted it relying on it being customary, and avoiding mentioning the reality in the definition of quiddity. And among them is he who explicitly stated the restriction and said: that by which is sought all that by which the thing is itself. And you are aware that that is exactly the meaning of quiddity and that this definition is verbal, so it is not a real definition, and it resembles that this is a delimitation, since there is no circularity. And it is sometimes explained by 'that by which the thing is itself', and there is no concept besides this. And some claimed that it applies to the efficient cause, but it is not so, because the efficient cause is that by which the thing becomes existent, not that by which the thing becomes that thing. For we conceive the reality of a triangle even if we do not know its existence or a cause. In summary, the meaning of this definition is that the quiddity itself is not made by the maker, as will be explained. Then, quiddity, when considered with realization, is called essence and reality, so one does not say 'the essence of the phoenix' or 'its reality' but rather 'its quiddity', i.e., what is intellectually conceived of it. And when considered with individuation, it is called identity, and by identity may be meant individuation, or external existence, or the individuals to which the quiddity applies.
Source pages p. 399

Commentary
This passage defines quiddity (mahiyah) and explains its meaning. The author begins by stating that quiddity is that by which one answers the question 'what is it?'; it is what makes a thing itself. He then distinguishes quiddity from the efficient cause, noting that the efficient cause brings about existence, not the essence. For example, we can conceive the reality of a triangle without knowing its existence or cause. Next, he explains that quiddity is derived from 'what is it', and the definition avoids mentioning 'reality' to prevent circularity. Some claimed the definition applies to the efficient cause, but the author refutes this. He then notes that quiddity, when considered in itself, is called essence (dhat) and reality (haqiqa), and when considered with individuation, is called identity (huwiyya). He clarifies that identity may mean individuation, external existence, or the individuals to which the quiddity applies.

Key Terms
ماهية
علة فاعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quiddity is the same as the efficient cause or existence.
Correction: Quiddity is what makes a thing itself, not what brings it into existence. We can conceive a triangle's quiddity without any knowledge of its existence or a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the quiddity of a thing?
A) That by which the thing becomes existent.
B) That by which one answers the question 'what is it?'.
C) The efficient cause of the thing.
D) The external existence of the thing.

Key Takeaway
Quiddity is the answer to 'what is it?' and is independent of existence or causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you define yourself, do you answer 'what am I?' based on your actions or existence? Or do you recognize that your quiddity – your essence – is deeper than what you do? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to define your worth by your accomplishments?
?
The triangle's quiddity is independent of its existence. What does that imply about your identity? Can your core be unchanged even if your existence changes? How would you live differently if you truly believed your essence is not made by circumstances?
?
If quiddity is not made by any maker, then what does it mean that God knows your essence? Does that make you feel seen or exposed? How does this affect your reliance on God's creation of your existence rather than your essence?

Terminology of Quiddity: Essence, Reality, and Identity
Sanusi explains the different names for quiddity based on consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how quiddity is referred to by different terms depending on the aspect considered: essence (dhat), reality (haqiqa), and identity (huwiyya).

Core Idea
Quiddity is one concept, but its names change with perspective: essence, reality, identity.

After defining quiddity, Sanusi expands on the various terms used for it. These terms are not synonyms in all contexts; they highlight different aspects of quiddity. Understanding this vocabulary is essential for reading later theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on terminology
Read the Arabic text beginning with 'ثم الماهية' and its translation. Note the three terms: ذات, حقيقة, هوية.
This passage is a direct continuation of the definition.
2
Understand the conditions for each term
Quiddity is called essence and reality only when considered with realization (tahqeeq). For non-existent things like the phoenix, we only use quiddity. When considered with individuation (tashakhkhus), it is called identity.
Realization means actual existence or mental conception.
3
Explore the meanings of identity
The author lists three possible meanings of identity: individuation, external existence, or the individuals that instantiate the quiddity. Reflect on how context determines which meaning is intended.
Identity is a polysemous term.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own beliefs about Allah, do you treat them as a mere quiddity—something you intellectually conceive—or as a realized essence that transforms your life? What concrete difference would it make to move from the first to the second?
?
The phoenix is an example of something that has quiddity but no essence or reality because it does not exist. Is there any aspect of your faith—such as your intention, your repentance, or your love for the Prophet—that you affirm verbally but which lacks real existence in your heart? How can you bring it from mere concept to realization?

Distinction of Quiddity from Accidental Attributes
Sanusi distinguishes quiddity from essential and separable accidentals using the example of four.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that quiddity (mahiya) is conceptually distinct from both essential and separable accidental attributes, and that accidental attributes are requirements (muqtadayat) of the quiddity, not part of its essence.

Core Idea
A thing's quiddity is itself; its attributes are not part of its essence.

Sanusi clarifies a foundational distinction in Islamic metaphysics: the quiddity (mahiya) of a thing is not the same as its attributes. He explains that even essential accidentals (al-‘awāriḍ al-lāzima) are distinct from the quiddity itself. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and the crucial example of the number four.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note how Sanusi distinguishes quiddity from accidentals using the example of four.
The passage ends abruptly with 'ضرورة', but the main argument is complete.
2
Identify the two types of accidentals
Sanusi mentions 'lazima' (essential) and 'mufariqa' (separable) accidentals. Both are different from the quiddity. Find these terms in the text.
Essential accidentals always accompany the quiddity, while separable ones may change.
3
Analyze the example of four
The passage uses the number four to show that evenness and oddness are not part of four's quiddity. When considering four in itself, it is neither even nor odd. Understand why this is a powerful illustration.
The phrase 'من حيث هي' (insofar as it is) is key to isolating the quiddity.
4
Grasp the key distinction
Sanusi concludes that every quiddity is distinct from all its accidentals. Attributes like evenness for four, oddness for three, walking for animal, and laughter for human are requirements, not the quiddity itself.
This preserves the integrity of the essence while explaining how properties arise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحقيق الماهية باعتبار تميزها عن العوارض قال: وتغاير عوارضها اللازمة والمفارقة، وتتقابل بتقابلها فحيث يقال: الأربعة من حيث هي زوج، أو ليست بفرد يراد أن ذلك من مقتضيات الماهية، وإلا فهي من حيث هي ليست إلا هي حتى لو قيل: الأربعة من حيث هي زوج، أو ليست بزوج، أو هي زوج أو فرد. قلنا: ليست من حيث هي هي بزوج ولا فرد. بمعنى أن شيئاً منهما ليس نفسها ولا داخلاً فيها ولا يصح هي من حيث هي زوج أو ليست بفرد، أو لا هذا ولا ذاك. أي ماهية الشيء وحقيقته مغاير جميع عوارضها اللازمة والمفارقة كالفردية للثلاثة والزوجية للأربعة وكالمشي للحيوان والضحك للإنسان، ضرورة
Realization of quiddity in terms of its distinction from accidentals. He said: And its accidentals, both essential and separable, are different from it, and they are opposite to each other, so where it is said: 'Four, insofar as it is four, is even, or is not odd' – what is meant is that that is from the requirements of the quiddity. Otherwise, it, insofar as it is four, is nothing but itself, so if it were said: 'Four, insofar as it is four, is even, or is not even, or it is even or odd' – we say: It is not, insofar as it is itself, even or odd, meaning that neither of them is itself nor internal to it, and it is not correct to say 'it is, insofar as it is itself, even or not odd' or 'neither this nor that'. That is, the quiddity and reality of a thing is different from all its essential and separable accidentals, like oddness for three, evenness for four, walking for animal, and laughter for human, necessarily.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage explains the distinction between quiddity and its accidental attributes. The author states that the quiddity is different from both essential (lazima) and separable (mufariqa) accidentals. He illustrates with the example of the number four: when we say 'four is even' or 'four is not odd', these are properties that follow from its quiddity, but they are not part of the quiddity itself. If we consider four in itself, it is neither even nor odd; these attributes are external to its essence. Similarly, oddness is not part of the quiddity of three, evenness not part of four, walking not part of animal, and laughter not part of human. The quiddity is the thing itself, while the accidentals are additional to it.

لازمة
مفارقة
ماهية
حقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributes like evenness are part of the quiddity of a thing.
Correction: They are requirements (muqtadayat) that follow from the quiddity, but are not identical to it nor internal to it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, is the quiddity of four even or odd?
A) It is even.
B) It is odd.
C) It is neither even nor odd.
D) It is both even and odd.

Key Takeaway
The quiddity of a thing is distinct from both its essential and separable accidentals; attributes like evenness are requirements, not part of the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider something you deeply identify with—your career, a character trait, or a belief. Are you confusing the quiddity of who you are with an accidental attribute? What would it mean to separate your identity from that attribute starting today?
?
When someone criticizes a persistent flaw of yours, do you feel attacked in your very essence? How might internalizing the distinction between your quiddity and your essential accidents help you receive correction without defensiveness?
?
You are not defined by your mistakes (separable accidents) nor by your ingrained habits (essential accidents). Does your self-talk reflect this truth? Write one concrete way you will remind yourself this week that your worth is in your quiddity, not your attributes.

The Essence in Itself and Its Accidents
Author explains the distinction between quiddity and its accidents and corrects a phrasing error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the quiddity of a thing is distinct from all its accidents, and you will learn the correct way to phrase attributions like 'the four in itself is even' without implying that the accident is part of the essence.

Core Idea
The quiddity in itself is not any accident—not even existence.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid clarifies a fundamental principle of Islamic metaphysics: the essence of a thing (its quiddity) is not the same as any of its properties, whether necessary or accidental. The author explains how to properly speak about attributes like 'evenness' for the number four, correcting a common misunderstanding. Understanding this distinction is key to avoiding errors in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the key distinction
Read the opening where the author distinguishes the agent that brings a thing into existence from the thing's essence itself. Note that we can conceive a triangle's reality without knowing its existence or agent.
This sets the stage: quiddity is independent of existence.
2
Learn the terminology
Identify the terms used for quiddity when considered in different ways: mahiyya, dhat, haqiqa, huwiyya. Understand that huwiyya can refer to individuation, external existence, or the individuals instantiating the quiddity.
3
Understand the distinction from accidents
The author states that quiddity is distinct from all its necessary and separable accidents. When we say 'the four in itself is even,' it does not mean evenness is part of the essence, but a requirement of the quiddity.
Pay attention to the example of the four and how it is neither even nor odd when considered purely in itself.
4
Master the rule for phrasing
Learn the correct way to phrase accidents of the quiddity: place the negation before the consideration ('it is not, in itself, even') to avoid implying essentiality. The author also clarifies when placing 'in itself' first is acceptable (for necessary concomitants) and when it is not.
Contrast with the error in al-Mawaqif mentioned at the end.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس كذلك، لأن الفاعل ما به يكون الشيء موجوداً، لا ما به يكون الشيء ذلك الشيء، فإنا نتصور حقيقة المثلث، وإن لم نعلم له وجوداً ولا فاعلاً. وبالجملة فمعنى هذا التفسير على أن نفس الماهية ليست بجعل الجاعل على ما سيجيء بيانه، ثم الماهية إذا اعتبرت مع التحقيق سميت ذاتاً وحقيقة، فلا يقال ذات العنقاء وحقيقته بل ماهيته أي ما يتعقل منه، وإذا اعتبرت مع التشخص سميت هوية، وقد يراد بالهوية التشخص، وقد يراد الوجود الخارجي، وقد يراد ما صدقت عليه الماهية من الأفراد. تحقيق الماهية باعتبار تميزها عن العوارض قال: وتغاير عوارضها اللازمة والمفارقة، وتتقابل بتقابلها فحيث يقال: الأربعة من حيث هي زوج، أو ليست بفرد يراد أن ذلك من مقتضيات الماهية، وإلا فهي من حيث هي ليست إلا هي حتى لو قيل: الأربعة من حيث هي زوج، أو ليست بزوج، أو هي زوج أو فرد. قلنا: ليست من حيث هي هي بزوج ولا فرد. بمعنى أن شيئاً منهما ليس نفسها ولا داخلاً فيها ولا يصح هي من حيث هي زوج أو ليست بفرد، أو لا هذا ولا ذاك. أي ماهية الشيء وحقيقته مغاير جميع عوارضها اللازمة والمفارقة كالفردية للثلاثة والزوجية للأربعة وكالمشي للحيوان والضحك للإنسان، ضرورة تغاير المعروض والعارض، ولهذا يصدق على المتنافيين كالإنسان الضاحك وغير الضاحك، فهي في نفسها ليست شيئاً من العوارض، ولو على طرفي النقيض، كالوجود والعدم والحدوث والقدم والوحدة والكثرة، وإنما ينضم إليه هذه العوارض فيحصل بها موجوداً، ومعدوماً، حادثاً، وقديماً واحداً وكثيراً إلى غير ذلك، وتتقابل تلك الماهية أي يعرض لها تقابل الأفراد بتقابل الأوصاف، فلا يصدق الإنسان الواحد على الإنسان الكثير وبالعكس، ولا الجسم المتحرك على الجسم الساكن، وعلى هذا القياس فحيث يحمل بعض العوارض على الماهية من حيث هي هي كما يقال الأربعة من حيث هي هي زوج أو ليست بفرد يراد أن ذلك من عوارض الماهية ولوازمها، ومقتضياتها من غير نظر إلى الوجود، ولو لم يرد ذلك لم يصح إلا حمل الذاتيات، فالأربعة من حيث هي هي ليست إلا الأربعة، ولهذا قالوا لو سئل بطرفي النقيض. فقيل الأربعة من حيث هي هي زوج، أو ليست بزوج. كان الجواب الصحيح: سلب كل شيء بتقديم حرف السلب على الحيثية مثل أن يقال: ليست من حيث هي بزوج ولا فرد ولا غير ذلك من العوارض بمعنى أن شيئاً منها ليس نفسها ولا داخلاً فيها، ولا يصح أن يقال هي من حيث هي زوج أو ليست بفرد أو ليست هذا ولا ذاك بتقديم الحيثية، لدلالته على أن ذلك الثبوت أو السلب من ذاتياتها. والتقدير أنها من العوارض، وأما إذا أريد بتقديم الحيثية أن ذلك العارض من مقتضيات الماهية صح في مثل قولنا الأربعة من حيث هي هي زوج، أو ليست بفرد دون قولنا الإنسان من حيث هو ضاحك أو ليس بضاحك. فما ذكر في المواقف من أن تقديم الحيثية على السلب معناه اقتضاء السلب، وهو باطل ليس على إطلاقه.
And it is not like that, because the agent is that by which a thing becomes existent, not that by which a thing becomes that thing. For we conceive the reality of a triangle even if we do not know for it an existence nor an agent. In summary, the meaning of this interpretation is that the quiddity itself is not by the making of the Maker, as will be explained later. Then the quiddity, when considered with verification, is called essence and reality, so one does not say 'the essence of the phoenix' and 'its reality' but rather its quiddity, i.e., what is intellectually grasped from it. And when considered with individuation, it is called identity. By identity may be meant individuation, or external existence, or the individuals to which the quiddity applies. Investigation of the quiddity with respect to its distinction from accidents: He said: Its necessary and separable accidents are distinct from it, and they are opposed by their opposition. So when it is said: 'The four, in itself, is even' or 'is not odd', it is meant that this is from the requirements of the quiddity; otherwise, it in itself is nothing but itself, such that if it were said: 'The four, in itself, is even' or 'is not even', or 'it is even or odd', we say: It is not, in itself, even nor odd, meaning that neither of the two is itself nor internal to it, nor is it correct that it, in itself, is even or not odd, or neither this nor that. That is, the quiddity of a thing and its reality are distinct from all its necessary and separable accidents, such as oddness for three, evenness for four, and walking for animal, and laughter for man, due to the necessary distinctness of the subject and the accident. For this reason, it applies to contraries, like man laughing and not laughing. So it in itself is none of the accidents, even if they are contradictory opposites, like existence and non-existence, originatedness and pre-eternity, oneness and multiplicity. Rather, these accidents attach to it, and it becomes by them existent, non-existent, originated, pre-eternal, one, many, and so on. And that quiddity is opposed—i.e., the opposition of individuals occurs to it by the opposition of attributes, so that one man does not apply to many men and vice versa, nor does a moving body apply to a stationary body, and so on. So when some accidents are predicated of the quiddity in itself, as it is said 'the four in itself is even' or 'is not odd', it is meant that this is from the accidents of the quiddity, its necessary concomitants, and its requirements without regard to existence. If that is not meant, then only essential properties are predicated. So the four in itself is nothing but four. For this reason they said: If questioned regarding the two contradictory extremes, and it is said: 'The four in itself is even' or 'is not even', the correct answer is: Negation of everything by placing the particle of negation before the consideration, such that it is said: 'It is not, in itself, even nor odd, nor any other of the accidents', meaning that none of them is itself nor internal to it. And it is not correct to say: 'It is, in itself, even' or 'not odd' or 'not this nor that' by placing the consideration first, because that indicates that the affirmation or negation is from its essential properties. And the presupposition is that it is among the accidents. But if by placing the consideration first it is intended that the accident is from the requirements of the quiddity, then it is correct in such as our saying 'the four in itself is even' or 'is not odd', but not in our saying 'man in himself is laughing' or 'not laughing'. So what is mentioned in al-Mawaqif that placing the consideration before negation means necessitation of negation is false without qualification.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is an extended explanation of the relationship between the quiddity (mahiyya) of a thing and its accidents (a'rad). The author begins by distinguishing between the agent that brings a thing into existence and the thing's essence itself. The essence is not caused by the agent in its quiddity; we can conceive a triangle's reality without knowing its existence or agent. Then he explains terminology: when considered with verification, the quiddity is called essence (dhat) or reality (haqiqa); when considered with individuation, it is called identity (huwiyya). Identity can refer to individuation, external existence, or the individuals that instantiate the quiddity. The main discussion is about the distinction of the quiddity from its accidents, both necessary (lazima) and separable (mufariqa). He states that the quiddity is distinct from all its accidents, so when we say 'the four in itself is even,' it does not mean that evenness is part of the essence; rather, it is a requirement of the quiddity. If we consider the quiddity purely in itself, it is not even, not odd, and not any accident. To illustrate, the quiddity of a thing is separate from its accidents, just as man's quiddity is separate from laughing and walking. The quiddity in itself is none of the accidents, not even contradictory ones like existence and non-existence. Accidents attach to it externally. The author then discusses the proper way to phrase attributions: if we say 'the four in itself is even' with 'in itself' placed first, it implies that evenness is essential, which is false. The correct answer when asked about such a statement is to negate, placing the negation before the consideration: 'It is not, in itself, even nor odd...' because the accident is not part of the essence. However, if by placing 'in itself' first we mean that the accident is a requirement of the quiddity, then it is correct for cases where the accident is a necessary concomitant, like evenness for four, but not for cases like 'man in himself is laughing' because laughing is not a necessary property of man. The author criticizes a view in al-Mawaqif that 'placing the consideration before negation means necessitation of negation' as unqualifiedly false. The key point is that the quiddity in itself is distinct from all accidents; accidents are either necessary concomitants or separable, but never part of the essence.

عوارض
حيثية
لازم
مفارق
ذات
هوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that saying 'the four in itself is even' means that evenness is part of the essence of four.
Correction: The author explains that such a statement means that evenness is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity, not part of the essence itself. The correct response is to negate: 'It is not, in itself, even.'

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the statement 'the four in itself is even' correctly mean?
A) Evenness is part of the essence of four.
B) Evenness is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity of four, not part of the essence.
C) The four in itself is both even and odd.
D) The four does not exist.

Key Takeaway
The quiddity of a thing is entirely distinct from all its accidents; when we predicate an accident of the quiddity 'in itself,' we mean it is a necessary concomitant, not part of the essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You conceive your own essence—who you are at your core. What accidents (status, wealth, relationships, achievements) do you habitually mistake for that essence? Before God, if all those were stripped away, would you still know yourself as His servant?
?
The quiddity of four is neither even nor odd; these are external attributes. Consider your own attributes: your failures, your successes, your sins. Do you let any one of them define you completely? What would it mean to see them as accidents rather than your essence?
?
This passage teaches that the quiddity is not made by the maker in its essence—it is what it is. What does that imply about your own worth? Are you valuable because of what you do or because of what you are? How would your life change if you truly believed your deepest identity is not earned but given?

The Distinction Between Essence and Attributes
Sanusi distinguishes essence from attributes with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between an essence (al-mahiyya) and its attributes (al-‘awāriḍ), and how opposite attributes can attach to the same essence without being part of it.

Core Idea
Essences are not their attributes; opposites can attach to the same essence.

This lesson begins by establishing the fundamental distinction between the subject (al-ma‘rūḍ) and its accidental attributes (al-‘āriḍ). Sanusi explains that an essence in itself is none of the attributes, even if they are opposites like existence and non-existence. This sets the stage for understanding how predication works when we consider the essence as such.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi distinguishes essence from attributes. Notice the examples of opposites like existence/non-existence and origination/pre-eternity.
Focus on the key phrase: 'فهي في نفسها ليست شيئاً من العوارض'.
2
Identify the distinction
Make sure you grasp that the essence itself is not any attribute; attributes are extrinsic and can be attached.
3
Reflect on the examples
Consider the opposites mentioned (existence vs. non-existence, etc.) and how they can apply to the same essence.
This shows the neutrality of the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تغاير المعروض والعارض، ولهذا يصدق على المتنافيين كالإنسان الضاحك وغير الضاحك، فهي في نفسها ليست شيئاً من العوارض، ولو على طرفي النقيض، كالوجود والعدم والحدوث والقدم والوحدة والكثرة، وإنما ينضم إليه هذه العوارض فيحصل بها موجوداً، ومعدوماً، حادثاً،وقديماً واحداً وكثيراً إلى غير ذلك، وتتقابل تلك الماهية أي يعرض لها تقابل الأفراد بتقابل الأوصاف، فلا يصدق الإنسان الواحد على الإنسان الكثير وبالعكس، ولا الجسم المتحرك على الجسم الساكن، وعلى هذا القياس فحيث يحمل بعض العوارض على الماهية من حيث هي هي كما يقال الأربعة من حيث هي هي زوج أو ليست بفرد يراد أن ذلك من عوارض الماهية ولوازمها، ومقتضياتها من غير نظر إلى الوجود، ولو لم يرد ذلك لم يصح إلا حمل الذاتيات، فالأربعة من حيث هي هي ليست إلا الأربعة، ولهذا قالوا لو سئل بطرفي النقيض . فقيل الأربعة من حيث هي هي زوج، أو ليست بزوج. كان الجواب الصحيح : سلب كل شيء بتقديم حرف السلب على الحيثية مثل أن يقال : ليست من حيث هي بزوج ولا فرد ولا غير ذلك من العوارض بمعنى أن شيئاً منها ليس نفسها ولا داخلاً فيها، ولا يصح أن يقال هي من حيث هي زوج أو ليست بفرد أو ليست هذا ولا ذاك بتقديم الحيثية، لدلالته على أن ذلك الثبوت أو السلب من ذاتياتها . والتقدير أنها من العوارض، وأما إذا أريد بتقديم الحيثية أن ذلك العارض من مقتضيات الماهية صح في مثل قولنا الأربعة من حيث هي هي زوج، أو لیست بفرد دون قولنا الإنسان من حيث هو ضاحك أو ليس بضاحك. فما ذكر في المواقف من أن تقديم الحيثية على السلب معناه اقتضاء السلب، وهو باطل ليس على إطلاقه.
The subject and the attribute are different, and therefore it applies to opposites like man laughing and not laughing. They (the essences) in themselves are not something of the attributes, even if they are on opposite extremes, like existence and non-existence, origination and pre-eternity, oneness and multiplicity. Rather, these attributes attach to it, and thus it becomes existent, non-existent, originated, pre-eternal, one, and many, and so on. And those essences are opposed—i.e., opposition of individuals occurs to them by the opposition of attributes. So one man does not apply to many men, and vice versa, nor does the moving body apply to the stationary body. And on this analogy, when some attributes are predicated of the essence as such—as it is said 'the four as such is even or is not odd'—it is meant that this is from the accidents and necessities of the essence and its requirements without regard to existence. If that were not meant, then only the essential attributes would be correctly predicated. So 'the four as such' is nothing but four. For this reason they said: if one is asked with the two sides of the contradiction, so it is said 'the four as such is even' or 'is not even,' the correct answer is the negation of everything by placing the particle of negation before the 'as-such-ness,' such as saying 'it is not, as such, even, nor odd, nor any other attribute,' meaning that none of them is its essence nor is included in it. And it is not correct to say 'it is, as such, even' or 'it is not odd' or 'it is not this nor that' by placing the 'as-such-ness' first, because that indicates that the affirmation or negation is from its essentials. And the estimation is that it is an attribute. But if by placing the 'as-such-ness' first it is intended that the attribute is from the requirements of the essence, then it is correct in such as our saying 'the four as such is even' or 'is not odd,' but not in our saying 'man as such is laughing' or 'is not laughing.' So what was mentioned in al-Mawaqif—that placing the 'as-such-ness' before the negation means the requirement of negation—is false not in an absolute sense.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage defines the relationship between an essence (al-mahiyya) and its attributes (al-‘awāriḍ). It begins by asserting the distinction between the subject (al-ma‘rūḍ) and the attribute (al-‘āriḍ), noting that opposites such as existence and non-existence can attach to the same essence. The essence itself is not any of the attributes; rather, attributes are extrinsic. The passage then addresses predication when considering the essence in itself (min ḥaythu hiya hiya). Using the example of 'the four as such is even or odd,' it explains that if one intends the essence alone, the correct answer is to negate everything by placing the negation before the 'as-such-ness'—e.g., 'it is not, as such, even or odd'—because no attribute is part of the essence. Predicating a positive or negative attribute with the 'as-such-ness' placed first would incorrectly suggest that the attribute is essential. However, if the intention is that the attribute is a necessary concomitant (muqtaḍā) of the essence, then it is permissible to say 'the four as such is even' (since evenness necessarily follows from fourness), but not 'man as such is laughing' (since laughter is not a necessary requirement of man). Finally, it critiques a statement in al-Mawaqif that placing the 'as-such-ness' before the negation implies that the negation is a requirement, calling this claim false in an unqualified sense.

Key Terms
الماهية
العارض
المعروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributes are part of the essence.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the essence in itself is not any attribute; attributes are extrinsic.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, can opposite attributes like existence and non-existence attach to the same essence?
A) Yes, because the essence is neutral.
B) No, because attributes define the essence.
C) Only if the essence changes.
D) It is impossible.

Key Takeaway
An essence is not its attributes; opposites can attach to it extrinsically.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you define yourself by what you do rather than who you are, you are confusing essence with attribute. Will you pause right now and say to yourself: 'My essence is that I am a slave of God, and everything else is accidental'? What changes if you truly live by this?

Have you been taking your existence for granted, as if it were a necessary part of you? How would your prayer change if you saw each breath as an accidental gift that could be withdrawn at any moment?

The lesson shows that even opposite predicates can be true of the same essence. Think of a time when you were both proud and ashamed of the same quality. Will you commit to affirming your essence as a Muslim even when your attributes disappoint you?

The Rule of Predication and the Role of Ḥaythiyya
Sanusi explains the predication rule and its exception, and criticizes al-Mawaqif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct method of predicating attributes of an essence as such, including the role of the phrase 'min ḥaythu hiya hiya' and the exception for necessary concomitants.

Core Idea
When speaking of an essence as such, negate all attributes unless they are necessary requirements.

Building on the distinction between essence and attributes, this lesson dives into the precise rules of predication. Sanusi uses the example of 'the four as such' to illustrate that if we consider the essence alone, we must negate all attributes by placing the negation before the 'as-such-ness'. However, there is an exception for attributes that are necessary requirements of the essence, like evenness for four.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on predication
Carefully read the second part of the source, starting from 'فحيث يحمل' to the end. Note the example of 'the four as such'.
Look for the phrase 'من حيث هي هي' – this is key.
2
Understand the correct method
The correct answer when asked about the essence as such is to negate everything by placing the negation before 'haythiyya' (e.g., 'ليست من حيث هي بزوج').
This shows attributes are not part of the essence.
3
Identify the exception
Sanusi mentions that if the attribute is a necessary concomitant (muqtaḍā) of the essence, then placing 'haythiyya' first is allowed (e.g., 'the four as such is even').
Contrast with 'man as such is laughing' which is not a necessary concomitant.
4
Consider the critique
The passage ends with a critique of al-Mawaqif. Understand why the claim about negation is not absolutely true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تغاير المعروض والعارض، ولهذا يصدق على المتنافيين كالإنسان الضاحك وغير الضاحك، فهي في نفسها ليست شيئاً من العوارض، ولو على طرفي النقيض، كالوجود والعدم والحدوث والقدم والوحدة والكثرة، وإنما ينضم إليه هذه العوارض فيحصل بها موجوداً، ومعدوماً، حادثاً،وقديماً واحداً وكثيراً إلى غير ذلك، وتتقابل تلك الماهية أي يعرض لها تقابل الأفراد بتقابل الأوصاف، فلا يصدق الإنسان الواحد على الإنسان الكثير وبالعكس، ولا الجسم المتحرك على الجسم الساكن، وعلى هذا القياس فحيث يحمل بعض العوارض على الماهية من حيث هي هي كما يقال الأربعة من حيث هي هي زوج أو ليست بفرد يراد أن ذلك من عوارض الماهية ولوازمها، ومقتضياتها من غير نظر إلى الوجود، ولو لم يرد ذلك لم يصح إلا حمل الذاتيات، فالأربعة من حيث هي هي ليست إلا الأربعة، ولهذا قالوا لو سئل بطرفي النقيض . فقيل الأربعة من حيث هي هي زوج، أو ليست بزوج. كان الجواب الصحيح : سلب كل شيء بتقديم حرف السلب على الحيثية مثل أن يقال : ليست من حيث هي بزوج ولا فرد ولا غير ذلك من العوارض بمعنى أن شيئاً منها ليس نفسها ولا داخلاً فيها، ولا يصح أن يقال هي من حيث هي زوج أو ليست بفرد أو ليست هذا ولا ذاك بتقديم الحيثية، لدلالته على أن ذلك الثبوت أو السلب من ذاتياتها . والتقدير أنها من العوارض، وأما إذا أريد بتقديم الحيثية أن ذلك العارض من مقتضيات الماهية صح في مثل قولنا الأربعة من حيث هي هي زوج، أو لیست بفرد دون قولنا الإنسان من حيث هو ضاحك أو ليس بضاحك. فما ذكر في المواقف من أن تقديم الحيثية على السلب معناه اقتضاء السلب، وهو باطل ليس على إطلاقه.
The subject and the attribute are different, and therefore it applies to opposites like man laughing and not laughing. They (the essences) in themselves are not something of the attributes, even if they are on opposite extremes, like existence and non-existence, origination and pre-eternity, oneness and multiplicity. Rather, these attributes attach to it, and thus it becomes existent, non-existent, originated, pre-eternal, one, and many, and so on. And those essences are opposed—i.e., opposition of individuals occurs to them by the opposition of attributes. So one man does not apply to many men, and vice versa, nor does the moving body apply to the stationary body. And on this analogy, when some attributes are predicated of the essence as such—as it is said 'the four as such is even or is not odd'—it is meant that this is from the accidents and necessities of the essence and its requirements without regard to existence. If that were not meant, then only the essential attributes would be correctly predicated. So 'the four as such' is nothing but four. For this reason they said: if one is asked with the two sides of the contradiction, so it is said 'the four as such is even' or 'is not even,' the correct answer is the negation of everything by placing the particle of negation before the 'as-such-ness,' such as saying 'it is not, as such, even, nor odd, nor any other attribute,' meaning that none of them is its essence nor is included in it. And it is not correct to say 'it is, as such, even' or 'it is not odd' or 'it is not this nor that' by placing the 'as-such-ness' first, because that indicates that the affirmation or negation is from its essentials. And the estimation is that it is an attribute. But if by placing the 'as-such-ness' first it is intended that the attribute is from the requirements of the essence, then it is correct in such as our saying 'the four as such is even' or 'is not odd,' but not in our saying 'man as such is laughing' or 'is not laughing.' So what was mentioned in al-Mawaqif—that placing the 'as-such-ness' before the negation means the requirement of negation—is false not in an absolute sense.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage defines the relationship between an essence (al-mahiyya) and its attributes (al-‘awāriḍ). It begins by asserting the distinction between the subject (al-ma‘rūḍ) and the attribute (al-‘āriḍ), noting that opposites such as existence and non-existence can attach to the same essence. The essence itself is not any of the attributes; rather, attributes are extrinsic. The passage then addresses predication when considering the essence in itself (min ḥaythu hiya hiya). Using the example of 'the four as such is even or odd,' it explains that if one intends the essence alone, the correct answer is to negate everything by placing the negation before the 'as-such-ness'—e.g., 'it is not, as such, even or odd'—because no attribute is part of the essence. Predicating a positive or negative attribute with the 'as-such-ness' placed first would incorrectly suggest that the attribute is essential. However, if the intention is that the attribute is a necessary concomitant (muqtaḍā) of the essence, then it is permissible to say 'the four as such is even' (since evenness necessarily follows from fourness), but not 'man as such is laughing' (since laughter is not a necessary requirement of man). Finally, it critiques a statement in al-Mawaqif that placing the 'as-such-ness' before the negation implies that the negation is a requirement, calling this claim false in an unqualified sense.

When Two Positives Act Like Contradictories
Ruling on two positives as pseudo-contradictories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Imam ruled that questions framed as two positive propositions equivalent to contradictories do not require an answer, and how this differs from true contradictories.

Core Idea
Two positives can't force a choice like true contradictories do.

This ruling from the Imam addresses a subtle logical point: not every pair of opposing propositions requires a decisive answer. When both options are phrased as positive attributes, the question is malformed. The passage explains the reasoning and links it to a broader discussion in al-Mawaqif about modal propositions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic ruling
Read the Arabic passage from the Imam, noting the key terms 'موجبتان' and 'النقيضان'. Read the translation to get the gist.
2
Understand the reasoning
The passage explains that with two positives, being described by one does not imply identity—only difference. This makes the question invalid.
Contrast with true contradictories where one must be true.
3
Examine the al-Mawaqif reference
See how the same principle applies to modal propositions (المعدولتان). Note the comment about deviation from the path.
This shows the rule's breadth.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you grasp why the question is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الإمام : ولو سئلنا بموجبتين هما في قوة النقيضين. كقولنا الإنسان إما واحداً أو كثيراً، لم يلزمنا أن نجيب عنه البتة ، بخلاف ما إذا سئل بطرفي النقيض، لأن معنى السؤال بالموجبتين . أنه إذا لم يتصف بهذا الموجب اتصف بذاك، والاتصاف لا يستلزم الاتحاد ، بل يستلزم التغاير، وهذا ما قال في المواقف : لو سئلنا عن المعدولتين فقيل الإنسانية من حيث هي أو لا لم يلزمنا الجواب. ولو قلنا لا هذا ولا ذاك. أي ليست من حيث هي ولا بتقديم الحديثية صح لما مر، ولا يخفى ما في المعدولتين من العدول عن الطريق. فإن قولنا هذا ليست من المعدولة في شيء.
And the Imam said: "If we are asked with two positive propositions that are equivalent to contradictory opposites—like our saying 'man is either one or many'—we are not obliged to answer at all, unlike when we are asked with the two sides of the contradiction. Because the meaning of the question with two positive propositions is that if it is not described by this positive, it is described by that one, and being described does not entail identity, but rather entails difference." And this is what was said in al-Mawaqif: "If we are asked about the two modal propositions (al-mu‘addalatayn) and it is said 'humanity as such is or is not,' we are not obliged to answer. And if we say 'neither this nor that'—i.e., 'it is not as such' and by placing the as-such-ness first—it is correct, due to what has passed. And it is not hidden what is in the two modal propositions of deviation from the path. For our saying 'this' is not from the modalized (al-mu‘addala) at all."
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Imam (al-Rāzī or another authority) regarding a specific type of question. It states that if one is asked with two positive propositions (mūjibatān) that are as strong as contradictories—for example, 'man is either one or many'—the questioner is not required to give an answer. This contrasts with a question using true contradictories (ṭarafay al-naqīḍ), which demands a response. The reason is that a question with two positive propositions implies that if the subject is not described by one positive, it is described by the other. However, being described by one does not necessitate that the subject is identical to the description; it only entails difference between the subject and the description. The passage then relates this to a statement in al-Mawaqif: if one is asked about two 'modalized' propositions (al-mu‘addalatayn)—e.g., 'humanity as such is or is not'—no answer is required. Saying 'neither this nor that' is correct if one places the negation before the 'as-such-ness,' as previously explained. Finally, it notes that the use of modal propositions is a deviation from the straightforward path, and that expressions like 'this' are not truly modalized.

Key Terms
موجبتان
النقيضان
المعدولة
العادلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any question with two opposite options requires an answer, even if both options are positive.
Correction: The passage shows that when both options are positive (e.g., 'one or many'), the question is invalid because the options do not truly contradict each other; they can both be false.

Checkpoint
Why is no answer required when asked with two positive propositions equivalent to contradictories?
A) Because the question is meaningless.
B) Because being described by one does not entail identity with the description.
C) Because the subject cannot be described by either.
D) Because the propositions are not actually opposites.

Key Takeaway
A question framed as two positive propositions cannot force a decisive answer because the two positives do not form a true contradiction.

Is it truly a logical contradiction, or are both positives that could be false in a deeper sense? What would it mean for your engagement with others if you realized you are not obliged to answer every opposing affirmation?

The passage warns that modal propositions (like 'humanity as such is or is not') are a 'deviation from the path.' How a Single Essence Exists in Multiple Individuals
Explains how a single essence is the same in reality but different in identity across individuals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a single universal essence can be the same in reality yet different in identity across multiple individuals, resolving a classic paradox in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
One essence can be many without contradiction.

How can humanity be the same in both Zayd and Amr? If it were identical in every way, they would have to be the same person—an absurdity. But if it were entirely different, then humanity would not be a shared essence. Muslim theologians resolved this by distinguishing between the essence in itself and its concrete instantiations. This lesson unpacks that solution.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Read the passage and identify the two conflicting possibilities: either the essence is the same in individuals (leading to absurdity) or it is different (undermining universality).
The passage presents this as an implied objection.
2
Find the answer
Look for the phrase 'same in reality, different in identity.' This is the core resolution.
3
Apply to examples
Think of other universals like 'animal' or 'whiteness' and see how they appear in multiple individuals without contradiction.
This reinforces the concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي قوله: تتقابل بتقابلها إشارة إلى جواب سؤال تقديره : إن الإنسانية التي في زيد إن كانت هي التي في عمر ولزم أن يكون للشخص الواحد في آن واحد في مكانين، وموصوفاً بوصفين متضادين، وإن كانت غيرها لم تكن الماهية أمراً واحداً مشتركا بين الأفراد وتقرير الجواب : أنها عينها بحسب الحقيقة غيرها بحسب الهوية، ولا يمتنع كون الواحد لا بالشخص في أمكنة متعددة، ومتصفة بصفات متقابلة، بل يجب في طبيعة الأعم أن يكون كذلك.
And in His statement: 'they are opposed by their opposition' is an indication of the answer to a question whose estimation is: If the humanity in Zayd is the same as that in Amr, then it would be necessary for the same person to be in two places at one time, and described by two contrary attributes; and if it is different, then the essence would not be one thing common among individuals. And the formulation of the answer: it is the same according to reality, different according to identity (huwiyya), and there is no impossibility in the non-individual one being in multiple places and described by opposite attributes; rather, it is necessary for the nature of the universal to be like that.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage addresses a classic philosophical problem: how can a single essence (al-māhiyya) be common to multiple individuals? It begins by explaining that a phrase in the text—'they are opposed by their opposition'—alludes to an answer to an implied objection. The objection is: if the humanity in Zayd is identical to the humanity in Amr, then one and the same person would have to be in two places at once and possess contradictory attributes. But if the humanity in each is different, then there is no single essence shared among individuals. The answer given is that the essence is the same in reality (bi-ḥasab al-ḥaqīqa) but different in concrete identity (bi-ḥasab al-huwiyya). In other words, the universal nature is one in the abstract but multiple in its instantiations. This is not impossible; indeed, it is necessary for a universal (al-a‘amm) to be like that: it can be in many places and have opposite attributes without contradiction, since it is not a particular individual.

Key Terms
الماهية
الهوية
الحقيقة
الأعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The same essence cannot exist in multiple individuals without contradiction.
Correction: The essence is the same in reality but different in identity; the universal can be instantiated in many particulars.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the same humanity in Zayd and Amr possible?
A) Humanity is entirely different in each.
B) Humanity is the same in reality but different in identity.
C) Humanity is the same in identity but different in reality.
D) Humanity is an illusion.

Key Takeaway
A universal essence is one in reality but many in identity, allowing it to exist in multiple individuals without contradiction.

The Ruling on Two Affirmatives Equivalent to Contradictories
Al-Taftazani explains the ruling on two affirmatives equivalent to contradictories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani holds that one is not obliged to answer a question framed with two affirmative propositions that are equivalent to contradictories, and how this relates to the nature of description and identity.

Core Idea
Two affirmatives do not create a true contradiction; they imply difference, not identity.

This passage from al-Taftazani addresses a subtle logical point about questioning in theological debate. He explains that when someone poses a question using two affirmative statements—like 'is man one or many?'—the respondent is not obligated to give an answer. The reason lies in the logical structure of affirmatives versus true contradictions. Let's explore his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms: موجبتين, نقيضين, معدولتين.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The passage contrasts two scenarios: being asked with two affirmatives (e.g., 'one or many') and being asked with the two sides of a true contradiction (e.g., 'exists or does not exist'). Recognize the difference.
In a contradiction, one side must be true; in affirmatives, both could be false.
3
Understand the reasoning: description vs. identity
Al-Taftazani argues that two affirmatives imply that if the subject is not described by one, it is described by the other. But description does not necessitate identity; it rather implies difference (تغاير). This is why no answer is obligatory.
Think of 'one or many' – a thing could be neither if it is not a man.
4
Examine the discussion on modified propositions
The author references al-Mawaqif and clarifies that modified propositions (المعدولتين) like 'humanity as such or not' also do not require an answer. He notes that terms like 'one' (meaning 'not many') are not truly modified.
Modified propositions involve negation, but here the affirmatives are not truly negated.
5
Consider the objection and resolution about common humanity
An implied objection asks: if humanity in Zayd is identical to that in Amr, absurdities follow; if different, then humanity is not a common essence. Al-Taftazani answers that the essence is the same in reality (ماهية) but different in identity (هوية), and a universal can have multiple instances.
This preserves the commonality of essence while avoiding logical problems.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الإمام : ولو سئلنا بموجبتين هما في قوه النقيضين. كقولنا الإنسان إما واحداً أو كثيراً، لم يلزمنا أن نجيب عنه البتة ، بخلاف ما إذا سئل بطرفي النقيض، لأن معنى السؤال بالموجبتين . أنه إذا لم يتصف بهذا الموجب اتصف بذاك، والاتصاف لا يستلزم الاتحاد ، بل يستلزم التغاير، وهذا ما قال في المواقف : لو سئلنا عن المعدولتين فقيل الإنسانية من حيث هي أو لا لم يلزمنا الجواب. ولو قلنا لا هذا ولا ذاك. أي ليست من حيث هي ولا بتقديم الحديثية صح لما مر، ولا يخفى ما في المعدولتين من العدول عن الطريق. فإن قولنا هذا ليست من المعدولة في لفظه كذا قولنا هذا واحد أي لا كثير وكثير أي لا واحد وبصير أي لا أعمى وأعمى أي لا بصير لم يقل أحد بكونها معدولة، وفي قوله: تتقابل بتقابلها إشارة إلى جواب سؤال تقديره : إن الإنسانية التي في زيد إن كانت هي التي في عمر و لزم أن يكون للشخص الواحد في آن واحد في مكانين، وموصوفاً بوصفين متضادين، وإن كانت غيرها لم تكن الماهية أمراً واحداً مشتركا بين الأفراد وتقرير الجواب : أنها عينها بحسب الحقيقة غيرها بحسب الهوية، ولا يمتنع كون الواحد لا بالشخص في أمكنة متعددة، ومتصفة بصفات متقابلة، بل يجب في طبيعة الأعم أن يكون كذلك.
And the Imam said: And if we are asked with two affirmative propositions that are in the strength of contradictories, like our saying 'man is either one or many,' we are not obliged to answer at all, unlike if we are asked with the two sides of contradiction, because the meaning of asking with two affirmatives is that if it is not described by this affirmative, it is described by that one, and description does not require identity, but rather requires difference. And this is what was said in al-Mawaqif: If we are asked about the modified propositions and it is said 'humanity as such or not,' we are not obliged to answer. And if we say 'neither this nor that,' i.e., it is not as such nor with the precedence of [?] it is correct due to what has passed, and it is not hidden what is in the modified propositions of deviation from the path. For our saying 'this' is not from the modified in its wording, like our saying 'this is one, i.e., not many,' and 'many, i.e., not one,' and 'seeing, i.e., not blind,' and 'blind, i.e., not seeing,' no one has said they are modified. And in his saying: 'they are opposite by their opposition' is an indication of an answer to an implied question: 'The humanity in Zayd, if it is the same as that in Amr, it would necessitate that one person at one time be in two places and described with two opposite attributes, and if it is other than it, then the essence would not be one thing common among individuals.' And the answer: It is the same in terms of reality, different in terms of identity, and it is not impossible for what is one not by person to be in multiple places and described with opposite attributes; rather, it is necessary in the nature of the universal to be so.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is an argument from the Imam (al-Taftazani) explaining the ruling on answering questions posed with two affirmative propositions that are equivalent in strength to contradictories. The author begins by stating that if someone asks with two affirmative propositions like 'man is either one or many,' he is not obliged to answer at all. This is different from asking with the two sides of a true contradiction (like 'man exists or does not exist'), which would require an answer. The reason is that asking with two affirmatives implies that if the subject is not described by one affirmative, it is described by the other. But description with one affirmative does not necessitate that the two affirmatives refer to the same thing; rather, it necessitates difference (taghayur). Then the author references al-Mawaqif, which states that if asked about modified propositions (al-muaddalatān) like 'humanity as such or not,' no answer is required. The author then clarifies that if one says 'neither this nor that,' it is correct due to the preceding reasoning. He notes that the term 'modified propositions' is often misapplied; examples like 'one' (which means 'not many') are not truly modified. Finally, he addresses a potential objection about common humanity: if humanity in Zayd were identical to that in Amr, it would lead to absurdities (one person in two places), and if different, then humanity would not be a common essence. The answer is that the essence is the same in reality but different in identity (huwiyya), and it is not impossible for a universal to be instantiated in multiple places and have opposite attributes. This preserves the commonality of essence while avoiding absurdity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that two affirmatives like 'one or many' form a contradiction, requiring a decisive answer.
Correction: The author shows they do not form a contradiction because they lack the negation required for true contradiction; they imply difference, not identity, so no answer is obligatory.

Checkpoint
Why is one not obliged to answer when asked with two affirmative propositions equivalent to contradictories?
A) Because the question is invalid.
B) Because two affirmatives do not form a true contradiction; they imply difference, not identity.
C) Because the Imam said so without reason.
D) Because the question includes a modified proposition.

Key Takeaway
Two affirmatives do not constitute a true contradiction, so no answer is required; when two affirmatives appear to be opposites, they imply difference, not identity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter apparent contradictions in your faith—like God's mercy and justice—do you assume they are true logical contradictories that cannot both be true? How does this lesson's distinction between two affirmatives and true contradictories challenge you to reconsider your reaction? What will you do the next time such a tension arises in your mind?
?
Think of a time you judged a person based on one attribute (e.g., 'he is generous') and then were confused when they also displayed its opposite (e.g., 'he is stingy'). How does al-Taftazani's insight that description with one affirmative implies difference, not identity, apply to how you view others? Will you now be slower to label people as one thing or another?
?
The passage resolves the problem of a common essence (mahiyya) being instantiated in individuals with opposite attributes. How does this affect your understanding of the unity of the Ummah despite differing appearances, temperaments, or opinions? Are you willing to see the common essence of faith in fellow believers while accepting their differences in identity without contradiction? What concrete step can you take today to embody that understanding?

Logic and Technical Terms
intermediate
5 min
The Three States of Essence: Mixed, Abstract, and Absolute
Al-Taftazani classifies essence into three states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ways essence can be considered — with accidents, without any accidents, and absolutely — and how they relate in terms of specificity and existence.

Core Idea
Essence exists in three modes: mixed with accidents, abstract from all, and absolute.

Al-Taftazani presents a foundational classification of essence (mahiyya) in metaphysics. He distinguishes three ways we can think about essence: as it exists in individuals with accidents, as it is conceived without any accidents, and as it is taken without any restriction. This classification helps clarify the nature of universals, particulars, and abstract concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three divisions
Read the Arabic passage and locate the three ways essence is taken: with the condition of something (mixed), with the condition of nothing (abstract), and without condition (absolute).
The first two are defined by a condition, the third is unconditional.
2
Examine the existence of abstract essence
Notice that al-Taftazani argues that abstract essence does not exist even in the mind, because mental existence itself is an accident. Understand why he says this.
This is a key metaphysical point.
3
Understand the logical relationships
The passage states that the first two are more specific (akhaṣṣ) than the third, and the third is more general (aʿamm). Also, the two restricted ones are mutually exclusive (mubāyin). Reflect on why.
Compare with the logical tree of genera and species.
4
Apply the classification
Give an example: human essence as mixed (Zayd, Amr), as abstract (humanity without any attributes), and as absolute (the concept of human without any qualification).
This helps solidify the concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني: تقسيم الماهية باعتبار ثبوت العوارض أو نفيها (قال : المبحث الثاني : الماهية قد تؤخذ بشرط شيء وتسمى المخلوطة ولا خفاء في وجودها وقد تؤخذ بشرط لا شيء وتسمى المجردة، ولا توجد في الأذهان فضلاً عن الأعيان وإن قيدت اللواحق بالخارجية، لأن الكون في الذهن مما يلحقها في نفسها، وإن لم يتصوره العقل، ولم يجعله وصفاً لها، وما يقال من أن للعقل أن يلاحظها وحدها، أو يعتبرها مجردة عن ما عداها حتى عن الكون في الذهن لا يقتضي تجردها، وإن اكتفى بمجرد اعتبار العقل جاز وجودها في الخارج أيضاً بأن يعتبر المقرونة بالمشخصات كذلك . جميع مقارنة العوارض وتسمى المخلوطة ، والماهية بشرط شيء، ولا خفاء في وجودها كزيد وعمرو من أفراد ماهية الإنسان، وقد تؤخذ بشرط ألا يقارنها. الماهية : تارة تؤخذ مقيدة بالعوارض وتسمى مخلوطة، وتارة تؤخذ مقيدة بنفي جميع العوارض وتسمى مجردة وتارة تؤخذ غير مقيدة بثبوتها ولا نفيها وتسمى مطلقة، فالقسمان الأولان كل منهما أخص من الثالث لاختصاصه بالقيد والقسم الثالث : أعم من كل منهما لإطلاقه من التقييد وكل من المقيدين مباين لصاحبه فهي أقسام ثلاثة .
The second investigation: The division of essence with respect to the affirmation of accidents or their negation. (He said: The second investigation: Essence may be taken with the condition of something and is called mixed and there is no obscurity in its existence; and it may be taken with the condition of nothing and is called abstract, and it does not exist in minds, let alone in external entities, even if the accidents are restricted to external ones, because existence in the mind is something that attaches to it in itself, even if the intellect does not conceive it and does not make it a description of it. And what is said that the intellect can observe it alone, or consider it abstracted from all else, even from existence in the mind, does not necessitate its abstraction, and if it is sufficient by mere consideration of the intellect, its existence in the external would also be permissible by considering what is conjoined with individuators similarly. All conjunction of accidents is called mixed, and essence with the condition of something, and there is no obscurity in its existence like Zayd and Amr from the individuals of the essence of man; and it may be taken with the condition that no accident accompanies it. Essence: sometimes taken restricted by accidents and called mixed; sometimes taken restricted by negation of all accidents and called abstract; and sometimes taken unrestricted by affirmation or negation and called absolute. So the first two divisions are each more specific than the third due to their restriction to a condition, and the third is more general than each due to its freedom from restriction, and each of the two restricted ones is mutually exclusive with the other. So they are three divisions.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a classification by al-Taftazani of the three ways essence (māhiyya) can be considered with respect to the affirmation or negation of accidents (ʿawāriḍ). The author states that essence may be taken with the condition of something (bi-sharṭ shayʾ) and is called mixed (makhluṭa); its existence is evident, like Zayd and Amr as instances of human essence. It may also be taken with the condition of nothing (bi-sharṭ lā shayʾ) and is called abstract (mujarrada); this state does not exist in minds, let alone in external reality, because even mental existence is an accident that attaches to essence. The intellect may consider essence alone, but that does not make it actually abstract. If mere consideration were sufficient, then abstract essence could exist externally, which is false. Then the author lists the three divisions explicitly: (1) essence restricted by accidents (mixed), (2) essence restricted by negation of all accidents (abstract), and (3) essence unrestricted by either affirmation or negation (absolute). The first two are more specific than the third, and they are mutually exclusive. This division is fundamental in understanding how universals and particulars relate.

Key Terms
ماهية
عوارض
مخلوطة
مجردة
مطلقة
بشرط شيء
بشرط لا شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abstract essence can exist in the mind or externally because the intellect can conceive it alone.
Correction: Al-Taftazani argues that abstract essence does not exist even in the mind, because mental existence is an accident that would still be attached to it. The intellect's conception does not change its non-existence.

Checkpoint
What is the third division of essence called in this classification?
A) Mixed (makhluṭa)
B) Abstract (mujarrada)
C) Absolute (muṭlaqa)
D) Conditional (muqayyada)

Key Takeaway
Essence can be considered in three ways: mixed, abstract, and absolute; the first two are specific and mutually exclusive, while the third is general.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your abstract essence (mujarrada) does not exist at all – even as a pure idea in your mind – then what are you truly? Do you tend to imagine a version of yourself free from all flaws, separate from your body, thoughts, and deeds? How is that a form of self-deception? What does this force you to acknowledge about your complete dependence on Allah for every aspect of your existence?
?
When you consider your own identity, do you usually view it as mixed (tied to your appearance, status, achievements, and relationships) or absolute (just 'you')? According to this lesson, only the mixed essence has real existence. How should this reshape your priorities and your attachment to worldly labels? Will you let go of defining yourself by accidents and instead ground your identity in your relationship with Allah?
